The Treasure of EVONYMVS, conteyninge the vvonderfull hid secrets of nature,
touching the most apt forms to prepare and destyl Medicines, for the
conservation of health: as Quintesse^ce, Aurum Potabile, Hippocras, Aromatical
wynes, Balmes, Oils Perfumes, garnishyng waters, and other manifold excellent
confections. Whereunto are joined the forms of sundry apt Fornaces, and
vessels, required in this art. Translated (with great diligence, and labor)
out of Latin, by Peter Morvvyng fellow of Magdaline Colleadge in Oxford.

Imprinted at London by John Day, dwelling over Aldersgate, beneath Saint
Martines.

Cum priuilegio ad imprimendum solum.

John Day to the Christian Reader.
COnsideringe with myself (louinge Reader) many and sundry times, how yt
nothing is so desired, so acceptable, or so necessary in the time of
dangerous infirmities, and pearel of bodily health, as to preserve those yt are
free of sickness from the violence of the same, and to restore the sick, weak,
and languishing paciente unto his former estate and pristinate health, which
thing is performed by the noble and excellent knowledge of Physic: and
again, how that this Art is exercised, yea of famous and learned men, which
with out any great profit to the pacient, or worship to ye minister, because
their medicines are negligently prepared: I thought it my part, by this my
trauail and expenses, somewhat to serve herein thy necessity. Wherefore, I have
caused this precious treasure to be translated into our usual, and native
language, that like as all men are subject to sickness: so in likewise all men
may by this occasion learn the way unto health. And because thauthor (whose
name I spare to manifest, for fear of inuious detracters) do in many places of
this his work, send the Reader unto Philippe Vlstadius and Brunsvvick: therefore
I will with expedition (if this my labors shall be thankfullye accepted,) also
publishe them, sparing neither labor, diligence, or charges for to bring
them both to their perfection, and also thy necessary use. As touching the
excellencye of the preparinge medicines, the utility springinge thereof, and
the argument of the work, because it is exactlye set out by the author in
the preface following, I will leave to trouble the further herein. Fare most
heartily well in Christ. At London the.ii. of May. 1559.

The preface of the author to the Reader.
i Of the invention of liquors and oils destilled. ii Of the scope and end of
this book. Whereof more also shall be spoken about the end of the Preface. iii
The praise of them that can be content to make common the best and most
effectuous medicines they have. iiii How much the dressing and manner of
preparans avails in every thing.
THe Art of destillatio^ (which they call Chymia, Alchimia, Alkimia, Chemia
Suidas calls it, and Alchemia) has invented many profitable things for ma^s
life, and in Physic also certain marvelous things, and praise worthy, if a
man prepare them right and diligently. For the lack of knowledge,
Covetousness or negligence of Physicians and commun Apothecaries is the cause
why many such preparations are set little by: and worthily, if you respect that
which they prepare: unworthily, if ye mark the Art it self, which certes is
most excellent and most profitable: which seems to be the cause why it lay hid
so long, and began to come in ure very late. Some ascribe unto Hierom of
Brunsvvick, which about.lxx. years past practiced Physic at Argentyn, the
original of getting out waters, as they call them, and liquors and oils out of
simple medicines by the strength of fire: but they are much deceived, for this
Art was not invented by him, but written in our Dutch tung and first set out by
him. In mine opinion this inuencion is so ancient as the inuencion of the very
Chymia, which I suppose was celebrated first and put in writing by men that
used the Punicall or else the Arabicke tongue, a little after the age of the
Grecian Phisicions, Of those I speak that writ almooste the latest, as Aetius,
Oribasius, Actuarius, Psellus. In certain libraries in Italy even at this day,
are there extant certain wrytinges of Chymia by certain late Grecians, namely a
certain Philosopher called Stephan: al so a book entitled of the changing of
metals, which co^munlye are called Chymia, or Arcymia. There is also a book to
be had of Alchymia made by Auicenna unto Assis a Philosopher. Geber (the Nepheu
of I can not tell what great Mahomet) who is celebrated as a captain and
prince of this science, what age he lived in I can not easily say. Although I
judge him not to have been the first in uentor of this Art, but one that
brought it to light and renouned it. This man in his work entitled Summae
perfectionis, discoursinge excellentlye and disputinge many things of
destillacion generallye, writes that diverse manners of destilling are known
almost of all men, even as an ancient inuencio^, yea in his time also, no new
thing. Certain late writers declare the wrytinges of Alchymia not only of
Albertus Magnus, S. Thomas, Rhaza, and Auicenna, Arabik Physicians, but also of
Aristotle, Plato and Solomon, at the least wise mention to be made by the^ of
it: to whom I give small credence, not that I think this study to be the
newer, but certes that it was either unknown to these philosophers or mentioned
in no place. Some there be that expound feetly and wittily all the feinings of
the Poets, and chieflye that of the golden fleece, sought by the Argonautae,
unto the multiplying of Gold or Art of enterchaunging of metalles. Some also
ascend higher, and make the first men by and by after the beginnidg of the
world, authors thereof. Some simplely make it most ancient, and affirm that no
certainty is to be had of the first inue^ters. At Padvvay in Italy in our time
was found a most ancient monument, namely an erthen pot, having written upon
it this Hexasticon.

Plutoni sacrum munus ne attingite fures,
Ignotum est vobis hoc quod in vrna latet.
Nanque elementa graui clausit digesta labor,
Vase sub hoc modico, Maximus Olibius:
Adsit foecundo custos sibi copia cornu,
Ne praetium tanti depereat laticis.
This sacred to God Pluto (thieves) ware that ye touch not,
Unknown is it to you all, this that is hid in a pot.
For the elements has ishut up digested with much pain,
In this small vessel the great Olibius certain.
Plenty with thy fruteful horn as a guard be thou present,
Least the price pearish of this liquor most excellent.
Within this pot was an other little pot with the inscription of these verses.

Abite hinc pessimi fures
Vos quid voltis cum vostris oculis emissitiis?
Abite hi^c vostro cu^ Mercurio petasato caduceatoque
Maximus maximo donum Plotoni hoc sacru^ facit
Away from hence ye mighty thieves, trudge else where and go by,
What seek ye with your spying eyes, why do ye por and pry
Hens with your hatted Mercury, and with his rod also.
This gift is sacred by the greatst unto the greatest Ploto.
Again, within this little pot was found a light yet burning between two
Phials, the one of Gould, the other of Silver, full of a certain most pure
liquor, by the virtue whereof they believe that this light had burnte many a
year, as did noote in their collections and gatherings of old ancient
inscriptio^s or Poesies Petrus Appianus and Bartholom eus Amantius Hermolaus
Barbarus also in his Corollarium or addicion upon Dioscorides made mention of
this same thing, whereas he entreats of waters in co^mun. There is also (says
he) a heavenly water, or rather divine of the Chymistes, which boothe
Democritus and Mercury Trimegistus knew: calling it sometimes a divine water,
sometimes a Seythicall liquor, sometimes pneuma, that is, spirit of the nature of
the firmament, and of y^ fifth essens or substance of things: whereof potable
gold, and that philosophers stone much spoken of, but not yet fond, consists.
Hereupon also is the name given unto the art calling it Psammurgicall, and
mystical, and Annophysiall and holy, and greatest: as though it had certain
secreate letters, and such as it should be conueniente to keep and restrein the
profane commun people from. This kind of liquor, as I suppose, does the
Epigram signifye of late found within the field of Padua nie unto the village
called Atesta, made upon earthen or brick metal, and therefore frail, and
broken unawares by the hands of a man of the country digging the ground in the
same place. The remembrance whereof least it should pearish we have added
hereunto the very words. This holy gift to the God, etc. as before. Therefore
it appears that the study of this Art was of most antiquity amongste the
Barbarus nations, but derived unto the Romans and Greeks somewhat late, nether
before perauenture that the Romans obtained the dominion of a great part of
the world: or if sooner yet covertly and retained emongst a very few.
Cicendulae y^ shine in the night (says Cardane) teach us that a liquor may be
made that may shine in the dark. It is made certes by the putrefying of those
things that have most erceding brightness, light and perspicuitie. I dout not
but it is possible to be made, whereof and how, I am uncertain. But these things
are almost besides our matter, saving that it might seem utterly that the way
to sublimate and destil, as they term it, has had his original of the
Chimists, and continued also alwais with their art: was opened unto the me^ of
our part of the world the^ first, when as ye world being made known bithimpery
of the Romans, sundry kinds of spices also and diners medicines unknown before
to the men of Europe, and to the Greeks and Latins also, began to be brought
in: and afterward also more, what time as the Mauritans and me^ learned in the
Arabik tung and doctrine, possessed a great part of Spain, whereby certain books
of learned me^ came unto us: and amongst the latter sort, as I think, the works
of one Bulcasis Benaberazeris who in the place where he intreteth of the
preparation of medicines, teaches also of many things to be sublimated and
destilled. About the age of the same man almost, I think Johannes Mesue was
famous, who^ we read to have lived about ye year of our Lord. 1158. But be
me^cioneth none other destild waters the^ of Roses and Wormwod. Me^ say
Auicenna flourished the year of our Lord. 1149* who also speaks of Rose water
destild. The vsa of metalli things sublimated is far more ancient, at the
least wise touching the tradition or tretises of authors, and like wise is the
use of oils that are made by descension or downwarde as in Rasis and Aetius. A
certain man (whose name I spare to rehers,) writ of late that these manner of
destilled liquors were not unknown to the latter Greek writers, using this
argument, that destilled liquors are sometimes named in the writings and works
of Actuarius, And in deed he names in so^e places Rhodostagma, as in his Iulep
against ye cogh: and a little following in an other Iulep intybostagma. But with
these words is signified nothing else the^ a simple syrup of Roses or Endive.
Rhodostactum does Agenita describe in his. 7. book, yt. 15. cha. with these
words. Sieth.ii. sextars of the juice of Roses yt nails whereof are taken away,
one sertar of honey skimming it til the.iiii. part be consumed. Ye a little
before also he describes Hydrorosatu^ rosed water, that also far differing
from a destiled liquor, or water destild for it consists of.iiii. pounds of
Roses the nails taken away.v. sextaries of water: ii. sextaries of honey. The
Arabians also or their expositors, when they speak of the water of any plant,
they understand the decoction of it in water: and likewise Nicolas Myrepsus
that writ in Greek of the compositions of medicines, whom it appears to be
a very late writer, by the barbarous words that he uses very often: wherein I
marvel this, that no mention is made by him of waters or oils prepared in
Chymistical instruments. Only the oil Capnistum or smoked, that is destilled by
descention, does he describe, as Aetius also. Moreover, the things that the
Chimistes make may I comprehend in.ii. kinds for the most part, that they be
either liquors or Massy things, the liquors again are either watery or oily: and
these either aerie or fiery. The Massy things, are either such as rest in the
bottom: or such as are carried upward: and that.ii. ways, either as pure bodies
and substauces, as they which they call sublimated, quick silver. etc. or Sout
for the remedies of the eyes with Phisicions. There be also other artificial
manner of preparings, wherewith the purest and most effectual part in medicines
is drawn out, and as it were the form is separated from the matter.

And although I am not greatly travailed nether in the Chymistical nor in the
other manner of preparings, but by the way have got the kuowledge of certain
things, partly by mine own experience in some things, and partlye of myfrends:
yet what so ever and how much so ever this is, I will communicate it with the
studions of Physic: not as though I entend to teach perfectly and absolutely
the very Art of destilling and preparing of other things, but as it were
writing unto such as now already are not all void and without knowledge of
these things, having either experimented them selves or red other men's wrytings.
For I have nothing so exrelle^t or secret, but I covet it might ge abroad to
the publik and common profit, and although in small wealth, yet have I ben ever
of nature ready to communicate and make any man priuye of any cunning I had,
which some refer unto simplicity or childishnes, other some better to the
liberality of my disposition. There be some that do utterly cloak and keep
secret their things, and that diverse kind of men: some for Ambicion, that
they may have wherewith to excel and pas other men: some also for
covetousness, to get gain thereby: other through the ignorance of the
aunciente writers, as though there were not put in wrytinge either the same or
far better by men of antiquity, which now are despised of many, and a folish
and insatiable lust is there alwais to find out new things. There wantes not such
as think, great and effectual medicines should be kept secret for this cause,
least uncunning men, as many practicioners be, without al reason almost, and al
learning, might abuse them, and convert those things which are invented for
the health of me^, to their destruction. To such would I answer, As no kind of
evil ought to be committed for this intent that any good thing might follow
thereof: so that which is good ought not to be left undone, least some evil
might ensue, for the world shall never be without them that will abuse good
things and profitable inuencions. But good men, simplely such good things as
they have, they make them common, and will not stick nor cese to do so because
they fear, that certain lewd persons may do hurt thereby. But I will leave of
this disputation, thye do I protest sincerely and as I thinck withal my heart,
that I covet by my example to stur up learned and good Physions, that laying
apart al Ambition, Covetousness, Ignorance, Envy, if they can bring any
excellent thing unto our profession, that they would do it gentlely and
publicly. As for the unlearned certes, they in ministering not only these great
and effectual medicines, of which sort we shall put diverse in wrytinge hear,
but meats also and most common drinks, out of time, do hasard and bring men's
lives many times to diseases and death: and it is well known that Hippocrates
writ how our Ptisan ministered not in due time, was the cause of death to a
certain man sick of the Pleurisy. These therefore ought to be left partly to
their ignorance, partly to their leude malapartnes. But they that have need of
the Physicion are to be admonished to do that in Physic, that all men are
wont to do in all other Arts: that is, to choose good Physicions, and learned,
and such as exercise and practice this Art by their profession, both with
reasons, as a part of Philosophy and also in the very actions and doings of
cures. But I return to the purpose. The preparing therefore (as I say) is of the
greatest moment and weight in every matter. In Rhetorik vtterans, gesture, and
pronunciation more almost move the minds of the hearers, then the very
argument that is handled. Hereupo^ it comes that things put in meter and in
numbers, do delight so greatly: which if a man utter in proos, they shall be taken
for cold, dnl and solishe. Hereof comes it that one being asked the question
what was the chiefest thing in an orator, answered pronunciation: what was the
next and the third: the same. Shows and plays, that they delight the hearers
and beholders so greatly, it is much more long of the form and manner of their
forniture and preparance then of the matter played or showed. In like manner in
the very things and works, the form, the fashion, figure, manner, as well of
nature as of Art, finally a certain preparation is more marked and commended
then the matter. Likewise in physsk, the conninge to prepare things aright,
and the descression to minister them aptly, have the greatest moment and skill
most. Nether is it of greater force what thou ministrest, then in what manner.
And although there be many circircumstances to be considered, that thou may
exhibit the medicin aright, yet the manner, and the preparing is with in the
medicine, and is at it were the form thereof and part the other circumstaunces
are without it, as time, place, and such things as are to be considred about
the sickman. But letting pas other ways of dressing and preparing at this
present, we will only touch those, by the which all the virtue and faculti or
operation is separated from the substance of the medicines, so that ye more
liquid and moist, the more pure and subtle part of every remedy or medicin,
may be bad severed and drawn out from the gross and erthy part: whether it be
gathered into a liquor drawn out of the sam remedy or medicin or into an other
certain ertarnall. etc. which the barbarous writer Arnold calls Eruirtuare
to outuerteuat, or Excorporare, to outcorporate. Furthermore, if sumthings shall
seem to be written somewhat curiously or double diligently, ye must consider
that suthe pertain not to the Physicions of the common people and pore fooke,
but to such as abound more with riches and ease, and have plenty of servants,
or such as remain in Princes or Kings courts: or also to Philosophers, that
busilye search out the marvelous mutacion and virtues of natural things, and
in them delight them selves. To conclude, let no man marvel that so great and
long commendacions and praises are added to certain medicines, as unto the
Quintessences as they call them, to Aqua vities, and made ba^mes, and that unto
some marvelous virtues are attributed, as to sharpen the wit and memory, to
co^serue the senses and youth. etc. When as even by the ancient Greeks and
Latins also we read such effects and operacions to be ascribed to Triacle and
other preservatives and compositions, specially by the Arabians unto diverse
things, yea even of Galen also unto Triacle. There be some that I will not auow,
and whose credit I leave to the authors, whose names I write every where. But
it is said before already, that we wryt all these things for the learned and
discreate men, which for the most part shall judge more easily how much credit
is to be given to every thing. Although in very many it is not sufficient to
be furnished with learning and judgment, except experience and practice be
joined also therewith. But I make an end hear of my Preface.

What Destillation is, and of diverse forms and kinds.
DESTILLATION not distillatio^ (as learned Siluius. Destillation by ascentio^ or
going upward, is when the vapors or fume is carried up and be there gathered
together into water and so drop down: The same author. Moist things put
into a body (for so do they call the bigger Siluius. Certain like things nature
has wrought, both in exhalatio^s above in the air, specially the^ the lower
parts. Therefore of a plant or any other substance ordained to be destilled:
what part of it is most meet to be extenuated and fynet (that is the purest
part, the lightest, the thinnest, the moistest, and the most superficial part
next unto the uttermost parts of the body) being first of all fined by the
force of heet, is lifted up: next such other parts as in puernes cum nie to
the first and last such a moisture of the things as is more gross that held
together the earthly parts, a certain (Pliny, I think, writes of the wode
that is called Smilax, how it willet sype through water mixed with wine, and it
true) but this is no destillation in deed, except unto such as speak
improprely. For that which is proprely called destilation is done by heet: and
that from the son, or of fire, corruption and rot tennes: By the son, as
certain men have inverted, to draw of flowers a kind of water, very nere to
them selves in smell and other pleasant qualities. By fire, that is, by flame
that come of aere and of aereall bodies: or else by burning cooles, that are
made of earth or earthly bodies enkindled,

Morouer the flame it self, as well as the cool, is diverse, not only in
respect of that it is great and little, but also of the wood whether it be
rotten and doated, or sound, stinking, or well smelling: grene or dry. Besides
this it is a great matter what bigness the furnace be of, what fashion, Syluius.
ascend up in their order: the lightest, subtilst, and hoatest first, that is,
the fire: secondarily the air: thirdly water: the earth remains in y^
bottom: and likewise I judge in the destillation of vinegar.

The nature of fire is to deminishe (as Cardane Balneum Mariae. The best kind
of destillation next unto this, is in horse dong. Then by ashes: the best in
this kind, is by the superfluous refuse of olives, after the oil is pressed
forth, for it being a hot and moist matter, will reteyne his heat very long,
yea a great meany of months: and so much the longer then the kurnels of
grapes, because the substance of the olives is thicker and fatter. But none

The heat of the first degree, which is moderate, as of horse dong, and Balneum
Mariae, is called the heat of digestion, resolution, putrefaction, maceration,

Of the virtues of licores destilled generally.
MAnarde in his Epistles. 15. book 15. chapter, says thus: when I perceived
in the commun waters that are destilled out of plants by fire, nether the smell
nor the taste remain, but many times the contrary (for the water of wormwode I
perceived to be chymici, by what devise both the smell and the taste might be
preserved in thies waters, that is found in the hoole herb. It were long to
write all the ways, which I proved to find out thies things: yet

Syluius. Waters destilled, reteyne and kiepe still the virtues of the simples
wheroute they be drawn: saving that they are more pure and strong, the oftener
they be destilled, which we prove in the water of wine, for the most part,
very seldom in other things.

The virtues of licore destilled, some do alter with some matter, as honey,
Ladanum and such like, or else by putting it in ye nose of the still, which
serves for the most part, to the grace of the smell: for they bind musk,
cloves, Caphura and such like

A doubt. If so be it yt fire makes al things hot and dry: all waters
gotten by destillation, ought to be hot and dry. Nether does the watery
substance disprove this, for when as brenning water is such, it does both bren
and mightly make hot and dry men's bodies. Again, al waters be cold and
moist, as long as the substance has the upper hand. But nether of thies is
always true: yea, some of thies ar more like unto

Therefore men be wont to axe whether these waters reteyne and kiep their own
propre virtues and strength. We once said, whiles we entreated of the evil
manner of curing, that they are no strengthes nor virtues, because they are
destitute of savor and taste. For water of wormwode, nether smells like
wormwode, nor is bitter yea that is more wonder, it is sumwhat swiete. Yet
burning water (to speak nothing of Rose water) well declares that there is a
certain virtue and efficacy in waters. For if you will say it is such
because of fire: how comes it to pas that none Cardan.

But me thinks thies things would be more diligently considered and weyed.
First, where he true, if a man destill it negligently as the co^mun apothecaries
ar wont, in leaden limbeckes. but destill it in Balneo Mariae, and see whether
it shall lack his smell and taste, or no. Yea, it shall retain boeth, and that
effectuously, not only this herb, but also any other plant whatsoever it be,
having any sauoure or taste: if it be dried and weikte or stiept in wine a few
days, then destilled in Balneo Mariae or by ashes gentely, as I shall show
further within. Now when certain things have Theophrastus writes, do retain
their savor very long: other sum be unlike to their plants. for such waters
as have their virtue and force in the uttermost and superficial parts, they
fume or else more nie to the midest or utter part of the same. I am surely of
that mind with Raimunde Lullus, that water of the same quality may be gotten
out of any plant whatsoever it be, of cold plants, cold water: of hot,
hot: of dry, dry: and of moist, moist. But I will not grant that the same
virtue remains, except like savor, or like taste or both, (as in smelling
things (be left.

The cause why the smell of certain flowers as of Iasmin, of the flowers of
cloves, remains not Cardane, where as we entreat of Balneu^ Mariae generallye.

It were good if every water once destilled, were powered again, yea or the
third time upon his own dreggees beaten, and to be suffered to putrify two or
three dais, and so to be destilled again: or rather, not upon his own dregs
that remain, but upon other like herbs of the same kind, and to putrify in a
circulatory or a blind limbeck, and to be destilled: Brunsvvick

I saw once an alchymist, that destilled not the very herbs renewing certain
times the destillatio^, and powering again ye water upon ye dregs ground
upon a marble moler: Gnaynerius.

Oats (whereof drink may be made as Ale or Bear of barley) do warm and make
dronk no less then wine. Men say that in Tartaria, water of milk destilled
makes men drunk. But every water, not an element (that is alone without any
mixture) but lyquor or juice mixed and compounded, being often destilled may do
the same: for it Cardane.

Also a lyquor or other thing be destilled, the thicker it is, the more it
seems to conceive heat and fire, if it be often destilled.

It is manifest (says Cardane) that a water may be made which shall
inco^tenent break the stone in the bladdar, if it be put in by a squirt or
syring. Tecolittius, or of the stones of crabes. for so may a water be made, to
break also the red marble. Moreover, that it shall not hurt the bladder, is
brought thus to pas, if the mattier out of which the water is taken be void of
any salt kind of thing, or alum, or coperoos, or of wine lies, but some of them
that we me^cioned erewhyle. But there is nied of diligent trial, in co^firming
a subtle means, that such things which we have serched out so subtly,
being surely confirmed first by experience and proof, we may then deduce and
bring them to the commodity of man.

In deed I know that pigeo^s donge and paritary either the one or the other,
destilled by this means, is able too break the hardest stoone that ever
Cardane. Perauenture also Chrysocolla would help unto this art, being
artificiusly made, and without sharpnes, such as is also praised of the
goldsmithes: wherefore to make Borace sum use rain water destilled, and milk
destilled, sum also honey marow: etc. I hard of late, a certain practicer cured
the stone Borace mixed with burning water, to the thicknes almost of honey,
mingling also Tartar, punned, or a stone cut out of a man or the grounds of
piss out of a pispot. He commanded that a man shall use this medicine by the
space of fourtene days, so that he should always mixed some with his wine when
he dronk, yea both at diner and supper. I remember I have red of certain
liquores, in which if a man put a stone or flint, it should be resolved. The
Chymistes and destillers use destilled vinegar, and

They dissolve with strong vinegar, chiefly destilled, or with the juice of
limons, perles, egg shelles, stones of the reins, of the bladder, both the
coralles, and they afterward dried, ar quickly and redily cro^med betwixt ones
fingers. Siluius.

I can not let pas here to speak of the water of Epiphanius the practicionar,
which is such. Re. Antalis et dentalis, boracis, sarcocollae whyt coral, white
crystal, claye, anessede, rys, meel of orobus trochiscos (little roules or
balls) with water of beans made with musk. The use of it is for women to
make their faces whyt and fair, but the face must be perfumed before with
water of a decoction of barley and oats: then let one baul be steept and
consumed in bean water, and anoint the face before you goo to bed, but in the
morning wash it away with water of a decoction of beans and bran: and again
with coold water. If the more beautify the face: for limones roasted and
anointed upon the face, they alone do beautify y^ face. If a man drink this
water fasting and anoint the place of his priuities where hear grows therewith,
it breketh the stone: which is prouid by this, that if a man lay Porcellanas in
it the space of a night, the next day he shall order them with his fingers
like warmed wax (Porcellanas men call certain shelles, and also precious
earthen vessels.) I have used the mo words in this, to give

Of the manifold use of lyquors destilled, both in physic, and otherwise.
I Do perciue a manifold use of destilled waters, but chiefly and most of all
for physicions, which use such stilled chymistically dressed and prepared, with
oyntmentes, either that they may have the better savor, or else to make them
hoater, and that they may perce the sooner, they put moist linen clothes in
thies voaters to cool and refreshe the parts of the body, parts about the
arms, and hot fyrie swellings. Surgeons use such waters as dry mightily,
to wash wounds withal. But the most common use, of long time has been in
mixture of syrups to be drunk, and to zulapia or iulebs, chiefly of roses and
violets. There be that make diverse kinds of liquors and oils alonly for the
good savor. Glasiars also that paint glass in baking in their colors, they
do use burning waters. Goldsmiths use aqua fortis (as they call it) which
signifies a Raymund Lullus writes of y^ marvelous use and commodity of
burning water even in wars, a little before the joining of battle, to styre
and encourage destillation is necessary. Sweet water may be separated from the
salt, in a great caudron with a great and hie keuer having a beacke or nose.

A way to purge and make clean troubled waters, out of Bulcasis.


FIll a great pot with the puddled water A and putting B lay two sticks or mo a
crose C upon the pot brinkes, and upon the sticks lay clean wol D well washed:
the^ whatsoever the woll drinks of ye vapors that ascend up, wring it out and
kiep it, and amilu^ or meel: for thies things go dounwarde, and draw with
them to the bottom of the vessel, the gross mattier of the water.

Of Balneum Mariae generally, and of those destillations that be done by vapors
of hot
Hot water or else ye vapor of the same, send les strength into the thing that
is to be destilled, then other fire alone or else such other dry means as are
me^cioned before. for yt cause as Galen says Diploma, that is, a double
vessel (the Apothecaries as the men that still lyquors also, call it Balneum
Mariae) melts, heateth, seatheth those things whose strengthes the violence
of fire will not dispers nor separate: so, such things as be tender and
gentle,



You must have a furnace A of this sor, upon the which you shall set a great
brazen vessel B full of water, C in a circle as many as it will receive, in the
botome, of the which vessels the things that you will destill, must be put.
Other build ye furnace A otherwise as though it were a vessels: B in their
broad bottoms standing inward, they contain the things that they will destill
the mouth without as though it were a bottell being covered, C in the which ye
vapor carried up by his open belly gathered together, and by the long mouth of
the same droppeth down. Syluius.



But why remains not the smell of certain flowers in the waters but in
Iasmin, Cariophillum, and le^is, the water comes forth wtoute savor? the
reason is declared otherwher: because yt unto so sclender and thin
substance, no substancial and thick part is joined. There Cardan. It appears
without doubt that those flowers should be destilled in Balneo Mariae, or in
vessels of glass

Balneum Mariae may be hansomly made hoate with a great pype of copper A set in
the midst, in the bottom whereof is a grate for ye B ashes to avoid at: men
call it communly a stowe harry. Vlstadius names it a furnace of sloth. Upon
that pype, do they make a cover of copper G with a small pype coming out a
shore, to carry and convey the smoke out at a window or some hoale. (So do they
also make warm house flors now a days to bathe in.) The commodity of this
pipe D is then E, a ten or twelve at once, to spare time, labor and cost.



Some use brazen cupping boxes to still withal in the Balneo Mariae, and
glassen limbeckes, whose noses if they be to short or broken, they join other

The herbs yt be to be destilled in a bathe or otherwise, some do pun them,
and let the^ remain so a while, (perauenture for certain days) before they
still them, thinking to have more plenty of water in a hot place, it were well:
but the most apothecaries and other, that sieke most for lucre and gain
thereby, leave it in cold places in open cofers, till the herbs lose their
smell, and be corrupted with a moist and gros air.

Some there be that put some sand also in the water of Balneum Mariae, to
the intent the heat may be the greater and more vehement: as Mathaeolus of Sena
in ye water that is called aqua philosophica against the frensh pocks. And he
affirms that

The water of Balneum Mariae ought to be no hoater, then that a man may suffer
his finger in it: Brunsuicensis.

Vlstadius prescribes the destillation of a certain kind of aqua vitae in
Balneo Mariae, to be done with so slow a fire, that a man may tell one, two,
three, until seven, before a drop fall.

Of such things as pertain to the commun wai of Balneum Mariae, you shall read
more with in where we make mention of aqua Camphorae out of Bulcasis, and of
Rose water out of the same.

This way of destillation in Balneo Mariae, is used also to the rectifying (as
they term it) of oils, to draw and purge the fleame from them, for only the
waters and nothing else may be lifted up and drawn out by the heat of the bath,
the oil remaining still in the bottom.

When as I perceived that waters, as they are accustomed commonly to be
destilled out of pla^ts, they kiep nether their taste nor the same smell: I
began carefully to think upon the matter and to try many ways, yt I might
find a means how to preserve and kiep in the waters the same qualities. It
should be long to rehearse all the ways yt I proved. Only one will I touch, which
seemed to me to be the best and ye easyest. That is this, to work on this wise
in a double vessel, after the manner that Galen would all oyntmentes to he
made, of that vessel A wherein the herb is contained B, do not touch the water
C that boyleth in the bigger D, but that it wax hot with nothing else then the
fuming moisture lifted up from the same. Manardus. Albeit things destilled in
this wise, although they of the plants (as the former smell, yet for as much as
they be corruptuous, they can not be long kept: Syluius.

The chief use of dong, or as some term it, a horse belly, is such: that the
matter which is to be destilled in a glasen vessel set in the dong may be
prepared by the heat thereof, as we shall declare more at large in his place,
where we shall make mention of putrifactio^ and rotting. It is possible
notwithstanding for destillation to be brought to pas in y^ Brunsvvick
supposes to excede in the middle degree, the heat of Balneum Mariae.

If you desire to have a water destilled of the flesh of any beast, you shall
strangle the beast, yt it bleed not in any wise, then take away all the fat
Brunsvvick.

The parts of beast or excrements, as blud, the liver, the lights, eggs, gall,
and ox dong, ought to be destilled in horse dong, with vessels not very close
stopped (but honey and milk a man may stop the^ close) least the water stink. But
if it chance to in Balneo Mariae, specially the water of excre me^ts of oxen
whose first destillatio^ is scarce found without stench: Brunsvvick. It would
frame better if a man put to a little curtsy of salt in the putrefying or
destilling of dong, that they may corrupt the less.

OF Worm wode water Johannes Mesuae has made mention: and of it and of Rooses

Which commonly they call Morsus Gallen

Caepa.




THe whyt Onion destilled breketh the stone. Martianus Sanctus.

Cerasus.


CHery water of what kind soever they be, is drunk against to much heat,
and is ministered with out ye body: but particularly of sweet black cheries,
which also is commended of many against the palsy, if it be poured into the
mouth: and the mouth be well washed therewith, that it restores the use of the
tung lost. They destill the flesh of it alone, or the kernels also beaten
together, that ye liquor destilled thereof may entye out the stony matter of the
reins and bladdar. The black and sower ar called Visula, they yield agues
which couleth and confirms the strengthes: it is profitable also against
thirst and bluddye flixe: Ryffius. The water destilled of the swiete black and
fresh Cheries, is maruelously commended of Remaclus F. of Lymburg: As Soon
(says he) as it shall be powered into the mouth of one sick of the falling evil
already taken with ye fit (the potion of it is.iiii. drammes or moor) by and by
he reuiueth refreshed, neither is he any moor drawn together with any cramp,
til in the ordinary

Camphora: water thereof or oyll is thus made. Take one of the vessels for Rose
water, that is called baten (that is a belly) and fill it with the sticks or
cips of pynappull tree which has great and brood leaves, and let it be
filled. Siluius takes it so as though the roose water should be poured to the
chips of the pynappull tree, but me thinks yt cucurbita or belly, that it may
be a certain repeticion of the same which he had spoken before, and let it be
covered with a vessel having a nose: then put the belly into a brazen vessel
full of water over the fire, till it begin to boil: for an oyll shall destill
(and yet they deny that oyll may be lifted up by the heat of water) subtle of
a good odor, which is called water of Camphora. Or if ye list destill it
destilled. Bulcasis. But Belluensis says that water of Camphora according to
the Arabians (says he) is a water that runs out of the tree that brings
the Camphora: which as his tree also is of a hoot nature in third degree: so
Camphora it self is cold: Monachi in Mesuae. Put three little bies in the vessel
of glass where the Camphora is, which shall so be turned into water.

Fragaria.


SStraweberies shall y^ putrify in a Vessel of bras (peraue^ture salt may be
put to it, or sugar) and destill them. This water (says Lullus in his.ii.
book of quintessence) is holso^ a woman having newly all over her face
blesters or wheals by the struck of a ston with heet, which the only washing of
this water was straight way heled with great admiration. But the virtue there of
is a hundred foold mor marvelous and stronger in operacion with burning water,
and much moor with quintessence. Yea, this water mixed with quintessence or
with burning water cures the Leprosye.

Fraxinus.


Three ounces of the liquor destilled of the inner bark of ash, with as many
ounces of whyt wine is dru^k against the pestilence, and the same drinck after.

it be drounke ons or twyes a week.

Which we call Parietary or Pelitory, the water thereof is profitable against

DEntdelion, in french with us it is called Dandelion, the water thereof siemeth

Iuglans.


THe water of walnuts not ripe made about saint Ihons tide, ministered without,
is good for wounds and hot byles, and the pestilent anthrax. Also being
drunk a two or three ounces, it cools and resists the pestilence. A water
also is destilled of the utter husks of walnutes (either ripe, so that they be
new brast and left of the nut, or not yet at all) in the month of September,
nether skilleth it if they be black, so yt they be not rotten: yet the black
are counted the best. A moderate potion if it invade a man with heet) letting
blood first, is given to drink against the pestilence, as a certain
experiment. It is praised also for ye noise in the ears and the difficulty of
heering, and for the diseases in the throat called angine being gargild. A
water destilled of ye leaves of walnut tree about the end of May, is maruelously
commended for the drying and knitting of sores, and to bring them to a skar, if
they be washed therewith morning and evening, and a linen cloth moystened
therein be Brunsvvicencis.

SAtyrion is destiled roots and al, good for ye falling evil (as men say.)

Rosa,


ROos water seems to be first invented: for I find mention of it in Auicen
the.ii. book, in the chapter of rooses: Roosewater drounke (says he) is good
in a Geleniabin, and helps to digest. And the roos and his juice are good
for a stomak to mich hoot. And although the Arabia^s for the most part ar wont
to say water for juice or Mesue in the chapter of Rooses. And again, The Roos
and his oil and water sublimated comfort the heart. etc. The same speeketh of
rooswater destilled, in the.vi. destinction, whereas he describes the iuleb of
roses, as the Munkes his interpreters do prove: of whom also this was and nomo
mentioned in Mesue, that is of rooses and wormwod. They make no epithe^ or
outward medicine at this day, but they put roosewater in it. In sharp vehement
and greet inflammations, to strengthen the principal members, it may be
commodiously used. It is good for the flixe of the bowels and vomiting. It
helps the inflammation of the eyes very much in the beginning. Sum are wo^t
to mixed with it a little thucia and sugerca^dy. It strengthens the eyes and
sharpeneth the Remaclus F. Of wine mixed with roswater in time of meet, see in
Arnold in his book of wine.

Rooswater although it be made diverse ways yet the best is made by Balneum
Mariae: Matthaeolus. If thoos rooses which we call commonly carnacion, dried
and moystened with the vapoure Syluius. They may be holden in a linen cloth
over hot water, til they have drunck enough of the vapoure.

Of Roose water out of Bulcasis.


THe making of Roosewater is known in most cou^tires. It is better made with
water then without: better also by fire of cooles then of Aharach. A. Thou
shall make therefore in a large house by a wall a little berchile B so does he
call the vessel that is filled with water the bottom and sides shall be of leede,
so well closed, that it leek not in any place. Then make meet a and make two
or three round hooles in it C C C moore or les, according to the largnesse of
the vessels, and as you desire to either much or little water D, then make a
pot of bras like to the pot made in Balneo Mariae, which thou shall set upon
the fornaice, so that the Berchile above the furnaice be lower in situacion
then the pot, (so that it may conduite the heat of the fire of the
Berchyle, to the pott: but me thinks thies words do either redounde or be
depraved.) Thou shall also is finished, put away the first rooses, and put in
fresh: thus says Bulcasis. Some man would marvel, that in such a making of
the furnace, where the fire is not put under the duble vessel or berchile (as
I conjecture, seeing he makes the bottom and sides of leed) but at the side of
the furnace, wherefore he should think it to skill any thing, whether the fire
put under the pot be made of wood or cooles. Moorouer it is known (says
Bulcasis in the same place) that rosewater of wyld

There is a destilled water made of thies with us a shorter way then that
before, is this wise.



A A brazen pot such as diers use, is set to a wall whereunto a cover B made
meet with hooles, wherein the be D it of the croppinges of vynes or such like.
But in process of the destillation thou shall close the mouth of the furnace til
the hot destillation be finished. if thou burn cooles, the water shall be the
swieter. (Here is more reason then in the former, because the fire here is made
immediately under the stillatory vessels.) The second way of destillacion with
out water with fire of coals is such.





MAke a square or round furnace A with a cover C made of glased earth, so that
they may abide the fire: And when the cooles are kindled, and the water
begins to destill shut the mouth of the furnace* and leave holes open for
the smoke to go out at. There is an other bridgemente of the third and fourth
way.



A Brazen pot A full of water is set over a furnace, with a cover bored B through
C more or les. Put under fyr of the cropping of vines or cooles till the water
sieth.

Sedum.




THe water of ye least syngrien or houslieke, Remaclus. F.

Solanum.




THe water of Solanum or Morrella is good against all agewes if the sick the
day of his fit abstain from all meat and drink: and a cloth dipped in it
be laid upon. It helps also the liver that is hot, and the lunges that are
dried and grieved with an Hecticke fever. Against all these diseases it ought
to be drunk with the third part of wine. Lullus in his book of waters.

Tilia.


OF the flowers of Tilia whose smell Tilia beaten. The dosis or quantity of
ministration is one or one and a half. It asswageth the griefs of the eyes:
and heals the places brent with fire or any hoot matter, and that tiliae be
stiept in this water or instead thereof the grains or kernels of quinces, or
psyllium, and the places be anointed with the horines or mouldines that bredeth
there upon: Ryffius.

Some use against the pestile^re a liquor drawn by the force of fire out of
the blood of a graye or badger.

Also of the blud of duckes against poison. etc. whereof thou shall read more
in the book of destillarions of Ryffius, written in Dutch.

Some mixed the blood of a goat with medicines against the stone to be destilled.

A water composed of the blud of a barrow hog and other diverse medicens will we
describe hereafter in some place.

I would adioyne here a table of waters destilled of plants, which are
described in the Dutche books of destillacions of Hierom of Brunsvvick, and for
the most part all that Gaulterus Ryffius has borrowed of him: saving that I
study to be short. as liquors also may, and are wont to be destilled of all
such plants whereof there is any use in physic. But to reckon up also the
virtues and faculties of every of them as some do, it is superfluous when as
none other for the most part, but even the very same also be attributed and
ascribed to ye waters which are unto the plants: so that these repetycions
move irckesomenesse to the reader, yea even if it be but meanly learned, never
a whit les then colewortes twice sod. Yet because Remaclus F. has described. I
will ad hither a rehersall and table of them, in like order as he uses and has
recited them him self.

Of waters destilled of beasts, or of their parts, or excrementes, and first
of all of the hoole beasts.
A Water destilled of whelps, will make that heir shall not grow again. And,
Furnerius. I give little credence to thies curious and exquisite remedies, and
although they be true, yet I do not allow them, specially

A young Storck some bid strangle and destil it like rose water and therewith to
anoint the parts taken with palsy, or shronke together, and at certain times
to be washed away with a decoction of sea crabes without salt: they say it
helps marueilously, if a man continue it. Some bid put an ounce of Camphora
and a dram of amber, in a young storkes belly the bowels taken out, but it must
be one that never yet flew: then in destilling, to gather

Of the liver and lights of a Calfe.

The liquor of milck destilled, the chymistes and destyllors do use: and sum
that go about to make Borax or Crhysocollam. It is a wonder that men say,
amongst the Tartarians, water destilled of milk, does make men drunk. The
milk must Carda. Some use water destilled of wine and milk together against
ye fever quartain: specially in England, as Brudus Lusitanus writes: Some
drink it against the iaundys, as witnesses Johan. Goeurotus. Ryffius.
