Of kowes donge look in the same.

Some say that water destilled of man's donge will heal fistulaes, also fretting
soores, and such Tinea or matering of the head: that it will also make skarres
like unto the other skin: and put away the spots or white webbes of the eyes.
If it be druncke, it is good for them that have the falling sickness: it helps
them that have the gowte, it drives away the stone out of ye rains and
bladdar, it is a preservative against the bitinge of a mad dog or other
venomous beast. But the oil of the same which destilleth after the water, with
a greater fire, is much better to fistulaes, and ca^cres, Matthaeolus of Sena,
and other, you shall read certain merueilous things in Brunsvvick.

Man's urine destilled, the Chemists do use to resolve certain mettalles:
Printers to make their printinge ink, but these destill it in Rose
stillatoryes.

Of vessels and diverse instruments belonging to destellation.
WHen men name a stillatory vessel, they understand the body of the still or
the Cucurbita, which the dutch men in their language, because of the fashion of
it, do call clauam (ein kolben.) The body or gross vessel some call the greater
vessel or Cucurbita wherein they put that which is to be destilled. Syluius for
this vessel compared to the limbeck or head, is Arabians name it, as Bulcasis
witnesses, Beten Batan, that is a bellye. It appears also that Atanor in ye
same Bulcasis, where he prescribes the destillation of vinegar, signifies a
Cucurbita. (A furnaice, Athanor or a fixed instrumente Geber describes in his
book of fornaces. The Cucurbita some barbarously do call bocciam a box. Of
the same ^igure and fashyon the greater vessels are meet to digest and
putrify: thereupon when they must be destilled, the matter digested is
distributed into some les Cucurbit or boxes. The limbecke is taken, as I
suppose, both for the body and for the head, but moore properlye for the
head, as I said, is the upper vessel, that has a certain similitude of
the head, in respect of the nether, that is bigger and longar. The same vessel
(late authors call a bell and (ein helm) a salet or helmet. Tubus which comes
forth of the limbeck a length turning downward, is called a nose, a beak, and
simple as it is a pipe: because the through the holownes thereof, the drops
gathered together in the limbeck, which all come together fold about the
lowest part of the limbecke, running forth do drop down into a vessel set
underneath, which commonly is called a receiver and a vrinall. The nose and
beak are termed of y^ like figure, because this part stands out, after the
same manner that beakes do out of the noses of fouls, and noses of.iiii. foted
beasts. The receiving vessel when as oils are destilled out of metals, as of
Vitriol or Corporoos, whereas there is need of a great fire, and continual,
must be very large: for

A blind limbek is that which has no nose nor beak, nor limb or hembe which
serves for preparation and rectification or circulation, that is, perfection.
vitrial.

Such heads as be put upon bodies not standinge vpryght, but as though they
were lying, they lack the limb folded skirt, and be like to commune earthen
Syluius.

I have sien also two heads one upon the other, that by the nether which was
bored through, part of the matter might be carried into the upper, which was
joined to the nether with nails, least y^ nether that is stopped with clay should
be taken away, when as

Some times the fashion of the head is like to a spyre ascending upward aloft,
sometime edged: sometime it waxes brood in the highest part of the head, that
it may receive the more of the vapors, Syluius.

Sublimation is taken of many simpely for destillation: other take it
particularly to lift up or cause to ascend up into the limbeck by the force of
fire, the matter that is to befixed in it: as many metally things ar
sublimated.

Some join glasse^ or earthen bottels together, which they call horned pypes,
the one whereof is set and cemented or clayed in the fire containing y^ things
to be destilled: the the other is a pretty way distaunt from Syluius. Thies
instruments are called commonly crooked: the frenchemen call them also horned.

This horned pipe is a musical instrument, which the Germans commonly call

The receiving vessel for the most part is a phiall of glass with is put, and
some times the belly thereof is set in a pot or some other holowe vessel that it
may stand the stedfaster in his place, or else for the commodity of the
thing, is set one way or other.

Vessels for circulation as they wherein the vapors ascend by course, and
again turned into lyquor, desce^d and go dounward as we shall declare within
where we shall make mention of putrificatio^ Of al thies the most excellent
vessel is that which is named Hermes vessel, because he was the inue^ bill:
eue^ so this vessel as though it were eared on both sides, has pypes that
beginning at ye head, that is the upper part, descend in manner of a half a
circle and ar bowed again and fastened as it were into the beginning of the
Cucurbita with a blind limbeck without a folded skirt or else a vessel of a
hooll glass, that is somewhat straiter in the midst, and out of the beginning of
the belly it has looking out a short pype, by the which liquor may be powered in
and out. We shall put the fygures of both thies within in the chapter of
quintessence: fornaces every where in this work.

The forms and fashions of vessels ar diverse, and almost without number
commonly used with the chymistes and destillers, Syluius. Who so desires the
figures and names of diverse vessels, let him look in Brunsvvick. Ryffius
Andro. Louicerus and other. A man may cause to be made, of what fashion he will
in the glass makers shops, such as be in Heluetia nie unto Scaphusia, and not
far from Basil and Solodourn. But the best

Of the vessel called aludel^s, look in Geber in his book of chief
perfection. 1. 4. 44. chapter, and again in his book of fornaces. Albertus
writes it a luttell, and expounds it a vessel appointed for sublimation:
likewise Bulcasis. The same wills arsnick to be sublimated in an earthen
dyshe glased, the fashion whereof he describes in Belluencis.

Of the matter for vessels of destillacion: and first against leaden and brazen
vessels.
WAters destilled in Balneo Mariae, do so far pas those that are made simplye
by the tire in leaden limbeckes, as gold Balneo Mariae, with large chaplets,
limbeckes or heads, and somewhat great and of glass, do give the natural savor
and taste of the herbs and flowers, wherout they be taken, without any noisome
smell of smoke or burning, which chances not al in the common leaden stilles.
For the waters that are made in them, very few and seldom, specially whiles
they be new, are without notable lothsomnes of smoke and burntnes, which unto
sickmen that drink, it is not only grievous, but also hurtful: Balncum
Mariae, and although it shall be a little more chargeable unto them and
painful, yet so shall they satisfy and please both God and man. Matheolus.

Such things as are destilled in lead, I judge them altogether to be
disalowed, because of ye Cerusse and other malicious qualities of the lead,
lead, Galen condemns, because it bredeth diseases in the entrails. Syluius.

Moreover the vessel alters much, as well in destillacions as in things
sodden. Wherefore a man must take great hied in these things, that the vessels
be rather earthen or glass then of metal, and those that be of earth, yt they
be of a very pure earth, and well baked, such as be the earthen vessels of
Paris, Bellonaca, England and Spain. But glasen vessels are the best because
they be more Galens reason in every one almost of these kinds of metals. For
if he affirm that water only conveyed in leaden pipes does bread diseases of
the bowels, how much more ought we to fear waters destilled in a leaden
limbecke or still. Besides that no small cerusse remains cleauynge to the
inner side of a leaden head, as in destillinge of Vinegar is gathered, so by
the force of the vehemente heat or brentnesse also tarte, that is of sharp
and tarte plants, which infecteth the very wa^ers, and for the most part
makes them white like milcke, till that it be settled and suncke to the
bottom. Vessels of other metalles that bried other roust or canker, are so
much the more pearilous, as rouste or canker is more hurtful the^^cerusse.
Vessels made of pure silver or gold, as they be least hurtefull, so are they
more hard to be gotten because of the cost. Syluius.

The best vessels be of glass: the next, earthen that be glased both within
and without: then limbeckes of tin, the bodies (in destillinge of Roses) of
lead. Thirdlye, the limbeckes and the bodies also of lead. For the strength
of the fire if it be moderate, it hurtes not the lead, but these bodies of
lead must be set in syfted ashes, a handful thick, not in sand.
Fourthlye limbeckes of copper tinned within. Fifthlye brazen. But copper and
brass have two discommodities, Christophorus de Honestis, admonishes to take heed and avoid them: Brunsvvick. Therefore saying all the most learned
Phisicions, and experyence it self, do utterly disalowe waters destylled in
vessels of lead, Brass and other unclean metals, matter to the Magistrates,
that it might not be lawful for Apothecaries other to prepare any more or
sell such waters partlye hurtful, partlye unprofitable. Bulcasis in deed,
uses leaden bodies in destillinge of dry Roses, which perauenture may be
granted for such waters as ought to be used only without the body. Some find
no fault with vessels made of pure and best iron, because no evil qualitye
(as they say) can issue thereoute into the matter or liquor: and troth it is,
that certain Berchile. Bulcasis does call a vessel in a furnace for Rose
water, which receives the boylinge water derived out of an other standing
by it, in the which vessel, the body of the still containing the Roses, is set.

Of Fornaces, etc.
I Declared before and described two manner of fornaces meet for destillation
by ashes, and Balneum Mariae also out of Bulcasis.

Hear will I trace out and descrybe another manner of fournayce, the use
chiefly when as any thing is to be destiled by a great fire, so that no mean
be betwixt the fire and the cucurbite or pan wherein is contained the matter
that is to be destilled: after which manner oils or certain liquors that burn
the skyn and flesh are gotten out of metals, as aqua fortis. etc. The use
thereof shall be also to sublimation: and if a man will set a kettell in at the
top upon theyron cross, then fill it with ashes or water and put clay about it,
he shall be gin what destillation he will. aqua fortis or such like, he shall
set a cucurbite or pan dawbed with clay by and by, right upon the cross. Yea
this furnace may be made also upon a strong board of wode, as I have made my
self, that you may remove it from one place to an other at your pleasure.
Therefore upon a woden table you shall buyl a four square furnace, of such
bigness, that it may be sufficient to set in a mean circubite. The walls you
shall make of brick, joined with mortar very well made, the hight shall O. where
as the ashes may be taken out, which fall from the coals from the grate
builded upon it. If so be it that an other grate were made also for the ashes
to fall through (and that lowest space to be somewhat dieper) a man should not
niede to take out the ashes so often, and the fire should be the more vehement.

The letter. I. shows an other door, streighte way above the grate, which I
have markte with certain pricks set overthwart in order. That door as the
nether also, it is sufficient if it be only so big as a man may easily put in
his hand at it. V. shows the iron cross, that is.ii. bars of iron, ye nether
whereof must have a bought downward in tee midst to receive the other in that is
laid overthwart him. A. and. E. declare the highest corners of the furnace, in
which so many breathing hooles (or ventes as they commonly call them) ought to
B. the board upon which the hole furnace is builded is noted with. C. as
apeareth in the figure here present. An other kind of furnace, for oil of
vitriol or corporoos, Balneum Mariae, y^ have already discribed before, whereas
we entreat of Balneum Mariae generallye out of Syluius. Also in yt making of
Rose water, out of Bulcasis. Of diverse and destillacions, there is a book of
one called Geber. Concerning the making of a furnace, look in his book
entitled Summae perfectionis. that is, of chief perfection. 1. 4. 43. Of
commune Rose stilles in earthe^ pottars fornaces, although some make them also
of bras, we shall speak with in, by and by after the destillation by ashes.

Of slow Harry, or the furnace of sloth, that is, a pype wherewith the Balneo
Mariae is made hot, we have written above where as we have entreated Balneo
Mariae.

The differences of coals shall we know in Theophrastus, but better of our
own smithes, specially goldsmithes, and of the very experience it self. Such
coals as ar dug out of the ground are condemned and forbidden because of
their foul smell. They are counted the best that are made of bieche or fir
tree.

Dioptra or a little skrien I call an instrument, which some men hold before
their face with their

Lyquors destilled should be kept in glasen phialles with narow mouths. Let
the mouth be shit with a stopple of wod, or rather of cork, and besides that,
a man must cover it with wax and bind a parchement about it also.

How to close vessels and to defend them, both with clay and otherwise.
CLaying of a thing, men do commonly say for yt which is to daub with clai, to
plaster, to pariet. Tothinte^t we may the better keep the smell and qualities
of such things as are destilled, we must close diligently and join together
with Vlstadius chap. x If the head be not miet inoughe for the body but somewhat
to big, you shall put paper round about between it and the clay. Without also
whereas about moist paper about it, or else a linen cloth, and after clay it.

The mortar to join the cucurbita with the limbecke withal. etc. ought to
be made with claye and hear, and to be dry before fire be put under, Bulcasis
entreating of oil of tilestones. The mortar shall be the stronger if it be
tempred not only with hear or woll, but also with gotes blood, and the more
also to be made with that is dros of iron, beaten as is found about smithes in
fields.

Of Hermes seal, that is, of joining together the mouths of glasen vessels
with a pair of hot burning tongues softlye thrusting them together, and how
after they ought to be opened again, read Vlstadius. cap. 20.

Let the cucurbita also be fensed round about with clay, specially those that
must abide the fire directly put under them and nothing betwixt, and that aqua
fortis and oil of Vitrial are destilled. For the destillation in ashes, there
needs no such mortering nor claying: albeit if a man will provide for the worst
considering the frailnes of the vessels, specially where many vessels are
set together in one furnace, it shall be sufficient once or twice to pariet
the^. Those cucurbitas which they use in Balneo Mariae, specially when many
are set together, some men use to like as it were hoose or cases.

They say glasen vessels will abide the fire better and longer, if they be
wared, that is, if they be warmed and don over twice or thrice with molten wax.

Of the preparation for destillation.
FOr as much as in destillacio^ we seek ye separation of the elements either
for one or mo of them, or else to thinte^t that they quintessens: separation
truly can not be don without heat. For heat unites and gathers together
such things as be of one kind and nature, and they that do differ and
disagree it separateth to the intent this might duly be brought to pass, both
the Chemists and Phisicions have invented diverse means and ways. They term
this preparacion diversely and give it sundry names, digestion, leauening,
putrefaction or rotening. Digestion they call it respectinge, the concoction
that a certain inward virtue, but increased or styred up by an outward heat,
comes unto an humor or moisture, so that a certain common and general
quality be mixed and spread throughout the h^ole body, with a hot spirit, and
one that moves and breadeth bubles, as it is in ye mixture of true leaven in
deed knode^ with meal: also in vinegar powered upon the earth. And this affect
is the beginning of rotennes in such things as have superfluous humor. That
which is called putrifactio^

The savor or other quality of ye putrefying matter, in my mind, it is not
possible it should infect the mixture that is put in a glasen cucurbita with
glass is: for the common grene glass will gather a certain duskishnes and as
it were a skin.

Destillation that is done by rottennes, or with horse dong both alone and with
lime be sprinkled and often changed, or with barks watered, or other rottening
things, how so ever it is profitable to the Chymistes and destillers many
ways, a physician not withstanding ought not to receive it, for a cause in
rotten wod and evil smelling, through the rottennes or some other cause:
specially if the Syluius.

Of this kind of destillation in horse dong read before in the latter end of
those things which we writ of Balneum Mariae generally. At this present we
should entreat rather of rotening then of destillatio^. But because of
rotening also in horse donge seems to be disalowed, if destillacion in it be
reproved, it is not altogether from our purpose

Putrification or rotening in horse dong with lime is otherwise done on this wise,
that the dong every day or every other day or by longar distaunce, be
sprinkled with warm water: otherwise, without sprinkling, when as the dung of
it self serves to be hot enough. A pit or hole being digd in some corner of
the house, they lai one course of horse do^g about a foot thick, that is three
handbread: then an for the by course. There is need of three burden for the most
part of dong, as much as a porter is hable to carry.

Some put the things that are to be destylled (specially such as be
stiepte in wine) into a tin botel, and that they do set in vnslaki lime closed,
which they quench at certain times now a little and then a little, with
rain water. Some mixte Oten straw with the horse donge, and sprinkling it with
hot water, set in their vessel, Cardanus teaches. If a man covet but a
light heat, it shall be sufficient to put it simplye in chopte straw.
Certain of the Germans that live in stouffes that is hot houses, the winter
time, make A. and in the vpperparte they set two or three glasen pottes. C.
upon little iron bars and pariet and rub them over diligently with clay.
In thies filled full of fine sand or ashes, both things may be destilled
which I would should be tried, and also certain lyquors may be rectified or
prepared. etc, and by this means a man shall save both labor and cost.

Some to digest the matter which they will destill in the son, provide a
holowe lokinge glass. D. beams. B. which they received: and direct them into
the vessel C. wherein the matter is contained: for this purpose (as I am
informed) the figure of a holowe parabolae should be more apt and meet: a
parabolae conus. Read Archimedes in his Book of burnnynge glasses.

The same also may be done very well with balls of Cristall. A* hanged between
y^ vessel B. so yt the beams of the sun may com unto the vessel. C by the
balls. The only end of all these preparations is, that the liquor may be
drawn out more easily, more abounda^tly, and more

The time of this digestio^ does vary according to the matter, which the more
massy it is, the longar time does it require: new herbs need the less time,
when they are stiept or weekt in wine or other liquor: for they be the
tenderer, and if they should be let stand long, they would have a certain
hoarinesse: the same dried must stand a little longar: then the seeds: last
of all the rotes: so that almost double time is necessary for them that follow
to yt which goes before them: as to new

The putrification of herbs to be destilled in horse donge, is wont to be
done almost by the space of.xiiii. days, but in Balneo Mariae. ii. or.iii.
natural days. Brunsvvick.

Such things require chiefly putrification, whose substance is somewhat dry,
gros, thick: raw have les nied which are contrary wise, and ar digested

Putrification is sooner done in cloudy wheather then in fair. Vlstadius.

To the most part of things that are prepared in horse dong, some salt is
cast, as unto beasts blood, to flesh, to fyshe: Guainerius.

To digest, take a blind lymbeck: if the matter be pure, as pure liquor, then
take a vessel for circulation, of which sort the pelecan is the best:
although it can skarsly be made, and not without great cost. etc.

But in digestion or putrification, the hoal vessel would be hid with horse
dong or with the refuse of grapes. etc. To circulation the half of the
vessel, or at the least the third part, must look out into the free and cold
air (as also to destillation.) Vlstadius chap.viii. where as he teaches at
large of putrification in horse dong.

Of the rectification of liquors destilled.
WAters destilled in Balneo Mariae, ought to be set in the sun and to be
rectified, namely in a glasen vessel, (the mouth whereof let it be bound
about and stopped with lether) full of the destilled water, so that the third
part must be left empty. then set it in hot sand by the space of forty
days, that all the fleme may be consumed, the third part of the vessel must
poured first into wine or burning water, least they should hurt a man by the
means of their over much heat, let them stand for the space of a monthe or
more if the water be hotter, in moist sand, in a cold place. Likewise the
third part of the vessel hid in the sand: or two parts of the vessel (two
third parts) let them be digd in the earth in some wine celler. Brunsvvick.
Every water if it be destilled again, specially upon the dregs and grounds
well pund and broken, it may so be rectified: quintessens must be added.

But ye the brentnes yt is in these, may brethe out it shall be best to keep open
a while the phials wherein the liquors stand: not withstanding you must take heed that the grace of the smell in the mean season and with it much of the
strength and virtue thereof, do fume out. Syluius. Destilled liquors of diverse
sorts if a man mixte them together sometimes they become troubled and pudled
like thin milk or whay, and for the most part an old lyquor make them clear
and pure again, if into three pounds, you cast six or eight drops of white
vinegar very sharp: for they will drive down the pudly matter to the bottom:
Brunsvvick.

Destillacion by a Filter or a list of Woollen cloth.
DEstillation by a filter is more in ure with the last vessel only and lowest
shall have no listes, which must only receive, not yield forth. The
destillation by a filter is done also in two crooked vessels of glass well
clayed, the one where of must stand higher then the other, when you will draw
precious and swiet smellinge liquors from the matter (digested first in Balneo
Mariae,) as Vlstadius makes mention. Chap. 56.

To the sirrup which is made of the sower juice of the citron, that juice must
a man destill by a filter, or elsse it will gather into a courd when it waxes
cold, and likewise ye juice of Lemons and Orenges.

By a list some destill also the decoctions of herbs sod with a soft fire,
which afterward by little and little they sieth till it be as thick as honey,
as of the rotes of black Elleborus or bears foot. Other thinck it sufficient
only to sethe it or strain it.

Of burninge water or single Aqua vitae, and of the strength thereof, and
manifold use.
BVrnynge water or Aqua vitae is drawn out of wine, but with us out of the
wine lies only, specially of them that sell it, and by this only almost get
their living. And parauenture it is never a whit ye worse that it is drawn
out of lies: for Lullus teaches it be destilled often, it shall be made the
more effectual, that is to say, hotter and drier: for dregs also or lies
burnt, are very hot, and give the hottest oil, which they call oil de
Tartaro, but that burning water that is destilled out of wine, me thinks it
shall be the swieter, and more plesaunt both in smell and taste, and without all
brent taste: besides that more apt also for quintessens. Burning water is
called also water of sapa, of which and oil, mingled together soope is made,
for it brenneth ye Monachus in Mesuaem.

Burning water often destilled, is brought into such sharpnes yt it can not be
drunk. Cardan. But this is commonly known: they that sell it commonly
serpentins or writhen or crouked vessels or in other accustomed vessels (with
pipes of brass as big as a man's arm) may afterward be perfected in ashes,
until all the steame be put away: by the which means it is made not only
hoter and more subtle and finned, but also more pleasant both form or
fashion of the bodies and heads which they call limbeckse, to destill all
^uyces: for some draw the water out of wine put in a great brazen vessel,
standing upon the fire, the neck or mouth whereof is great and broad, upon a
three foot, and upon this neck stands the head of brass also with a top
like a spire steple, and a thing like a paile or bucket. D. compassing it, full
of cold water, that the large vapoure may be thickened the sooner by the head
made cold, and least the water of ye wine Syluius. We did see diverse fashyons
of vessels set out in figures to destill burning water or aqua vitae, in
Gualter Ryffius and Andro Lo nicerus. Bulcasis says wine may be destilled
after the same manner, as vinegar, the destillacion where of he described. Read
within, straight after the title of destillacion in ashes.

Some make a long round pype. A. going vprighte from the cover of the
Caudron containing the wines or lies, which pype is larger beneathe after the
fashion of a figure in Geometrye called conus which they bow at the top
almost with a straight corner, and by a bucket full of water not a crouked nor
wri then course: and so they say, shall a man have more plenty of water. Such
like little instruments certain apothecaries do make, and call them bladdars,
for this purpose, that if they need

Aqua vitae is thought then to be stilled enough, that is, after it be stilled
four times at the least, and pourged from all his fleame, when as if a Aqua
vitae so often destylled should atteyne, ought too be lyghter it should also
become thin and pure, more and more, and when every fire, the thicker and
grosser it is, the more it burns: contrariwise, the thinner, the less: that
which is most thin should burn least of all. By the same cause it comes to
pass, that a drop of Aqua vitae poured in the palm of a man's hand, the better
it is, the sooner it vanishes away and is consumed. I hear that this is a
laudable note of this water, if succinum amber being set on fire and put to it
into a spone do burn. When wine Vlstadius. 10* chapter.) is destilled twyse or
thrice, you shall cut a fine sponge into pieces, so great, that on every side
they may touch the cucur bita or body within the upper part (that is in the
inner circuit) the spo^g must be tied in three or four stringes hanging
without, when you shall set on the limbeck, so that the sponge can not fall
into the bottom of the still: let the sponge be dipped in oil olive, and
crushed out a little again, least pexauenture the oil should fall into the pot
or body of the y^ me^ of our time call it dead water: for it is unsavory and
stinking. Such, that is the stinking water, whether it be yeilden of lies
only, or also of wine, I have not tried.

For the destillation of aqua vitae or burning water, a man must choose the
best wine, black, red, or white. Black and old, yelds more plenty and also
better water, then any other. The fire at y^ first must be light and soft, and
after more vehement: but you must take heed in any wise yt the wine sethe
Albertus as some allege.

Of five parts of lies, when it is twice destilled I hear, one remains or
somewhat les. When y^ fleame is one clean consumed, nothing is lost after in
the destilling, but the hole is yielded again. Of.iiii. measures of wine, for
the most part one measure of burning water is gathered in the first
destillacion or there about according to the goodness for the most part we are
content with the third or the fourth destillacion, and call it perfect. And
surely it rests much in the instrument, that the water should be perfected
sooner or slower. For the vapor is more destilled and perfected in the first
destillacion in a serpentin (that is in a crooked and bowed pipe) the^ thrice
destilled by a straight pipe. Note further that there diverse waters may be
gathered in the first destillacion, whereof the first is perfect, that is, that
which burns and is consumed Lullus.

Of the strengthes and virtues of Aqua vitae, in the book of Arnold, De Villa
noua, which is written
ARnold in his book of Aqua vitae describes many qualities thereof both of it
alone, and also mixed with other medicines after the destillacion, observing the
order of the.xii. signs, from the head to the fete, for as he says, a man
must minister much more effectual remedies to the head, if a man have need, at
such time as the moon is in the ram: and likewise in the other, which thing
how true I judge it, I will not say at this time: notwithstanding this will
Arabians have brought into physic. Aqua vitae simple and alone (says
Arnold out of one Theoricus I can not tell whom) breaks an impostume or
recours of matter both within the body, if it be drunk, and without, as
botche, if it be anointed therewith. It helps read and duskish eyes. It
stauncheth the running and watering of the eyes. It is good for them that have
the falling sickns if they drink it. It cures the palsy iuila fallen down with
humors, also the salt fleme, the rose drop and the touth ache. It is merueylous
profitable for fre^tik me^ and such as be mela^choly. It erpelleth poyso^, The
smell thereof burnt, kills flies fishes from roating. It restores wine that is
turned or putrified. It draws forth the virtues of herbs and rotes, if they
be laid in it.iiii. days (otherwise.iiii. hours) except only the Violet. It
ought to be set up in a glasen or silver vessel and to be well stopped. There is
more of it and better made of old wine pure and read. This he. He that desires
more concerning the virtues of Aqua vitae, let him read that which we write
above of ye virtues of certain liquors, which our men call golden, Aqua vitae,
as to the other, specially to simple Aqua vitae, and much more to compound or
Aqua composita, but chieflye to the quintessens thereof. Read more over Lullus
in the book of waters. I have seen also a certain broken work ascrtbed to
Albertus, of the virtues of Aqua vitae. But practicioners, ignorant of things
and times, or else of a purpose to deceive me^, as many as they could, have most
impudently fathered Galen, Hippocrates and Aristotle of the virtues of Aqua
vitae, or burning water, as written by them. All kind of cold passions or
greues, that be curable, it helps in short space, specially diseases of the
brain, sinews and joints. Also worms in the bellye, biles and all scabbines,
if it be washed often therewith, it heals it. It helps ye spleen, it kills
all worms. It me^deth a stinking breath. It takes away the disease of the
loins. The hurt members if they be anointed health. It preserves fish and
flesh from corrupting, but before they be eaten, they must be washte with
common water. Camphora put into it, will dissolve. If common water be poured
into it, it goes to the bottom: and likewise oil. This writes Albertus as
some allege.

The taste of it excedeth all other tastes, and the smell all other smelles.
Lullus, It comfort the natural heat more then any other remeadye: it is most
holsom for the stomach, the heart and the liver: Lullus. Anoynte the head
therewith and it helps it of the head ache: it kills the worms drunken
fasting: it puts away away diverse kind of touth ache. It puts away the
blemishes and whit spots in the eyes, if it be poured into them: and auoydeth
the running and watering of the same, if a man do but hold it in his mouth. It
lets the leprosy a space and hides it It helps rotten and materinge
biles, if linen cloutes be dipped therein and laid upon them. With a little
decoction of parcely, it drives away the stones in the bladder. It makes
women apt to conceive, but anoyeth them that be great with Lullus.

I knew an old woman, that was cold and Cathectica, to be restored by a
handkerchief made warm with Aqua vitae set a fire within it.

Some allege the testimony of Constantine, of

Aqua vitae besides other things is commodious and profitable for the strangury
and other diseases of the bladder, for the tertian ague and quartain also which
are joined with cold humors: against the disease called the Wulfe: against
worms: against the sens or painful fealinge of a man's body like unto
biles. It furthereth and provoke wemens flowers. It breaks and puts
away the stone in the reins, and expels a of the bellye and fluxes, it
helps, what so ever they be. They say, that Aqua vitae is perfect when often
measures is made one, (which I suppose will come to pass in the third or
fourth destillacion). Annoint the head therewith, and it heals worms, the
scuruinesse and scabbes, pusculs and skailes. It puts away the spots in
the face and other where. Broken eggs or eggs that that lack a shell, put
them in Aqua vitae and it will sieth them. It healpeth the numnesse of a
Apoplexia, swellings, steames, tenesmum or desire to the stool and weariness.
It amendeth the morphew being drunken or anointed theruppon. It makes the
skin soft and pure. To conclude, it is good against the bitinge of a mad
dog. It will heal any wou^d excellentlye, if it be washte therewith, and so
that no evil effect shall come thereafter by the means of the same wound.

I perceiuinge Aqua vitae to be hot and to dry, apomel^, which with us
commonly they make of honey co^bs. etc. Some wet figs there in and set them on
fire, then eat them hot. A man may also mixed any sirup therewith, one or other,
according as ye disease shall require. To preserve wine y^ it be not troubled
nor putrified, put to it the tenth Aqua vitae. in deed sulphur is far better
for the same purpose, but it bewrays the craft and the smell there of. The
same will alum do, but being both hurtful, they are very noisome to the
health. Cardane.

If wine by the means of the savor of the vessels or taste of the grapes be
corrupt and soured, pour Aqua vitae into it and it will restore it. The same
purges ha^ging wine that is new: also wine that is putrified and soured also:
for it conuertes vinegar. To conclude it brings a good smell and Albertus as
some allege. When the wine is to be drawn into an empty vessel, most use to
put in a ball of brimston set a fire: other use Aqua vitae, dipping towe
therein.

Wine that is made to have any taste or smell, out of hand in a momente, is
both a curious thing for rich men cheiffy, which will aduaunt them of ye sorts
of wine: and also profitable. For the qualities Aqua vitae for the space
of.xxiiii. hours for so the strength of them shall be drawn out. Then ye waters
so affected and died, shall be mixed with a little wine when ye will drink.
Arnoldus de v^lla noua.

Of such things as be destilled dry, put into any liquor.
THe things that be dry can not be destilled, except they be stiept in some

hereafter amongst waters Aqua vitae rectified seems to agree best to the

Some still Cinnamon beaten with spring water poured to it, as we shall show
amongste Oils. Seeds also and other spices, wheroute oil is taken, are stiept

thick, and beaten things, the^ hole: the rotes then the gentian, astrantia, ye
flour deluce, y^ beries of juniper and other: I would more allow ye liquors of
drithinges set in wine or other where: but if ye things be thin or slendar, or
of no savor, or ought to coul and moiste^ they should be destilled rather new.
Sometimes the time of the year causes a man to take dryethinges because of
the lack or scarsity of fresh.

Gentian water. Take.iiii. pounds of the fresh and new rotes or rather dry cut
in small pieces, of great Gentian or white Gentian, (whose rotes are most fat,
and being set in wine, they nether corrupt them selves nor suffer the wine to

glasses diligently stopped, and put into them pure good wine, so much that the
wine be.ii. fingers above the^: and you shall ever put new wine unto them still,
cucurbitas, ye wine and the rotes distributed together, and destil them in

and gentian together destilled in wine, read within amongst composed waters.

Water of Roses with dry Roses is so made. Pour water to dry Roses, not more
the^ is sufficient to stiep them in: then put them from thence into glasen

The water of the nux vomica or spueinge nut, or the juice gotten out with

which by it self resists poison, and is good to provoke vomitting.

Waters destilled of new and fresh pla^ts (says Brunsvvick) ought to be
preferred, which if they can not be had, either for some other cause or because

apart by the^ selves) put them in a glass, and pour upon them that first thou
power on so much, that the herbs be sufficiently ouercouered with water: and
again twis so much. Then set it in horse dung two or three days, still it,
and putrify it again, so that it be thrys putrified and destilled by course
in order. This water surely shall be much better, then if a man, as Bulcasis
writes, to one pound of dry roses (or other herbs or flowers) shall put ten
pound of common water and destill it by and by. A certain cunning physician
writes that if water be aqua vitae. Hitherto Brunsvvick.

Of quint essence of remedies.
QVint essence they name to be, the chief and the heauenliest power or virtue
in any plant, me and purity of the hoale substance, not by any elimentall or
sensible quality (although it be not without qualities) conserueth the good
health of man's body, prolongeth a man's youth, differs age, and puts away
all manner of diseases. Of this first of al me^ writ Raimu^dus Lullus, although
it were unknown to all the physicians of his time, nether written of in any
book, nor tried or gone about in any use. After him follows besides other
Johannes de Rupe scissa, (whom one certain Lullus, as I writ before, I judge
they were both in one time) Hieronimus Brunsvvick, Philippus Vlstadius, and
perauenture a few other which writ sumwhat of the same. Sum kind thereof is
simple, as yt most famous quint essence of wine or Aqua vitae, of chelidony or
selandyn, of man's blood, of strawberys, of Antimonii. etc. Other ar co^pounde
that is when certain remedies ar put to sum quint essence now perfited, yt it
may draw out yt virtues of the^, whereunto

But here springs a doubt, says Cardane, whether a man may make ye water
te^perat, which they call quint essence: It will be, as I shall prove, of the
nature of the firmament, that is a most pure and thin substance moveable, and
which by the moving reteineth a temperat heat, and very much thereof. This
thefor is of power to co^serue al strengths, and topro long life. For being
most subtle, it mixith it self with the first moistur, pearcing the massy
things, and separateth And because it has much heat, it expels what soever
is unclean, and therefore restores the natural heat. For age is nothing else
then a lessening and diminishing of natural heat, which is therefore diminished,
because the mouinge is hindered: as I said of fire: for there is like reason of
this unto that. Moving is hindered, because of ye abundance of earthy matter,
because the earth only has very much matter, and is destitute of all moving.
Therefore that water being of so te^perate Aqua vitae reteininge his purity and
subtle matter: by the long mocyon of circulacio^, has put of and rid away his
heat and sharpnes of taste and smell, it is becomed of the nature of the
firmament: and then first of all it smells swiet and fragantly. For a
fragra^t smell is nothing else then a sharp smell when it is moderate. Aqua
vitae, if it come once to a temperatnes, the thinnes and purity remaining still,
it must needs be made fragra^t and swiet smelling: and if it be once fragrant,
it must necessarilye be turned into the nature of the fyrmament. There is also
a water made of the flesh tyrus or an adder, that restores youth: there is
made olso of Elleborus, which I saw at my fathers. But these vex and unquiet
the body, and firmamentall water does it in deed, which reteineth long that
which a man has and adourneth the same. But is the firmament more pure then
the fire which is most hot* It is surely so: for it is next unto the heaven,
and therefore most light. For by his circulacion it moderateth the heat caused
in it by the stars: and after ye same manner this water, brought to the
most puritye by the heat of the fire, by mocion it self is coled again and
obteineth a temper. Wherefore both Stoikes believe man's soul to be.) All this
write Cardane.

The virtue of every thing rests in the quintessens quintessens. Somthings it
is sufficient to destil the^ few days, if any part of the earth or lies remain
in the boto^ of the circulating vessel, to put it away pouring it forth. The
quintessens of wine will no more burn the mouth, nether is it burning wine any
more, but more subtle, and is called heaven: whereunto we say his stars is
added, when as herbs of diverse qualities, and such as be excellent against
diverse sicknesses, are sokte and stiept therein, as we shall show hereafter in
Aqua vitae compounded. To be solificate or made golden, is Lullus book of
quintessens.

The quintessens of any medicine has a thousand times the greater virtue,
then it had before, whiles the thing had yet the element in it. Vlsta.

How the quintessens of all things Lullus, intreatinge of quintessene.
THe quintessence of any thing may be drawn out from it, as of wode, fruits,
flowers, rotes, leaves, seeds, stones, metals, flesh, and of what spices a man
will, in this sort.

Any thing that you will separate the quintessence from, you shall put it into
the quintessence of shall set it forth to be solified and sonned in ye spring,
or else in a vessel to a light fire, and within three hours you shall have the
quintessence of ye thing mixed with the quintessence of the wine, which shall be
converted into such a nature as is of ye thing that is put in, whether it be
hot or cold, moist or dry, purge or what other condition or smell so ever it be
of: and it shall not only have the same operacion, but also in the same degree,
yt is nether more nor les. etc. After this he reherseth remedies one
quintessens. Rogerius Bacho also writ a little book of the qualities and
strengthes of Aqua vitae, throughout the.xii. signs according to the diseases
from the head to the fete, adding waters and medicines according to the nature
of every part and disease, for one, one, for an other, an other. This book
some ascribe to Arnold de villa noua.

Ihon Brasescus a man of our age most exercysed in the mysteries of the Art
of liquors, as his writings do testify, in a certain dialoge, the spekers
whereof be Raymund Lullus and Demogorgon, (which ye author set forth in
Italian, with an other also upon the exposicion of the books of Geber. Ihon
Petreius at Norinberg Prynted them both in Latin without the authors name)
affirminge that quintessens which serves for the conseruing and lengthening
of man's life, is not to be lokt for out of plants, beasts or precious
Raymund. Seeing it is necessary that this medicine should be utterly
incorruptible, and in this thing it ought to pass and excede al things that
have any part of the elements in them: it must needs be drawn out of such a
matter as is most far from all corrupcion, and least subject to the same.
Demogorgo^. Yet thou said in ye book which thou writst of the secrets of
nature, yt it should be drawn out of red wine, ye same confirms John de rupa
scissa, other suppose it should be made of Celedonie, other would take man's blud.
Ray. Thou art not a little deceived, if thou think ye writings of philosophers
ought to be taken and understood according to ye bare letter, chiefly in this
science, ye more clearly and plainly they speak, so much ye more darkness have
their writings, for they have spoken by similitudes and ridles in ye darkest
form of spech. Demo. In this thing what similitude have they used? Ray. Senior
the philosopher says this medicine and from taste to taste, and from nature to
nature: and therefore the names thereof are multiplied. Also Minois a
philosopher, if a man ask (says he) why is it made read before it received
whyt: Answer, that it was twys made black, twys turnid into orrenge color,
and received twys red color. Seeing now that it receives twys red color,
as red wine and man's blood, that is to say, after the putrefaction and in the
destillation: therefore the old men speakinge by quintessence now perfect,
which Io. de Rupescissa does: that is, that al men entering into the house may
be marnei lously allured unto it set in a corner of the house: and an other.
that all birds that perceine that swietnes of sauoure, may flock together
unto it set in the top of a toure: this is expounded allegorically, that by the
toure, the furnace set in a corner of the Rasis also was. Thus says he.

Of the dravvyng out of the quint essence from wine, out of Vlstadius and
Raymund Lullus.
THou shall take the best wine thou can get, what color soever it be, let it
be wyny rather then swiet, destill it.iiii. times in a lembeck, so as commonly
burning water is made. If so be it, it be ofter destilled, it shall be the
better. To know when it is sufficiently destilled, you shall consider, side
one, which also some call the vessel of Mercurius, by the hoal whereof in the
top, the matter that is to be circulated, may be put in and out. And when the
quint essence. Then the wine so destilled as we have said, when as it has all
the four elements, yet by the often moving agitacton and going up and down, it
passes and is turned from corruptible into almost incorruptible. If so be it
that by the often ascension and descention of destillation, the is separated,
how much more does it come to pas in a still where it is tossed up and down a
thousand thousand times. By this moving therefore, it is to be supposed, that an
elementall matier is turned in to a substance void of all elements and a body
incorruptible, so that this quint assence is of so much the further from the
corruption of a body that has part of the elements in it, as the heaven it
self is more incorruptible the^ the four elements. But when this circulation
is many times done, quint essence. For if it be perfitly done, there will issue
forth a most fragrant and pleasant smell, so excellent that they that feel it,
shall think them selue to be rapt from the earth into heaven, and to have the
fruicion of a certain heavenly sauoure. This vapor if it chance to insinuate
it self into some close place

If it be set in the top of a toure, it will allure all kind of birds unto
it, that be there about. But if that excellent grace of sauoure appear not
yet, then thou shall shytte it close again in to the Pelecane, and when it is
diligently clayed, fet it in again to the circulating destillation, until the
perfect quint essence it self appear, or else as Raymundus in his first book
the second chapter, calls it quick Mercury. And it has not only that most
excellent savor and taste, but also a certain incorruptible nature to be
occupied about other medicines.

And surely it has no brentnes in the mouth as aqua vitae, nether any moisture
or fleame, because all the earthy and elementall matter is settled down to the
bottom.

How qvint essence may be drawn


PVtt Horse dung A in great and deep vessel B or in a pit made for this
purpose: and in midst of the dunge set C full until two third parts of the
matter to be destilled, so that the third part remain without the dung
empty, that the matter may ascend and descend, and so to be turned into
clear water. But the dung must be renewed once at the least every wieke. The
same after they be pressed in the wine harvest: or else in the dog days with the
sun. It is possible also to draw quintessens out of troubled, unclean and
corrupt wine, if it be not sower or like Vinegar: For we see even of corrupt
wine, if it grew in a good place, although it be troubled and of an ill
taste, yet very good Aqua vitae may be destylled therout.

There is also an other way to draw it out without fire and labor. Take Aqua
vitae the best with a long neck, in the top whereof must be a hole, why the
ought to be closed and stopped with wax prepared accordingly (as Vlstadius
techeth after in the tenth chapter.) Then let it be turned vpside down, and
with the neck let it be put into the dung, so the grosser matter shall settle
and lie in the bottom of the that is, the very quintessente, stop the hoole
with thy finger and turn the glass upright. So have thou quintessence, but les
precious then the other before. In like manner may burninge water be put in a
glasen vessel, not turned, but buried vprighte in dunge for a certain space.
After by streinynge may you separate the groser matter which is settled to the
bottom, from the pure that is above: but this is yet les precyus.
Notwithstanding it draws out all the virtues of the herbs, and other
Vlstadius the.viii. chap.

Of the extracting and dravvyng forth of all the virtues of Chelidonia or
selandine: by the which example, every man of any understanding, may use to
draw out the virtues
BY the quint essence of selandin, Joan. Brasescus thinks sumwhat else to be
allegorically understood, as I declared before where we entreated of quint
essence generally.

Chelidonia, Sela^din (says Vlst.) has innumerable virtues: and the quint
essence thereof, which we will teach here to get out, goes cucurbitam or body
of a still of earth glased: when the body is full shit it close, and clay it
round about, then set it in now horse dung, for the space of iii. weeks. After
put it in a limbeck and destill it in Balneo Mariae, with a slow fire, and the
fleame shall avoid out of it. Then shall you draw out the dregs, and stone,
put the^ again in a cucurbita with a blind lymbeck, and let them stand in
Balneo Mariae a seuennight, or in horse dung mo days. Afterward the matter by
little and little being couled put on a nosed limbeck, and destill it in ashes,
according as in the.x. chap. of the separation of oil from the earth, we have
spoken: and there shall issue out a clear water containing in it self air
and water. Thou shall separate the water from the air in a new cucurbita by
Balneo Mariae, with an easy fire: Balneo Mariae seven days at the last thou
halt destill the^ in sand with a great fire, and the fleame shall issue out
first, then a radishe water, or rather an oil, which is the element of fire,
from the which thou shalt separate the Balneo Mariae, as is before said. But
the dregs that left, which conteyne nothing else but earth, must be urged
with a strong fire and brought unto lyme, by the space of ten days. (that is
in a furnace of calcination or reuerberation, or a very lyme kiln, as I have
taught in the tenth chap.) Then grynd them again upon a marble stoone and sooke
them in the fleame, and let them be destilled in a limbeck, until you see in
the matter little white stoones like salt. And out of which you have destilled
it, and after, you shall destill it again, and again so often, until the earth
chance and put away from it all unclean and earthy color, and be brought to
a very white (to the whytnes of wax) and so it shall be rectified earth. The
other elements also ought to be rectified, so that every one be destilled
seven times, pouring every time the fleam to the air and fire, and after
separating them as is before said. When as thou will do this an easier way,
dissolve every quintessence, but it must be presupposed that every element be
first justified. Then let it be put in a vessel of circulation in horse dung or
in Balneo Mariae. xxx. days, and then destill it again. So shall the very
body as a gros matter be changed into spirit or most subtle and pure
substance. quintessence. The matter is judged to be sufficiently circulated,
when the quintessence swimmeth above the other matter.

Of the virtues of every one of the liquors of Selandine. The element of the
water is good for all the diseases of the body, both hot and cold. It tempereth
all ve nom from the heart: it cures all the diseases that chance unto the
lunges. It purges the blood, and preserves a man from all corruption of the
natural strength and power. At once, it is good for all sick men in what
disease so ever they be.

The element of air, like unto oil, confirms and increases the strength and
beauty of young persons, if they use it sometimes with meat, for it lets the
blood from corrupting by any menes.

The eleme^t of fire, if so much as a wheat corn in quantity be mixed with the
best wine ye can get and poured into a sick man's mouth, yea if he be half
dead, it restores and refreshes all the stre^gthes of the body: for it perceth
unto the heart, and makes it warm: and expels all poisons and moist
superfluities from the heart. Lullus with the quint essence of wine mixeth a
little drop of this oil, to restore the^ that are about to die, and past al
hope, in that within the.xx. part of an hour.

Some draw out the quint essence from Selandine an other way and shorter. They
cut Selandine together with the rote and flour in small pieces they wey it, and
pouring well water upon it, they sethe it til it be brought to the same weighte.
Then they pun it in a stone mortar, and when the juice is streined out through
a linen clothe and purged it be ad consistenciam mell is as thick as honey.
After, they put it in a cucurbita so that it be half full, and by destillacion
in Balneo Mariae, they gather ye water or fieme. Then translating the vessel
into ashes, they receive the aerie oil, whereupon when they se an other kind of
oil swim above (the fire being encresed) they set under an other receiving
vessel, wherein the element of fire is gathered. Every one of these liquors must
be rectified, that it may be mete for the medicines of man's bodi, that Balneo
Mariae, so yt at every time the cucurbita be diligently washed, (made clean)
from the dregs which remain, which ought to be mixed with the element of
thearth, which remains in the bottom of the cucurbita after ye destillation
of ye fiery liquor. Likewise thou shall rectify their, destilling it in
ashes.vii. times, mixting the dregs with thearth. Afterward ye liquor of fire
likewise. The earthy matter, in such manner Selandine puts away al heats and
poisons out of y^ breast. It is good for the stoppinges of the liver and lunges:
for it consumes ye superfiuous humors and fleme: Ty conclude, it delivers a
man within the space of.ix. days, free from all infirmities. choler, no
bitter, nor fleme in ye body to get ye moisture. It encreseth blud, and
destributeth it into all the parts of the body by his pearcing. Wherefore they
that use this oil, do let blud the ofter. If a man be in ieoperdy ^f losing of
an eye, let him drop in a drop or.ii. thereof every day by the space of xxx.
dais, and it shall do him merueilus much good. The firye liquor is much more
effectuous then the watery or aerie, and helps where they fail. It conserueth
the youth, it makes age liuelye and elipir of life. Moreover ye earthy matter
rectified by dissolucio^s, coagulations and ielyings: calinatio^s and sub til
salt of ye earth, wherewith al metals may be turned into stone, and al spirits
may be fixed: having radicable and natural moisture. It norisheth lepre me^.
Of this ye ancient philosophers made a stone which they called ye philosophers
stone. The manner to receive y^ for said liquors within the bodi is Sela^dine,
iii. sponeful of rosewater, put to it a little sponeful of y^ sanguin air (y^ is
the liquor of the air) and give it to be drunken fasting, if the disease be
hot, with wine: and if the man be past.xxiiii. years of age give it him with Aqua
vite. In hot agues it ought not to be taken in no wise. All this writes
Vlstadius.

Of quint essence of man's blood, eggs, flesh, and honey.
How quint essence is gotten out of ma^s blood, eggs, and flesh, read
Vlstadi' the xiiii. chap. They put unto them the tenth part of salt, wherewith
they are well mingled, putrified and destilled and that four times, length they
are perfited by long circulation, until they come to the most swietnes of
sauoure, and pure fynnes of substance: Lullus also in his first book the.iiii.
chap. mencioneth of quint essence, but the printed books left out that, that
salt must be added unto it. It seems that salt may ryghtly be added to the
destilling of moist things, specially those that would easily corrupt,
such as chiefly the parts of beasts are.

Io. Bracescus, is of this opinion that the old writers would signify
allegorically some other thing, and that of metal, when they speak of man's
blood: as I recited before, where I write of quint essence generally. Vlstadius
in the.x. chap. of his teaches how quint essence of wine is made, every
element drawn out apart by him self: And this (says he) which is destilled in
the seventh time, is called man's blood, which the destillers, chieflye
search, and it is very air. This says he. In deed the liquor of the air,
which in the most part of destilled things is oylye, seems to be called
by the name of man's blood, for as much as our body consists of four humors
as elements, whereof the blud is compared Ihon Brasescus, man's blud is a certain
metally thing, so called of the color.

Of quint essence of metals.
IHon Brasescus in the dialog of Raimund and Demogorgon, when he had affyrmed
that quint essence which is profitable to the preseruacion and lengethening of
man's life, can not be had of any other thing the^ of metals only, he adds
at length. When as according to the opinion of the ancient philosophers,
every metal after their simi^itude, in every metal, as it is plainly
declared, in the book of the exposition of Gebrus books: therefore this our
medicin also although it be extracted and drawn out of some one metal only, yet
nevertheless it shall have the virtue of al metals and plants, and the virtue
over the hole body of man to heal many infirmities that be curable. Joan de
Rupescissa speking of our radical and natural moisture, and of quint essence
under the name of Aqua vitae, would signify the same, for he Brasescus, wherein
he expounds Gebrus.

Of the dravving out of quint essence from Antimonia, lead, white lead out of
Vlstadius.
QVint essence of antimonio is thus made. Incorporate and mingle the powder of
antimonii most finely beaten, with most sharp vinegar destilled, and let it
stand til the vinegar be turned into a very red color. Then drive the
vinegar out, and in a clean vessel put other destilled vinegar upon the
antimonium, and set over a little fire till the vinegar be colored* This shall
you repete so often, till the vinegar will be colored thou shall destill it in a
limbecke in ashes. First the vinegar it self will run out, after this thou
shall see a matter issue forth of a thousand couloures, and this is that
quint essence which is called of the Philosophers. Philosophers lead, and of
some virgins milk (it differs notwithstanding from it, whereof shall be
spoken here after, says Vlstadius) and it is almost like blessed oil in
color. Put this in a Pelican to be circulated for the space of fortye days.
It dries

Yea also quint essence is extracted and drawn out of lead or white lead
after the same manner as out of antimonio, pouringe destilled vinegar upon it,
that the vinegar be over it four fyngers deep: after let it be digested in
dunge, as it is said of quint essence of herbs and flowers. quint essence of
lead: and it has in it a certain swietnesse like* as the oil of antimonii.
It is good against all burnynges with fire and hot water, as also against
itches, as ringewormes and chollericke bladders.

But a man must noote, that the white lead ought first to be washed often
with water until none of the powder of the white lead remain in it. Then when
it is dried, reserve it to your use. So does Bulcasis and Joan of Sanct Amandus
upon the Andidotarie of Io. Mesuae, will to be done.

Of diverse kinds of Aqua composed.
Waters of life composed are proprelye called, when as certain medicines are
stieped in the very Aqua vitae and destilled together, improperly when as they
are put to after and not destilled together. There be also waters of virtue or
golden (as they call the^) when as the medicines stieptfirst not in Aqua vitae
but in wine, but of which we will write hereafter: those that we describe here:
saving that they are les vehement and les hot and dry.

Sum sorts of aqua vitae are commanded to be made simpely, putting the
medicines into y^ aqua vitae: other into aqua vitae thrys or four times
destilled. Sum there be also to whom a certain time of infusion or digestio^
is prescribed, as. xiiii days. etc. Sum are put in punned in the beginning*
other hoal, and broken after a certain days fermentacion and stieping. Sum are
destilled on Balneo Mariae: other in Balneo Mariae once ortwys and thirdly in
ashes, as that which Vlsta. describes xlvi. chap. although there is nothing
else done but infusio^ in wine. In the same author.xliiii. chap. Aqua vitae
is described to be destilled thrys, the first water whereof is called Blessed,
the second Aqua vitae, the third, the mother of balm. Again the destillatio^
of certain is repeted twys or thrys, that the water first destilled, be always
powered Vlstad. teaches in the.xlix. and.lvi. chap. Also sum are commanded to
be drawn out with a slow fire in Balneo Mariae. Other in the same with a
strong fire, as the the water described in Vlstadius. xlvi. chap. Many times in
the first destillacion, only fresh herbs, and fresh seeds are put with the
aqua vitae: and dry swiet smelling things, spyces and other, also honey is
added after to the water drawn out of the herbs or seeds, and then are
camphora put unto it, thirdly, folding the limbeck every foot with moist
clothes with the whytes of eggs and bran together. Read Vlstad. li. chap.
and.lvi. Concerning the matter, none almost is composed without spices: unto
diverse also swiet smelling herbs are added: unto some, both the flowers and
the siedes, sumtymes also other destilled waters: Also malmsey, honey, sugar,
figes. To conclude, unto some hoale pieces of gold: I suppose it to be added in
vain except it be que^shed Vlstadius described.xiiii. kinds of aqua vitae
composed, which we let pas because of brevity and shortness. We read in
Vlsta. the.liii. chap. of aqua vitae, with spices and honey, etc to be destilled
in Balneo Mariae, and an other likewise, but without honey, the.liiii. chap.

Vlstadius describes a certain marvelous aqua vitae in the.lvi. chap. First
he bids to digest diverse fresh and new medicines, in Balneo Mariae. xiii. aqua
vitae powered unto them: then to be destilled in ashes with a slow fire. After
he adds spyces and other dry things: and bids digest the^ xiiii. days: and
destill them again in Balneo Mariae. Afterward he added camphoram, rhabarb,
safron, amber and mosch. If so be it (says he) thou will have the water yet
better, put unto it certain precious and costly powders or spyces of
confections made of amber, made of mosen, made of precious stones. etc. and a
few ducates of the fynest digest it three days in warm water of Balneo
Mariae, afterward destill it by a filtrum or list of woollen cloth in crooked
glases well clayed, so that the one glass stand higher then the other. And this
(says he) is the most excellent Aqua vitae amongst all other.

An aqua vitae, which may be a remedy against the most part of diseases, put
rosemary, cinamo^, geloflowers, ginger, and maces, and two or three pieces of
gold, (which will be never a whit the aqua vitae, four times destilled. Use
this water.iiii. times destilled, morning and euenyng before slepee. It dryueth
away diverse kinds of diseases, and restores youth.

What aqua vitae can do, wherein rosemary is stieped, see hereafter where we

An aqua vitae or quint essence, whose effect is ready Matthaeolus Senensis, in
his sixth book of his commentaries upon Dioscorides. A pound of our antidotu^
now described, (the description whereof we let pas for briefnes sake: a man may
use good triacle in the place of it, or Mithridatu^, or an other effectual
antidotum) and a pound of syrup of the barks of citron, mixed them with five
pounds of aqua vitae so often destilled, till it cum to quint essence, and put
cucurbita of glass as big again as the things do occupy, (that is of.xiiii.
pounds,) and when it is well clayed move it moderatly and softly so long till
the antidotus be holly resolved and mixed with the liquor. So let it stand a
month, moving and chafing it in like manner twys a wieke. The month being
ended power out softly by little and little the clear water which is ascended
above the antidotus which is sattled in the bottom, into an other vessel of
glass, and reseruith very well closed. proved by innumerable tryales, that if
half an ounce thereof be dronken with wine, or with any convenient water
destilled, or else alone, it restores a man infected with the bytyng or
stinging of any beast althoug he have lost both his voice and his senses, and
calls him again to the great wondering of all mem. For the most part also
the humors now infected are avoided by vomit. The same virtue has it against
poisons which a man has reciued in meats or drinks. For ye strength
Mattheolus.

Aqua vitae devised by George Alapide. Take cinnamon cubebarum, ginger,

Charles used this, and lived. Cxxiiii. years.

What medicines be mixed with Aqua vitae, without any destillation, first
within the body, then without.
MAny times instruments, time or cost fails a man, that those medicines,
whose strength he would have in his aqua vitae (as though it were by a certain
metempsy chosin, y^ is a transposinge of the souls or principal virtues) he
can not mixed them with it by destilation, which only way is in those bodies
that are called met^ora, that is things bred on hy in the firmament, moreover
in many other things mixed either by nature or by art, is easy to be
understood. But for so much as in so diverse states of men, sum for one
hinderaunce, sum for an other, they can not always follow that which is best,
if quint essence can not be made, at the least the second or the third, or as
many as may be: let the destillacions be repeted with a slow fire. for any
mixture is done better by little aqua vitae, the medicines whose strength he
desires to mixed with it: yet at the least wise let them be broken and stiept a
while in it: for it draws unto it the virtues of all things that are put in
it. There is a book of Arnoldes de villa noua, or rather of Rogerius, which I
have written, where in is declared particularly, to what diseases and
sicknesses, what medicines ought to be put to sooke aqua vitae, for every part
of the body, which he does attribute to the twelve signs of ye zodiak.

It is well known in many ages hitherto, that gentian is the best preservative
against certain most grievous diseases and poisons: but sum use to power the
powder of the same with burning water, as much more effectual, into the
throotes of beasts, whom they know or think to be hurt with poison in
their meet or drink, or else by biting or stinging: and if gentian can not be
gotten, certain sicknesses of man, specially of the bulk or breast apomeli may
be put. for both certain men count it otherwise for dainty, to have apomeli
mixed with burning water, and also a toost of breed mixed therewith, many take for
a breekfast. A man may also against diverse sicknessess, give men to drink the
water of gentia^ artificially destilled with wyn, mixed after with apomeli or
swiet hypocras.

of wormwod,

In the disease called the French pockes, sometimes the jaws and throat are
eaten with evil destillacio^s, which onles a man find remeadye for,
oftentimes the gargil is wasted, oftentimes the corruption passes to the very
bones. Therefore a man must purge them, and turn them aside. etc. There is a
marvelous water made for Aqua vitae and vinegar in a limbeck a most clear
water issues out of many virtues, but chiefly it helps where the strength of
the medicine ought to be conducted so^what diep, if therefore thou dissolve in
it bole armoniak or sphragida, and touch the parts that be freten, thou shall
both kill the cause of that contagion, and heal all the freting, Fracastorius,
in his.iii. book of contagions.

Of destilled waters composed, but with other, then with Aqua vitae.
SVche waters as are destilled of two or mooe medicines mixte together, I call
them composed. Of this sort some are used for medicine, some for smellinge,
some are invented for garnishinge, trymmynge and deckynge. There be some that
will do two of these or all. Notwithstanding we will refer every one to one
kind, that is, wherein it excels most. Again, of them that be used in
medicine, some are received into the body, other some are minystred only
without other some both ways. We will make only two Chapters, for all
though some be used both ways, yet they are moore used other within and
without. Again, of them that be received into the body, some are moore
simple, Aqua vitae composed, where the spices are sooked in pure and only Aqua
vitae, we have written seuerallye by them selves. Hear will we put the other
(as I said,) and those which they call waters of caponum, and one with certain
medicines put into swines blood.

To be sooked in wine and after to be destylled, it seems to be ordained,
chieflye for those Melissa called Baulme, Wormewode. etc. which more over by
that means, do retain more firmlye their own sauoure, the wine drawing it
and drinking it up, that it can not dispersed for the thinness, we shall
speak hear of certain things to be stieped in wine, but while they be
new, for we have entreated of.iiii. dry things stieped in wine other liquor,
and so destylled.

Betany, commonly called betany, and in dutch eerenbrey, is stiept in wine a
few days, and likewise melissa. They shall have the same virtues and
strengthes, but more effectually and more subtle, then the herbs by them
selves alone.

Melissa, that is citraria (says Lullus) let it be put in wine to be
destilled. Let a man drink a sponefull of this wine fasting. It sharpneth the
understanding and the wit, increases the memory. To a man that stutteth* lay a
linen cloth wet in this wine upon his tongue and he shall speak right, It is
most profitable against scabbednes cumming of could. It sharpeneth ye sight of
him that drinks it. It takes away duskishnes, tears of and superfluous
humors of the eyes. It is wholesome for the breast, profitable for co^coction
against evil humors that lets it. Drunken with good wine, it stireth the
appetyt. It puts away spots and frakenes of the face that is washed therewith,
if so be it a little baulm be put to it and then the face washed tnerwith, it
makes also good color. It Lazarus from increasing. It is good also for them
whose brain is perished, and for the frantik. Lullus. Melissa beaten is
stieptin wine a night. Men say this water destilled and drunke^ every day and
holden in the mouth, it cures the benumming of the senses: all so the falling
sickness, the dropsy, the quartain ague, and other diverse diseases of black
choler or fleume. It is ministered also to the strangleng of the womb, and toth
ache, Remaclus. F.

A water of the les centory, worthy to be compa red unto gold.

The sage and penroyall of either like much, and castorium, as often as a man

Of waters of virtues or golden waters and certain other composed of many
medicines, destilled with wine.
WAters of virtues, which the Germans name golden, all are destilled with
spices and odoriferous herbs (specially sage, which seems to be a foundation
in these waters composed) put first a few days into wine: and they are in
more use to be ministered Balneo Mariae, in the sun, in chaf or chopped straw in
lime whereunto rain water must be sprinkled in a bottell.

Waters of virtue. Guatlerius Ryffius in his book of destillacions, describes
very many

And a man may in these also gather four kinds of waters differynge in
virtue, of the which I would most chiefly allow the myddle. For this is truth,
Aqua vitae comes out out more pure at the beginning of the destillacio^,
about the last end, it runs somewhat watery. Of spices and gummes, the parts

makes them to run out and at length heals them.vi. It cures the blemishes
or fautes of the eyes, as blerednes both it a while in his noos.xvi. It
helps the diseases of the teeth.xvii. It cures the maladies of ye heart and
breast when it is drier or moyster then it should be, or is sick with the
coughe or short fetching of breath. etc.xviii. It increases the memory, and
takes away forgetfulness in a man, that receives oftentimes by times, no, but
one drop thereof.xix. Scuruines, skailes, scabbednes, byles, wheales, and what
soever uncleanness else vexes the skin, or else the inward parts of the aqua
vitae composed, which are destilled of spyces and odoriferous herbs, etc*
stieped in burning water of the best, yea and almost unto simple aqua vitae.

water drunk of one fasting, is a true medicine of the falling sickness, what
cause so ever it cum of. And although a man have had that disease.v. years, he
shall be heeled, if he drink Lullius and Aegidius.

A water composed, the first amongste Aegidius waters.
HIssop, penny royal, Charyo. Cikory, of every one a dra^: let them be pu^d and
destilled. After take tutiae, persly of Alexan. Rue, Zedoaria, aloes, and the
stone called calaminaris, of every one a dram, when they are pund, sieth the^
in ye forsaith water till y^ third part be wasted, and the liquor streyned with
a cloth, thou shall kiep it in a glass diligently closed nine days
(otherwise.xl.) Afterward let it be given in drink every day in the morning
before day (other wise, by the space of.x. days) to the sick fasting. It is
proffitable against the falling sickness if he that takes it continue fasting
after it six hours. And truly it is a most effectual remedy. It heeleth al
resolucion of the sinews, and the members are Castorium, it is good against
all goutes, which has not taken rout yet in the members. If it be drunk ix.
days together fasting, it puttes away all manner of agues, what matter soever
it cum of (if it be drunk.ix. days every morning early. This water is also
most profitable to wash wou^des in which the sinues are cut.)

An other of Aegidius, the.ix. in number, otherwise they call it double. The
siede of Apii, the sied Apii, ginger (otherwise, The sied of Apii, white Popy,
sugar, Carioph.) of every one equal weght. Pund in a mortar, put to it

chiefest remedy for the cough and breast ill at eas. If a man drink it could
fasting, and in the evening as hoot as he can. If it be drunken hoot with
Castorio, it is good against the disease called Apoplexia, it heals also the
members sick of the Palsy, if so be the Palsy be not it chereth all the
members, it drives away il humors, and strengthens the heed and the brain.

within.iii. days. The same description find I in the book of Lullus of
waters.
