Of pur ging medicines, composed destilled.
THey also are to be called composed waters, that wine, burning water or other
liquor. Certain co^positions of spices to restore the strengtes of the heart and
the spirits, are mixed with waters of capons, dressed by destillations as I said
before: also with burning waters, or rather Quint essence of wine, against the
pestilence and poisons, as we declared before. But also purging medicines,
Electuaria chiefly in the which Dacry dium and other vehement things hurtful
to the stomach are received, mixed with the liquors, specially with burning
Lullius also prayseth greetly, and certain practicioners of any acquaintance
have used it with praise. I know Hamech, and that which is of ye juice of
Roses, and gave to drink unto the weeker sort the liquor that he received by
it self: to them that were stronger, he myxt sum of the elctuary with it: and
so he said he purged sick men without any grief.

With Helleborum is a water made, that restores youth: such one saw I my
father have. But such waters vex the bodies, and make a fallible image of
youth. Cardanus.

Gold Potable or that may be dro^ken.
OF potable gold, who so list he may read much in the book of Vlstadius which
he names the heaven of philosophers: and in the book of Lullus of quint
essence. That there is virtue in gold, which comes of it made hot and
quenched in water, that may be an argument, that the water wherein a wedge of
iron or gold is slekt, is commended of Nicander Aconitum: for it seems to be
understood of water, wherein these mettalles should be quenched, when as he
names none other liquor. Quench (says he) red hat iron or the dros of iron,
or red hot gold or silver dip it in a troubled potion or drink. Where the
expositer says: Quensh iron in water and drink it, and a little after: quench
the dros of iron in honey and drinck the intinction, so calling the liquor
wherein any any thing is quenched. Dioscorides bids to quench Auicenna also
has, and Aegineta, and also Aetius, who says that a miln stone so slekt is
holsom, and that the wine should be druncke hot) with these words: and the
dros of iron, or iron it self or gold, or silver red hot quenched in wine, if
yt liquor be dronk. And trueli it seems that wine is more apt to receive ye
virtue of gold, then water. When as I on a time tasted water, wherein gold was
often quenched, I could perceive no quality of the savor or the taste to be
altered in it. Again, it brought unto quint essence does draw more strength of
the gold the^ wine: and the more if the gold be beaten into most thin plaits,
and most of all if it be bete^ into powder. But the oil that coms of gold
shall pas al these. As for gold simplely sod, as in ye brothe of capo^s, there
is no strength in it all, except a ma^s opinion ca^do any thing, as I believe
withal learned men for the most part. Of the virtues of gold, read Auicenna in
his second book. 78. cha. But because ye purest is to be chose^ for medicines, I
will Plinies words out of his. 33. book a bout thend of the.iiii. chap. of ye
purging of gould. Let gold be roasted and broyled with thrice as much in weight
of the clots or lu^ps of salt: and again with.ii. procions of salt and one of
the stonecalled schiston: so it yeldeth his strength to the things burnt with
it in an earthen vessel, it self remaining pure and uncorrupted. I conjecture
yt Plini in this place did mistake schistu^ the stone, for schistu^ an alu^:
for in an other place (the. 35. book. 15. cha.) he writes schistum, yea and
the reason taken of the virtues, makes more for Alum: for he says gold is
purged with salt only and schistu^ put unto it: but Alum has more like effect
unto salt then the stone Schistos, whereunto the old writers ascribe none other
virtue, but yt which it has co^mun with the Haematit (of which kind it is) that
is to stop blood. But Alum is taken and used in the purging of metals: also
Aqua Forti (as they call it.) Notwithstanding Plini may be excused, because the
word stoone is more common and of larger signification with him for he names
both quick silver and many other metally things, stones, wherefore he might call
the Alum Schiston by the name of Schiston. Albeit he should not have so done,
for the difference of that which is proprely called a stone Schistus. In the
same place of Plini, after the words now rehearsed is put: The rest of the ashes
(that is to say, of the salt, with the which being burnt together, ye gold
Schistum) kept in an earthen pot, and tempred with water, anointed upon the
face, it heals the disease beginning in the chin called Lichenes, or like
foul breakings out: which shall be conveniently washed away with Bean meal. It
heals also Fistulaes, and they that be called Hemorods. If so be it when it
is beaten Spuma be put to it, it amendeth corrupt and stinking biles and sores.
Decocted with honey and Melanthio, and anointed M. Varro says it heals
wartes. Here in the first words, the ashes kept and anointed no man can
doubt, but he speaks of ashes: but that which follows of decoction and
annoynting, seem to be long to the gould it self. But in my indgemente, ought
to be red so that those words decocted and anointed be referred unto the
ashes, as both the consequency of the text, and also the manner of the medicines
do require. For salt is used of phisicio^s (Diascordes and other) against
Lichenes, ringworms itches, against al manner of vucums, red infla^macions, and
tetters: and broyled or parched with honey, against running cankers. etc. The
men of our country rub children warts with salt and soute. And alum, as Plini
says, asswageth rotten sores and biles, with fat: the frettinge cankers of
biles with vinegar or burnt with as much in weight of gals, with.ii. parts of
salt (in which manner of proportion also it is mixed for to purge gold) the
diseases that sprede abroad. Moreover by the name of Spuma which signifies
foam. Plini understands Halosachne^, as also the. 31. of ye. 7. I find
nothing noted by Hermolans or Gelenius upon this place. But as gold is purged
with alum, so is also Misy, as the same Plini witnesses. 34. 12. and by an other
menes, with quick silver, read Plini. 33. 6. That it may be purged, it is sod
with leed: the same. 33. 3. An other way to pourge the same, Cardanus
describes in his.vi. book of subtilty.

Potable gould. Take the honey and combs of a ambra^ grisea^, Sperma ceti.
Agallochu^, long peper, Cariophyllos nuce moschata, sau^ders and pure gold. Let
these stand in horse do^g. 30. dais. After destil the^ in a limbek in ye bath:
then grind yt matter yt is remaining upon a stone, and pour again upon it y^
water destilled, and destill it again in ashes: This water dissolueth gold. If
the potable gold be hardened, take of it ye bigness of a pees, and put it into an
egg hard roasted, ye yelk taken out: so shall it be resolved: give

The chymistes make a lyquor of massy gould, which drink, as they say
cheereth the body. George Agricola.

It lyketh wise men, that to eat meat dressed in vessels of gould, or with the
decoction whereof plaites of gould were adjoined: and to drink wine wherein
plaites or money of gold were quenched oftentimes, procures unto the heart good
state, and has great force. Arnold of conseruing youth.

In the Quint essence of wine, gould, silver, peer Philip. Vlstad. the.ix.
chapter, and furthermore: where as who so list shall reed more of potable gould.

Wine wherein plates of gould have bien quenshed.xl. or.l. times, is used of
certain in the steed of potable gould. Arnold de villa noua.

The same in his book of wyn: Wine that has ponderosity and weight thereof to
inclyne the superfluities to the parts of expulsion, and to conserve youth. It
conserueth the virtues of the principal parts in his actions, and by his
temperatnes it louseth the urine restreined. It heeleth the falling sickness and
them that have lost their senses, it is wholesome also for lepers. Many at this
time rich men and princes will have certain parcelles of gould to be sodde with
their dishes* other use the^ in Panellis with electuaries, other in pouders:
Diacameron the fyling hoth of gould and of silver is used. Sum are wont to hold
a piece of gold in their mouth and to swalowe the spetle. It is plain that
silver kept in a man's mouth quenshed the thirst: and corrall comfort the
stomach, both holden in the mouth and hanged about the neck, so that it hang
toward the stomach: for I have tried that it lets the troubling thereof.
Other convert gould into a water that may be drunk, which way without doubt
deed and natural, not to chymisticall gould.

Sum extol and praise highly the spirit or Quint essence of gould, for to

A Marvelous water to conserve ye sight, and against the blemish or spot of

A water for all the diseases of the eyes that be curable, out of Aegidius and

A water composed for the eyes. About the beginning of May gather Selandyn,
Veruin, Rewe, Fenell, pun them severally, and take.iii. ounces of the juice of
every one of them, then mixed them, put to a little of the grien branches (as
the French men call them the Pampes) of Roses.iii. ounces of sugar candy.iiii.
ounces of the best Tutia, and as much of Dragons blood. When all thies ar pund
thou shall mixed them together and destill them in alymbeck of glass. The liquor
that runs forth, thou shall let stand.ii. or.iii. days in a receiver, and
then use it. It is of great virtue for eyes that be ill at ease, red, or have
the web in the eye.

The water of the vyn together with honey sublimated by the fire, cures the
bleerednes of the eyes specially. The munkes in Mesuen. That is Rogerius.

Certain waters for the eyes out of Rogerius.
Fill a stilful of the leaves of Agrimony, Memitha, and Leuisticus cut: sprinkle
upon it a little white and clear wine, and destyll it in claied vessels. This
liquor represseth the swellinge of the eye lids of a cold cause: it dries up
the blearednes: it stops the flowinge of tears: it clears the sight:
breaks bleamishes or spots (I suppose he means corns or Pearls.) If
thou will have it stronger to break spots or perls, ad unto it Gallitricum,
and Morsum Gallinae (anagallis)

A man may get a water out of Fenell also for the same causes. For a liquor
gathered of ye rotes and leaves of Fenell sod in water, with a basen laid upon
the water while it yet sietheth, is kept in a phiall, and one drop is put in
the corner of ye eye every dai morning and evening for the forsaid causes, by
commun experience.

To break the spot or perle, mixed with the forsaid waters, myrhe, and Aloes
pund: and put a drop of the liquor streined in either corner of the eye

A water destilled of the flowers of white thorn and willow, puts away
prickinges, heates or rednes of the eyes: it stops tears coming of a hot
cause, and breaks the spots or pearls of the same cause.

A water of the leaves (flours) of Eufragia stops tears coming of a cold
cause, and makes slender the eye lids that swell of the same cause: it
breaks spots or pearls of the same cause, and Enphrag did not heat, but were
temperate, or else does coule moderatly in the first degree, and dries in the
second.

An excellent water for the debility of the sight, described by Gordonius. Take

eyes.

A water for the Cancar in what part of the body so ever it be. The herb

Of waters of svviet sauoure.

comes in ure to sauces of meats: and only it, as I think of all

The Powder of the Flower Deluce, mixte with hot water, makes it to smell,
and is used of Barbers. The Flowers of Lauendula or Lauender, and much rather
of that which is communly called Spick, both grien and dry are put into
water, or wyn, or burning water in a vessel well stopped and set in the sun, that
they may infect it with their smell. But if they be yet fresh and moist, they
turn the wyn almost into vynegar, which if they be dry they do not so. The
liquor shall be made the more smelling if the flowers be dried in the sun in a
glass closed, and afterward whyt wine be put to it. If so be it a man desyer to
have a swiet Caphura, Agallochu^, or meaner things as Assadubis, Styrap, and
Stacte, Myrh, or any other spyces, chiefly cloves: or else things of les
Arentii, Bay leaves, commun swiet herbs, Rosemary, Amaracus, Basil. etc. they
shall be made much the swieter. Saffron is to strong, and stuffeth the heed.

Waters destilled called Cosmeticall,
DEstylled waters for garnishinge, are divided also into certain differences,
for some are for the face, to make the color of it white, ruddye, bryghte, to
put away wrinckles, to preserve and keep it from Sun burning, or to abolish
the spots and and to the chaunginge of the color in them. Other make the
tieth white.

All the use of Cosmetical and garnishing things, ought not to be taken for
dishonest and indecent for a man that is well instituted and godly mineded, for
Galen also the most famous of all phisicions, prescribes cosmeticall
medicines not a few in his work of composicion according to places: and he
declares that the use of the^ is many times profitable and honest. For the
lewdness Galen of composi medic. sec. locos, the first book. 2. chapter

Such Cosmetical waters as be simple, we will not declare hear, as be the
waters of Bean flowers, Strawberies, Dew, Gotes milk: Read before and other in
Brunsvvick or Riffius. Yea Aqua vitae has a certain cosmeticall and garnishing
virtue, as we rehearsed in his place.

Waters destilled of the leaves of the peeche tree and Willowe, of lyk weight
mixed together, do hele the red whelkes in the face, (the French men call them
rubiz) being moistened therewith.

A spyced wine for the garnishing of women, which makes the skin whyt, fine,
pure, and well colored. Put Ginger and Cinnamon in wyn and Arnold in his book of
wine.

A garnishing water, wherewith women amend their thick gross skyn, black and
skaly or skuruy, with Quick silver sod in a raw eg, etc. thou may find in
Nicolas Massa upon the french disease, in his.vi. book.ii. chapter.

one an an ounce. strein it through a cors cloth, anoint the face and neck.
Epiphanius Empericus.

A water procuring unto the face a Rooselyke and fair color. Take a pynt of
Aqua vitae thrys Prasiliu^: Cloves to the number often, and as many grains of
Paradys: five Cubebas when they are all pund and sifted, heet them a little with
Aqua vitae in a vessel diligently covered, that nothing breeth out by any
means. Afterward when this mixtur is could again destill it in a le^beck of
glass, with a very slow fire, and thou shall have a good water and a clear. When
thou will use it, wiet the face and skyn of the person with a sponge moistened
therewith, for it makes without all this dying will continue a long time,
for.ii. or. iii years. If thou can not have Aqua vitae, take reed wine of
Rupella the best thou can find, about the measure of Semiloti, a dutche
wourd for half an ounce, for they call an ounce a loot, for there must be more
measure of wine, then Aqua vitae. But Aqua vitae is much better to the
preparing of this water. This water garnisheth a man's skin subtly and
maruelously. Out of the written book, the author whereof is not declared. A like
unto Gordonius. An other that takes away the wrinkles and spots of the face,
and clarifieth the skin, of whytes of eggs destilled: Reed before amongst
the simple waters destilled in Balneo Mariae.

A marvelous water that puts away Napas (little whelkes or pushes or little
teetes, sum call them Napas, I think the Italians) Lupinas acorns, kurnelles,
Porros, that is, wartes, and what euel so ever grows in any place of the body
it takes Arabic, clear Tur pintyn, of every one.iii. drams: When they are
beten mixed altogether and destil them in a lembeck. And in this water thus
destilled put half a pound of Cineris terrae and destil it again: and kiep this
water as a tresure. The author is nameles. But it seems that this liquor will be
rather an oyll then a water, and save only that ashes is added unto it, it
migth have bien well asscribed unto Balmes.

A washinge or Kings water which abolysheth all spots. Take water of clear
Turpintine, as much as can be gotten out of.ii. pounds of it: put therein

ounces, three Nutmegges, when all these are pund, myxt with the strayned Swines
grease: put unto these two ounces of quycke (la feuille ou de lauenre, as it is
written in French) of quick silver (de couppelle.) When all is mixte
together, destill them, and keep the water. Afterward take vi. ounces of the
water of the forsaid Turpentin, and of the latter water twice so much, mixed
them: and when you will use them wash the face first with a decoction of
Cerussa and wipe it.

Then pour about.vi. drops of this water mixed together into the palm of the
hand, and where thou will anoint, covering the place anointed or wet with a
linen cloth till it be dried. A marvelous effect and operacion shall follow
thereupon.

much anointed, which ye list to make reed. If so be it ye ad a little water
of salt Ammoniack destilled, the color should be the faster and abide the
longer. Yet take heed of this water (Ammoniack) because it fretteth every body,
and if ye list to use it, take but a very little lest it corrupt the medicine.

Whosoever requires mo liquors, specially destilled, for the purity of the face
and brigthnes, let him reed Rogerius in his.iiii. treatise the.v. cha. where he
describes the water of Beenes and Limons, whose use is either by it self or
with a certain composition. etc. Also the water composed with Bryonia and
Dragons: and simple, of the herb of strawberies, of hasta regia, of herba
Muscata, of the flowers of Nigellae, etc.

Waters for the dying of
SPonsa solis beeten (otherwise the siedes of Solsosium beeten) put it in milk
of a woman that nurceth a boy, ten (otherwise.xl.) days, and then make an oil.
This oyll sod with leued gold, seething it gentely by the space of one day, is
marvelous: for if a man it shall be plain and clear, that it shall seem
angellike continuinge for the space of.v. days. It clears the sight also,
and cures any disease of ye eyes within ten (otherwise. iiii) days: and al
kind of tothache within.iii. days: and if the jaws be well rubbed with it,
the worms fall out and die. Aegidius, amongst whose waters this is ye fourth.
If a man drinck of this water.ix. days, he is heled of the Palsy, what cause
so ever it come of, although it have endured the space of.iiii. years. Lullius
in his book of waters. It appears that this water is not made by destillacion
but by expression, that is, wringing out only, as I shall declare amongst oil
of seeds.

A water destilled of larde, that the hears may be made long and yellow and
shininge, and the face more elegant. Scrape larde as much as ye will and shave
it very small: then beat it in a marble mortar, til it be like paste knoden.
Of this destylled in a limbeck, thou shall gather a white liquor

A water destilled of honye makes the hears fair and long. Read here after
where we shall speak of simple quint essences: and amongste waters that be
destilled in Rosestilles.

A whitening water. etc. of a Mouldwarpe: the vi. amongste Aegidius waters.
Bring a Moule into powder with brimstone and the juice of Selandine put to it,
let it stand a certain days, afterward any beast what so ever it be and it
shall be made white. If thou mixte water, otherwise (the word water is left
out) aloes and wax, annoint the place diseased and thou shall heal al manner
of Gutta: and as they call it nolime tangere, if thou lay a plaster made
thereof upon the soore. Likewise it heals the skalles of the head anointed
therewith, and cures the guttam rosaceam laid upon it in manner of a
plaster. But mixed with the stone called Calaminaris and Aloes, it heals
Lupum perfitelye, laying a plaster of it thervpo^ twice a day, if also the
superfluities be washed with the same mixture. It must in no wise be taken
inwardly within the body.

A water that dies a Griene color. A pound of cuperoos (that is to say
Vitriolum.) Half a pound of Smerillum. Destil them and anoint Epiphanius
Empericus. The water of Capparorum, Capers destilled, makes grene hears.
Cardanus.

A water commodious for purging the tethe. Ammoniak, Salt Gemmae of every one
three ounces: Sugar Alum an ounce and a half. Let them be destilled, or
soked.viii. days in two pound of water, and strained, rub and wash the
tethe therewith. Epiphanius Empericus. Other.ii. like, for the same purpose
shall thou find after, next to the descripcion of Aqua fortis.

How waters of herbs, flowers, and rotes be destilled by descencion, that is
downwarde.

A way to destill svviete waters, and effectual, out of Flowers and
Herbs by descencion or goinge downwarde.
TAke an earthen vessel, upon the which strain or spread a thin and fine
linen cloth and upon it sprynckle Rooses, (for so the Roosecake will prove
marvelous swiet) or coueslops, or other Flowers or Herbs. Then must ye have
Cardanus.

Fresh Rooses laid upon a linen cloth strained upon a Basin, if they take a
vessel full of hoate coals, they destyll much water and swiet Syluius. In my
mind this kind of destillacion is commodious for all such things as be
cold, or ought to coule, chieflye if they lack smell, as the most part of
binding things: and more also, such as be cold and moist to. Yea also we shall
have much water and in shorter time, and with les cost by this way, nether is
there any ieoperdy that they should fume out. But a man must give diligente
head least the vessel laid upon be to little hot, least the water taste of
burning.

If.ii. vrinals be set together, the upper full of Roses and set in the sun
(with a linen clothe betwixt,) a most swiet water destilleth into the
nether. Syluius.

Roose water, Moschata, how it is made by the sun by descencion, we prescribed
before in ye Chapter of swiet waters.

The liquor of yelow violet flowers, that destilleth by it self into a vial of
glass, amendeth the eye Alexander Benedictus.

Take the tender buddes of Fennel before they flourish or go abroad, full of
juice with the leaves. Put these in a Phiall of glass, but fill it not up:
turn it up side down and put the mouth of it into

Scillae whiles they be fresh and new, the utter a vessel full of hooles in
the bottom, covered above and well closed with clay. The bottom of this pot is
put into another, put under it in a pit of the earth, and the joining of the
pots is compassed with clay cloos. Then make they a fire about the upper part of
the pot by the space of one night (.x. hours or more. So the water runs in
to the nether pot, which mixed with meel or breed, it kills mys quickly that
taste of it: the sooner if thou mixed a little Litharge or whyt Leed. Bulcasis in
his Syluius out of him.

Sumtimes certain waters and oils ar made by descencion, like as of Roseny
tries when they are burnt pitche. Syluius. But of Oils which are made by
descention we will speak hereafter. Certain are made by a middle way between a
discention and ascension, as Oyll of Coperos, by a Cucurbita laid along
overthwart upon the side.

Destillation in Ashes, or Sand
IN Ashes are both waters and oils destilled. Waters with a most gentle and
soft heet, which thou shall discern by touching with thy hand both the ashes
and also the vessels of destillacion. And perauenture there is no difference,
for the destillacion of any kind of waters, in Balneo Mariae or in Ashes, if
so be it thou observe the measure of ye fire. For Balneo Mariae, the water that
ye stillatory is put in, may be skalding hoot: the ashes may not be to hoot,
that is when herbs, roots, flowers or any liquors ar destilled to derive a
water of the^. Men think them to be made so much the swieter and les smelling
of any fyrines and brenning, being destilled in such ashes because the thing
is done more softly and with more leysur, so that the heed or lid be not hoot,
and sumtymes between the falling of the drops, a man may tell swiftly til he cum
at fifty. But the Oils have nied of a gretter fire Quintessence.

The destillacion by ashes is easy and ready, if an earthen or brazen vessel,
diep enough, and able to receive the Cucurbita or body of the still, be filled
with fine ashes and sifted or fined sand, so full of every side is sufficient
to be one foot long, the height six fingars or there about. All thies shall
we strengthen every where with clay (and pieces of shelles mixed together) that
the heet may the better be kept in.ii. breething hooles left in the corners.
But this manner is for upon a soden and only miet for one vessel. I bielded once
a more laborious furnace on this sort. In a corner of the house I raised a
foundation of brick and lyme, a foot and a halfhy. Upon the foundacio^ or
harth,

This round place above the plait was filled with ashes, in ye ashes round about
was there set about five stilles of glass, that at one time and with one fire
many waters or Oils might be destilled together. Round about the furnace was
well fensed with wrought claye with very salt water, wherewith sum Horse
dounge was myxt.

This kind of furnace is surely very commodious, because it niedeth little fire,
which also it self in this form endures very long: that it is almost
enough to see to the fire morning and evening. But it must be made only of
cooles, which are put in to the furnace with a little fire shouell, only that
they may be gotten in at the little door hansumly: the edges of this fire shouel
must be a little turned up, that it may hold the cooles ye better. There is nied
also of long iron fyershouel (as the furnace shall require) turned in at the end
according to a streigth corner, and there in the end to be sumwhat made brother,
wherewith the cooles within may be Gentian, Juniper beries, and Aqua vitae is
sumtimes repeted, in which I have observed this, that the better part always
and the purest issues out first, and what so ever is flegmatyck (or deed
water, as sum call it, which is unprofitable, hurtful, and almost stinking)
follows last: which is well perceived and known in a glass heed for the
still: For while the purer matter, that is, the fyry and aerie substance
issues, no fleume comes once, and tokens of the water following by straikes
appear manifestly, as ye see them, remove the receiving vessel, and what so
ever remains in the Cucurbita or belly of the still, pour it out as good for
nothing: and begin a new destillation of fresh and that must be repeted, until
no fleume appear any more, which chaunseth lightly in the fourth or fifthe
destillation. Afterward there needs no more destillation, but circulacion,
that the liquor be rectified, as they term quint essence.

Things destilled in the vapor of hot water, retein more perfitly the virtues
and qualities of of plants, but because they have some coruption in them, they
can not be kept long. Those things longar which by the heat of ashes or dros
of metal beaten to powder are destilled in vessels of stone or glass, how many
so ever they be, upon a long or round furnace covered with slates or tiles
which may hold the hie sands compassed with Syluius.

Vlstadius prescribes a certain destillation in ashes with so soft a fire,
that thou maiste number one.ii. iii. between one drop and an other.

Any kind of fruit thou will, when it is well ripe cut small and beaten,
thou shall destill it in sand with a slow fire. Vlstadius.

A certain way to make water by and by.

WAter is made also vppn a sodein, whiles the juice is made hot or skalding, a
glasen cup is set upon it, in the which the fume passes into drops: and the
drops gathered thereupon are driven together into water. By this means
Vinegar is easilye turned into water. This is wholesome for spots and
blemishes in the eyes, and for the web, to put them away: specially if Rue be
sod in white Vinegar. Cardanus.

Some are put resinam larigenam in a trene vessel, whose bottom must be made
by a tornar as thin as is possible, so that a man may see through it, so in a
hot place the purer part and the thinner destilleth.

Of rosaries, that is to say instruments wherewith a destilled liquor may be
gotten out of Roses and other medicines, fire being put under (for the most
part without any
FIre in destillacion does more procure the sauoure of adustion and brentnesse
unto the water that is to be destilled, then do cooles or water, which thing
to avoid put sand in the pan in commun Rosaries: or rather see that sand be
betwixt the fire and the pan, and also about the pan, and that pan: for
it is made also of brass. The joining together of the pan and the lembecke
thou shall fense with clay, that is to say with a lynnen clout perieted or
closed with clay and hansumlye laid about it: and the receiuinge vessel with
the nose of the lembecke likewise. Brunsvvick. Some now a days make these
Rosarie pans of bras, and upon the sand they lay the herbs that be to be
destilled: and round about the pan (and within it also except I be deceived)
they fill a void space Balneo Mariae, or in ashes.

Vinegar is made white with destillation, as well for other uses of Chymistes
and Phisycions, as also to be mixte with Sirrupe of Vinnegar, Bulcasis words.
Build an Atanor like unto that wherein Roosewater is destylled, and ever take
a stillatorye vessel of glass or of earth glased: fill three parts of it
with good Vinegar, that the fourth part of the Vessel above may be emptye,
least when it doothe seethe the Vinegar be spilte and run over. Then cover
the vessel with some Vessel upon it, as ye know, having a noose, as the
manner is in rosewater, and make a the Vinegar shall not become so white. The
vinegar that is destilled must be of white wine, and very sharp. So shall
thou gather a white and pure liquor, which thou shall keep in store. After y^
same manner may wine be destilled. This writes Bulcasis. I destilled it once in
glasen vessels in ashes, which I keep yet hitherto certain years, like to
Vinegar not destilled both in sauoure and taste, differing only in the color
and substance, where in it is like to pure water to see Bulcasis seems to be
of this opinion, that vinegar should be destilled with fire or water, not in a
Balneo Mariae, but in that manner that he makes the first and second in
destillinge of Rooses, that is, without water with fire of wood or rather of
cooles. I have destilled very excellentlye in ashes. Pearls, eg shells, stone
of the rains and bladder are dissolved with strong vinegar, specially when it
is destilled or with the juice of limo^s: so is also both kind of corals, and
they afterward praecipitatum and sublimatum and cinabrium, and they return
into quick silver. Syluius. The Chymistes say metals are resolved with vinegar
destilled: also with man's urine destilled.

The leaves of hemp turned into meal, because they pearse the head, make
drink which will make a man pleasauntlye drounke. Cardan. It seems water
should be poured to it, and when they are made dowe together, then to be
Rosestil, or like instrument where the fire is put under without any men, and
perauentur the destillacion should be twys or thrys repeted. I would say the
same of Ootes water, whereof likewise Cardanus writes: The Moscouites (says
he) because they lack wyn, use water destilled of Ootes which enflameth and
makes a man drunken no les then wine. For Ootes being of a more gros
substance, it must niedes when it is destilled make hoot, becum more subtle
and sharp, and draweny

The like may be done of any drinck that makes drunken, as Ale, Bier, Curmus
and Meed, specially when it is old, and that which is sodde with Mill.

Honey while it is a destilling is wont to rise up and run over, when it is made
hoot. But this is avoided, when a man destilleth in a co^mun Rosestill, by
putting upon it within the pan a wooden sieve (made with horse hear) so that it
touch the honey. Cucurbita or body of glass, mixed with it pure and well washed
sand, and make a slow fire. The first water is cast away, but the second is
kept which has a goulden color, and a little before the end reed. The use of
this water is for women to their hear, that they may grow, be soft and yelow
being moystened therewith specially in the sun. It amendeth the shedding of the
hear. It heeleth swelled and blered eyes, and puts away their watery cootes
and their duskishnes: and full of wheales. It heeleth notably the places that be
burned, specially them that be soft and ten der, so that no skar or blemysh
shall remain. The latter water that is redish, purges corrupt mattier in roten
byles, if they be washed therewith, and lynnen clothes moystened therein be
laid upon them, and when it has purged them, it rayseth the flesh also.
Ryffius. Reed Vlstadins..

Of oils destilled, and first generally then particularly.
Of oils of Plants, Flowers, Herbs, Gums, Rolines, Siedes, Barks, Woods.

Of oils of Beasts.
Of oils of Metalles.
Oils which are made in Chymisticall vessels either by descencion or by
ascencion, sum be simple, sum compound: of thies sum are composed of many, as
certain

Also certain are made of pla^tes or their parts, Flowers, Routes, Siedes,
Barks, Rosines, Gums, Drops. Sum of beestes, or of sum parts things or such
like as Antimonium, Leed, Am ber. We will speak of every of them by thies three
latter places. Of other kinds of oils which are made by any other manner, as
