Like as oil Benedict is destilled by sublimacio^ of hoot burning till stones
quenshed in oyll, even so of Ladanu^, wax, liquors, gums, rosins, boones,
marowes, and other abou^ding with fat humors may oil be gotten by sublimating.
Syluius.

An oily nature seems to be in all mixed things or co^pounded by nature
whatsoever they be, wha^ as it is in salt also the most dry body that may be,
as Cardanus writes. (There is also in salt a fatnes, which we may maruaill at,
Plin.) but in sum more, in sum les. For it is an oily and fat humor that is in
mixed things, not that watery and ea^y to be dried, but an aerie which also is
hoot. Boeth kind of elements have the moisture in the^ that belongs to
their kind. This liquor in certain Griekes call them opus: teeres, gumes,
rosins, Elaeomel: but also in deed bodies, as in fat metally things, brimston,
quick silver, also in diverse kinds of pitch, Naphtha, aumber, ambra, and
P^^r^l^on, which has his name because it flows and runnethout of stones.
But most manifestly in beests, both by other parts of the^, and also chiely ye
fat marow, blud and in the^ yt have no blud, sum fat humor of ye nature of blud.
in the massy parts both of beestes and plants, in all such chiefly siemeth
it to be which have thriedes or veins manifestly, by the which they draw
nourisheme^t. For unto nourishment swiet things are most of all ordained,
which by a moderat heet are turned easily into a fatnes, ye y^ very fat
things them self are sumwhat swiet. And the abundance of fat juice is tried
and found most in those things which are nourished with many swiet things.
Notwithstanding fat things Gnaiacum because it sinketh in water, abounds
therewith. Thinner and lyghter woods convert and turn the fat juice in to Gum
or Rosyn, wherefore they have les plenty thereof: such as be Gnaiacum, the
walnut try, the Ash.

Moreover the very ashes show that Oyll is in all woods: for the ashes of
every one have their fatnes, which it leeueth in lie. So durable is the aerie
fatnes and yet more the fyery, as when earth is burned, the water goes out in
vapors, the other remain, part in the ashes, part in the Sout: although in
both those also sum substance of earth remains. But thies things perteine to
Philosophers.

How oil must be drawn out of spices, as Cloves, Nutmegs, Saffron, Mace,
and other.
MOysting mesurably in Aqua vitae rectified and circulated, any kind of spice
what ye will beaten and broken somewhat gross, and when they are stiept
together, at the last destil them with a very slow fire. And when as the Aqua
vitae once drawn out clean, the oil begins to still, the^ cucutbita and
put it in a little bag well knit and tied with a thread: and in a pres for ye
purpose pres it out, both ye iron plates of the pres well made hot first. The
oil pressed out so must ye destil, rectify and circulate: that the pure oil
may be separated from the grosser matter. The dregs afterward may be digested
or putrified again with the Aqua vitae first separated from them (and be
destilled again.) And this waisemeth to be the most commodious and most
Ryffius: but we have translated it as well as we might, somewhat darker then
it should be.

How oil is drawn out of vvodes and other like, as Cloves by destillacion
out of Cardan.
THe things beaten are put in a vessel of glass A. and are destilled with as
easy a fire as may water put round about the Vessel, it shall be much the
better. A head of glass. B, is set upon the top of the vessel, and is
cloosed with claye least it brethe out, whereunto a pipe. C. is adjoined as
the fashion is: to yt an other pipe of glass D. is joined, so compassed with
claye that nothing can breethe out. This pipe passes through a trene
Vessel. E. which ye must have alwais full of very cold water, drawinge out by
the spigotte so much water as has gotten any heat and putting in other water
again late and new drawn D. is made coule, and the fumes are not burnt, but
pass into water or into oil, and being turned by that crooked part of the
pipe which stretches beyond the vessel with water, fall droppinge into the
vessel.

Therefore as I did once see it, at the first a troubled matter runs out,
caryinge forth the more subtle substance of the thing: after that a more
clear water comes out, at the last oil, which declares the sauoure
rather of a thing burnt, then of that thing wheroute it ran. In this manner
the destillacion of waters also (of herbs and flowers) may be done: but that
way is far better which is by descencion downwarde etc. Some put Musk about.
F. and other precious smellinge things, and the water that rettineth the
smelles not only of the own proper matter, but also of the Musk and of other
things that be put to it.

Of destillation by descencion or going downewarde. Vlstadius in his.xix.
Chapter teaches of this way of destillinge universally: and specially or
seuerallye of oils of Juniper sticks, of the yelkes of Eggs, Nutmegges, and
Benedicto: where through I will go about to declare the matter moore
clearelye and more shortly. He teaches to make a furnace meet for this
destillacion, like to such a Chunney as is used to be made amongste the
Germans, of brickes vnbrente of a Cubit and a halfhie, on every

This furnace shall be meet also for an other use, that is to seal with the
seal of Hermes, as they call it: when the mouth of a Glasen Cucurbita being
put into the hoole of the furnace, the length a hand breadth, that is.iiii.
fingers, and if any more about it be open, that being stopped with claye, as soon
as it is red hot, it is nipte together with a pair of tongues likewise red
hot. When thou will destyll therefore in this furnace, take a Cucurbita of
the best earth: or if not, of Coper or Latin: syll it unto the third part
with the matter that is to be destilled, and shitte it with a very thin iron
lidde of iron plate full of little Cucurbita of glass (for the puttinge in and
taking out whereof, and that thou may see when the hoole matter is
destilled, the foore part of the furnace must Cucurbita must be joined, (but
not with clay.) Then let a fire be made above about the vessel that co^teineth
the matter on every side. But the fire at the first must be little, and as
far of from the vessel as is possible: afterward by little and little to
make it bigger and nearer the vessel.

Ye must understand that of every matter, at the first water destilleth,
moore or less according to the difference of the matter.

When it has left running, and now the oyll has begun to destill, thou shall
empty the Cucurbita of glass that stands underneath, and set it under aagain:
and continue in encreesing the fire, and to make it ever nerer and nerer the
vessel, until no more oil destille. For then by and by the fire must be
removed far of that the upper Cucurbita may be couled. Then take away the
nether that is the receiver, and keep the oyll. But as I said, a little fire is
required at the first, and is to be encreesed Cucurbita, burn it, breeke it, or
infect the oyll with sum vnplesaunt Benedict, thou shall increase the fire only
at the end of the destillacion: and thou may prepare such a place for the
destillacion: Dig a pit in the earth, long, brood and diep, accordining to the
measure of the pot that is to be put into it. Let the pot be glased, washed
with water, and upon the mouth of it let a thin iron plate be put, bored
through with many holes, so that the plate be a fingar thicknes within the brim
of the pot: upon this set an other the mouths of them be joined together, and
all well clayed, then let a fire be made about the upper pot, which shall be
hool without the pit save ye mouth. But this second way is not so commodious as
the first: because the water can not be separated from the oil: nor easily
known when the hooll matter is destilled, except it be known by use and
custom. The furnace may also be made in a stiep hill: as the same Vlstadius
shows: I let that pas that I be not to long.

Oil of the leeues of Citrum, or Orange, Arantia, or Limons when it is
destilled, first the water is gotten out (then the oyll) and both ought to be
kept severally: the oyll is very swiet and good. It is made on this wise. Take
the leeues of any of the foresaid tries which so ever ye will, when they are cut
beet them in a mortar, and as ye destill them receive the water: and when ye
see any drop of diverse color swym upon the water, the receyuer being changed,
encrees the fire and gather Ambra, hoot Styrax, Ladanum, Cinnamon, Benzoum: not
the substance of them, but the fume or smoke only: for they put thies into
Rose water upon the fire, and with the vapor of them they season the leaves.
When thies are so diligently certain spices ar stiept, as Cloves, Galangal, Put
megges. etc. for the space of one day, and the powder of the leaves moistened
therewith, they destill it it ashes with a slow fire, till the watery liquor be
separated: then they encrees the fire and receive the oyll, which is of a
wonderful pleasant and swiet savor. Furner.

A certain practicioner showed me, that the oyll is separated co^modiously from
herbs and routes in this manner. Put the herbs or routes in Turpentyn Larigna,
rosin washed (eigth or nine times) till it wax white, and let it stand in hoote
sand q. s. Then destill them with a slow fire, and the Turpintyn shall run out
first* by and by at the beginning: or if it move but a little, being shaken
together it shall then run out hoolly, first in a color white as milk,
afterward yelowe. Thou shall perceive by the taste when the liquor that thou
desire begins to run out: when the color is changed, thou shall change
also the receiver, that

Such destilled oils as be to be ministered with in the body, al must be mixed
with sum liquor, wyn Meed, or Syrup with sum destilled water, or other
medicine: As we shall declare also hereafter among the balsameles. And this is
it oils as unto spirits or souls, sum body ought to be added.

Of oils of flovvers.
OYll of Saffron is prepared thus: Thy matter is digested by sprinckling Aqua
vitae upon it, and when the Aqua vitae is drawn out by destillacion, the other
liquor is wrong out with a pres, as it is written Ryffius.

Oil of Spick, out of the French book of Furnerius. Set the herb (the flowers
rather) of Spik or Lauendar a while in the sun, then draw out the water in a
le^beck. This set in the sun in summer in a very hoote place brings forth an
oil in the superficial or upper part of it, which being separated now and
then from the water, thou shall reserve. For it both smells most swietly,
and is

An other way of the same man's. Thou shall stiep in a glass the flowers of Spick
well rypened in as much as thou think good of Oyll of swiet Almondes, in
horse dong.xl. days. Then destill them with a slow fire: at last rectify them
in the sun, the vessel diligently stopped. But if there be any smell of adustion
or brentnes, make.ii. or.iii. little hooles in the cover. So shall it remain
purged

The third way of the same man's. Fill a glass with the flowers of Spike
well ripened, shit it, and every day put as much to it as ye can. Do this
continually for the space of.viii. days: then stop the vessel and putrifye it
in horse donge iii. months. At the length destill it a lembek with great
diligence, and when the oil is drawn out set it in the sun, so that thou
seperat always that is clearer and reserve it Some as soon as the matter is
drawn forth from the fire (so is it in Furnerius.

The same oil Ryffius says is made, as oil of Roosemary, the flowers being
cut and stiept in old wine, then destilled, so that the spirits of the
lembecke be couled. etc. Read before in the oil of Roosemary. Some sell this
oil of Spike commonly and name it Balm, with tables wherein the virtues of it
are described. It is very hot and dry. I knew a woman which received into her
ieopardy: but therewith she avoided many worms. It is mixte with many
things, chieflye for the smelles sake. One drop of it chafed with a great
quantitye of water, makes it all swiete smellinge. Phisicians also mixte it
with Oils and hot oyntmentes, both because of the sauoure, that it may
increase the pleasantness of things that be somewhat swiet of them selves,
and to conserve them, or else to hide and cloak an evil smell: for it
ouercometh all smells, and perauenture

Oil of Roosemarye that is in stied of Balm. Take a Phiall full of the
flowers of Roosemary: burye it in sand (shitte with a double cerecloth, or
with wax and a cover so that it may brethe, Atnold: with a double linen, and
then also with wax to be closed, Hollerius:) til the middle of the vessel:
and let it stand so a month or more, until it heateth them for the most
part, and heals them sometimes. It resists salsfleem. Fistulaes and
Cancars that give not place to other medicines, it heals them throughlye.
Aqua vitae destilled of wine, wherein Rosemary is decocted and sod, does the
same things. Lullius. I would not sethe the Roosemary: for the strength of the
wine and quintessence does fume out in vapors: but I would stepe them in a
vessel closed, or putrify them: then first in Balneo Mariae, afterward in
Arnold de villa noua, which in his book of wine, (where he writes of the
oil also of Rosemarye the very same things, which we rehearsed now out of
Lullius, if so be they be Lullius words.) Of tentimes (says he) have I tried
that Aqua vitae, made of wine wherein Rosmary has been mollified, cures
salsfleum, scabs, cankar, and the fistula, which cannot be healed otherwise.
But this Verbascum not by destillatio^.

Oil of Rosemarye should be made of flowers cropped of with the tops of the
tender buddes or bows wherein they are sprung, pund and stiepte or putrified in
very good old wine. They ought to be destilled with a slow fire, so y^ the
spirits of the lembek be now and the^ couled, afterward to be circulated, till
yt all the gros matter be separated from the subtle as much as is possible:
Gualterus Ryffius. It may be destilled, as I conjecture, either in a Cucurbita,
specially somewhat short, with a limbeck: or in a crooked still with a
receiver of a diverse figure and fashion, or rather in two crooked stils, whereof
the one may be the receiver, or else in brazen vessels which the Apothecaries
call bladdars as water of Cinnamon, or burninge water.

In like manner be oils destilled, as the same man teaches of Vernix or Gum
of Juniper: of the flowers of Spike or Lauender, of Anes seeds and many other
seeds.

The.iiii. essence or fire, of Selandin, which is like a liquor of oil. etc.
how it is prepared and to what use, read before, where we have entreated of
quint essence. It is possible also to draw out.ii. manner of oils, an aerie and
afyerye out of the most herbs and other medicines, after the same manner it
is prescribed there in Selandine.

Oil of Rue. Cardanus in his second book of subtlety, writes that there be
certain poysones which slay with the only touching. Against these Lullius:
but that whereof Cardanus makes Rue it self, that is of the extreme tops of
the bows thereof which florishe.

Of oils of siedes and fruits,
OYll of Cloves did I taste once in Italy wonderful swiet and strong: how it
should be drawn out, we declared before with Catdanus words, where we write
generally of drawing out of oils: also another Ryffius (that is) the same way
yt ye may out of what spyce a man will, which stiept in burning water, they
destill the^ till the burning water be separated, and as soon as the oil
begins to run, the matter taken out of the Cucurbita is pressed. etc. as
before it is written out of Ryffius, who describes also particularly the
virtues of this Oyll.

Oil of Nutmegges how it may be gotten out, fee before where as we entreated
general of the Ryffius, who describes the virtues of either of them severally.

Oyll of Anis sied is drawn out (as out of other dry things which be in the
kind of plants) the siedes well beeten stieped in the best wine, then
destilled by little and little by ascencion, so that the spirits in the Lembeck
be continually refrigerated The virtues of it Ryffius rekoneth, we leave them
out as also of other for the most part, where no peculiar effect or working is
attributed to the waters and oils, yea the same ar agreeable to this medicines
also wherout they be drawn, or ever they be destilled, saving that by
destilling they are made more subtle and more effectual or stronger operacion.

Oils of the beries of Juniper and of the beries of Bays, are made one way:
that is pund, Cardan, to be gotten out of woods and cloves: or else as out of
spyces, by Ryffius description. I think it makes no great matter, so be that
the spirits which be carried about the lembeck, may be couled commodiously in
it and in the noos.

The virtues of thies oils Ryffius rekoneth Agyrtae or iugleres also ar wont
with us to sell oil of Iunipers, with tables imprinted co^teining the rehearsal
of the virtues thereof. This emongst other heeleth them that be sick of a
tercian as I am infourmed.

Sum make oyll of wheet pressing it out between two iron plates reed hoot:
other think it to be nothing worth thatis made thus, and that it should be
made as oyll of Juniper, that is of the sticks of Juniper by descencion or
dounwarde. Mesue says that wheet after it be husked is destilled in a vessel
of sublimacio^, as the Philosophers oyll. It cures ring worms, and ruggednes
of the skin which springs of drynes, it mollifieth and moysteneth, and other
infections, also of the skin it remedieth them. It is described also by Rasus
in his Antidotarium. Look within emo^gst the oils not destilled. There might
be made also of Barley and such both by sublimacion, and also by descencion.
Monachi upon Mesue.

Oils of Musk ben, (that is Balanus my repsica,) Fnrnerius calls Retraban
or retraha, of most precious smell. Like as of Almondes, so shall thou press
out an oyll of the fruits of Ben (which are found about Genua ple^teously
enough, of the same price almost that Almondes be) thou shall let it stand, and
put a good quantity of musk into it, and so in a glass well stopped, thou shall
set it in a ketle that it may sees a while, then receive the oyll by
destillacion, which shall be most swiet and most odoriferous.

Certain oils of siedes which I found in a book of Waters of Raimund
Lullius.
THis oils I thought good to write severally, because I doubt whether the
author would have them made by destillacio^ or simpely by pressing out: in
every one he adds thies words and let an oyll be drawn out after the manner
of the to signify, simpely by the pressing out of the siedes in a pres, as the
commun people is wont to do, so is oyll to be gathered, specially when as he
would have it drawn out of the same dried in the sun. Notwithstanding it is
out of all doubt, that the same things destilled accordingly, should be far
moore effectual: But for destilling, the liquor wherein the siedes pund or
stiept ougth to be left with them and putrification to go before. etc.

Stiep the siedes of Sponsa solis in woman's

Oyll of the siedes of Rue. Mixed the siedes of Rue pund with leued gould, and
put it in vynegar a day. Then dry it lightly by the sun and draw out an oyll
after the manner of the lay men. It is precious and resists poisons, so that
they shall avoid by vomit, if so be it a man drink of it the second time, the
other humors also that be infected wound and cure be had accordingly. Also
being druncke fasting and puts away the Gutta or drop and all scabbednes:
and the dropsy sprunge of a cold cause, if it be taken but two days. To
conclude it stirs up and restores all sliepy and benumbed members, (taken
with the Palsy, being anointed upon.)

Ruberta (if it be right written, perauenture of the color he so calls it:
I would rather read Sperma,) that is oil of Nasturtium (I would rather
Nasturtium, as in other in the same place, before and after the oil) moystened
in sharp vinegar, dry it in the sun, and draw out the oil after the manner
of the lay people.

This mixed with Aloes and oil of bays, cures all scabbednes and the drop,
the places being anointed. Drunken with Aloes and cooles of an elm, it
heals the Tercian and quartaine, and all breches in the body. Soden with a
ptisan and drunken, it stauncheth the bloudye flux and binds

Oil of the seeds of Pimpernell. Put seeds in read wine a few days, dry
them, punne them and make an Oil after the lay manner. This oil drunk
fasting, dissolueth, breaks and expels the sand (and any stone of the
bladder, yea if it be finished (gathered to y^ form of a stone) what matter so
ever it be of. It lighteneth the wearied members of a man's body. These

Of oil of the beries of Juniper, destilling first by ascencion, then by
descencion as I learned of my friends.
TAke a pint or a quarte full of the Bearies of Juniper, when they are beaten
stiepe them two days in well spring water: then put those beries together
with the water wherein they were sooked into a Cucurbita or bladder, as they call
it, Alsatia, about iiii. ounces of oil. Other to separate the Oil from the
water, put it into a drincking glass that has the foot taken away either by
coming with a wire of iron read hot, or else by chance so that a little
hoole in the bottom be left for the oil the hoole is stopte, and then the
oil may be poured out into ye vessel where it shall be kept. So^e destill it in
like manner, but they move the matter oftentimes in the still, least it be
burned. And they say spices beaten, as Cloves and cinnamon may be rightlye
destilled likewise. Some, as I hear say, destill in a crooked vessel not of
glass, but of Copper, tinned in the inside: which is put into a long pipe of
coper, as they do for burning water.

An other way of preparing the same by descencion

Then being well beaten, thou shall put them Aqua vitae,) specially the
arthritical parts and joints, this juice is easelye conserved, neither is it
corrupted lightlye, it is also swiete and effectual or strong. Some get out
the waterye humor not by a pres, but separate it in a commun furnace, (Balneo
Mariae.) The reast of the matter well dried and beaten, put it in a Cucurbita
that is hable to receive a galon, but thou shall not fill it up. In it (shit
with the clay of wisdom least any thing brethe out) shall thou putrify the
matter, about.xxviii. days in horse donge: or if it be winter (in Germany) nigh
unto the furnace, for to putrifye it in horse donge a and make it sure with clay
(and the hooll also of the bottom shall thou fill every where with clay) then
let down that part of the vessel by the hooll in the middle of the furnace so
far, that the nether vessel also (that is) the receiver may be put with in the
nether skirt of the plate, and fensed with clay. The clay ought to be tempered
with salt water and flox: this) sum call clay of wisdom. The manner of the
furnace is this: In the midst must a floor or chamber be made with a hooll, as
I said: above Cucurbita is sand laid almost til the third part of the
Cucurbita: above the sand, cooles burning, more then the third part of the
Cucurbita, nether needs it to put away the ashes: the rest of the Cucurbita,
the fourth part perauenture, or little les then the third part must be above
empty and at liberty. It so be thou feer that the upper Cucurbita perauenture
shall not be able to abide the force of the fire, thou shall pariet it with
clay, at the least wife in that part that the cooles oyll certainly drawn out
by descretion in a glass, siemeth to me to be les infected with ye faut of
adustion or brentnes. Hitherto although me thinks I have described the matter
plain enough as my friend rehearsed unto me, yet will I ad sum figure, that I may
lay the thing before the eyes most clearly: A. is the Cucurbita containing
the beries: B. the cooles: C. the sand: D. ye plate E. the floor whereupon the
sand lies. F. the Cucurbita, that receives: G. the round wreeth of straw
whereupon ye receiver is set.

Oyll of the nuttes kernels of the pyn tree for the polishing and putting
Syluius.

Of oils of gums, teeres or liquors, thickened or congeled, and rosines.
OYll of Mirh. Look before emongst the swiet waters of Furnerius, where we
have described one which is made with one part of Mirh, and the half part of ye
juice of Roses, most odoriferous.

Liquors thikned by nature, and gums as they call them, of a hoot and dry
temperatur, that they may be prepared unto destillacion, when they are pund,
put them in a vessel well stopped (wyn also, except I be deceived, may be a little
sprinkled upon it) and dig it in a could and moist place so diep as a man is
hy, without putting to it any hoot matter, and it must be left a good long
space: notwithstanding it should sooner be resolved if thou put to Caphura also,
seeing they contein a fat liquor and whyt, that which by destillacion is drawn
from them, do easily savor of the injury of the fire and faut of brentnes,
that although they be destilled in vessels very mien, yet issues forth a
licour full of dregs, gros, brent, and of an vnplesaunt as power forth any gums
or teeres.) Thies for the most part writes Ryffius in his first book of
destillacion.

The same Ryffius in the treatise of the same work of oils prescribes no
peculiar way to draw out oil of gums: but sends men unto his first koke. He
describes severally the virtues of oils of Ammoniacum, Belzoum, Camphora,
Cloves, Euphorbium, Galbanum, Ladanum, Myrh, Opopanax, Sarcocolla, Sagapenum,
Stirax liquid and Calamita. In the only oyll of Mastick he wills the Mastick
when it is pund, to be resolved with old wine (and to be degested) then
destilled. And the gum of Juniper likewise to be resolved and stiept in wine,
because of the drynes of the substance thereof, and afterward to be destilled.

Oil of Belzoum. Thou shall water a pound of Belzoum or more groos beeten, with
burning water: and in a crouked still with a receiver set under thou shall
destill it in ashes, with a slow fire Furnerius.

Oyll of Styrax out of ye same. Thou shall beete somewhat groos Styrax Calamita
that which is full of juice and fat, and water it with the best Aqua vitae:
then destill it in a crouked still as the oyll before, and kiep the water by it
self. This oyll excels with a marvelous fragra^t and swiet savor

Oyll of Camphora. Look before in the wawater of camphora, amongste the simple
waters destilled.

Of oil of Turpentine or larigna resina.
PVt.iiii. pound of Turpentin Rosin or of larix in a larg crooked still or
cucurbita of glass, and destilling it, get out an oil, so that the cucurbita or
crooked still be put in sand, first of al with the water shall an oil issue
Valerius Cordus. More of oil of Turpentin, and of the preparinge of it and of
the hertues thereof writes Ryffius, which I for shortness sake let pas. This is
chiefly tobe taken head of, that in the destilling, it sieth not, as in honey
also: for they rise and swel quickly these liquors when they are made hot:
wherefore at the first the fire must be made very light and scle^der Benedict yt
by the same means of the oil incresed. The description following makes with
me, which I found in a certain written book. Take pure sand, or little white
and clear flintes and put them over the fire in a vessel till they wax red
hot, then quench them in turpentin, that they may drink well, and that sand
quenched destil it in a lembeck. Some commend oil of turpentin for the grief of
the stone. Also those, oil of Turpentyn, of a pound of Turpentyn, an ounce of
old tile slates, (or as Albucasis says, new tile slates because they may
drink the more oil) and Mastik and Styrax, of either an ounce. The tyles made
red hot are slekt in oil: when they are quenched and pund they are mixte with
the other in a lembeck of glass. Three liquors run out, whereof y^ third is the
best. Iac. Hollerius amongst oils without smell for could greues.

Otherwise out of a written book. In a cucurbita half full of Turpintyn, put a
handful of glass pund, and.ii. sponges of the quantity of.ii. fingers
cucurbita, and let the fire be continued.xxiiii. hours: when the first
destillacion is finished, destill it again, renewing y^ glass, the cucurbita and
the sponges. To put away skars, or rather to assuage and mollify them, oil of
Turpentin does chiefli profit, except those that remain after warts. For they
that commend this oil for the putting away the marks of wartes, they are
deceived. Brasalonus.

Oil of Tartarum sublimated. Put Tartarum beten in a vessel (a cucurbita of
glass parieted with claye, or an earthen cucurbita) and when it is put inalembek
of glass destil it. First of all water will run forth, then oil, which thou
shall receive by it self increasing the fire by little and little til it leave
running. The dutch written book: Certain practicioners commend the spirit or
quintessence of Tartarum against inward impostumes and kings evil.

Oiles of barks.
Oil of Cinnamon is made, as we declared before out of Cardanus, how oils be
drawn out of woods and like things, as Cloves: where is also described the
instruments. Or else as we described out of Ryffius of the drawing out of oils
of all kinds of spices. Cinnamo^ may be stiept about viii. days in burninge
water.vi. times destilled, and the^ be destilled, as I was informed of a fre^d.
Here will I rehearse also the way to make water (as they call it) of Cinnamon:
for in the destillation or rather longer, an other showed us he had seen in
the destillacion of this water: but peraue^ture it shall be les nied of such a
long one, when no great plenty of water is destilled.) It may also be
destilled in a Cucurbita of glass parieted with clay, after the manner of Aqua
fortis, and perauenture it would be best that way. Put a pound of the best
Cinnamon pund not sifted, in the bottom of a still warely, least the powder s^ir
abroad or cleue to the sides, and by and by pour to it a.iii. pintes of freash
and put linne^ clouthes dipped in could water about the pype, and about the cover
likewise if it be too hoot. For it skilleth much that thies parts be couled
that both more plenty of water and also better may run out. This water of
Cinnamon I have seen my self and tasted, very plesaunt and swiet in savor and
in taste. The liquor which I saw was sharp, almost of the color of milck and
troubled: whereupon certain sumwhat red drops of oyll did swim, which were les
swiet then the water, Cardanus writes generally, of liquors of swiet woode^
things as Cloves. etc. This liquor restores the strength excellently: the
other virtues thereof Ryffius describes: but every man may know the^
manifestly by the nature of cinnamon it self.

In like manner is the oyll of the husk of Nutmegges which they call Mace,
prepared, which also is described by Ryffius.

An oyll marvelous swiet smelling, wherewith Furnerius. Thou shall take in the
month of May the.iii. parts of the second barks of the tree of Almon (so is
it red says Furnerius in the book out of which he writ it, but doubted
whether it ought to be understood an Elm or a Limon a kind of Orenges) and
the fatter parts of the flowers of the same tree (Les espis de la fleur qui
sont grasses,) in like weight, dry them in the sun. That kind of this tree is
thought best that the third part of the beries of Juniper new, and destill
them dounwarde, with a little fire at the first, then greater and greater
increasing it till all the Oyll be cumd down into the receiver, which should be
of glass and closed with clay. Afterward thou shall set it in the Sun, with the
mouth of the vessel open a while, or shit with a parchement pricked through
with a few hooles, till the smell of adustion and brentnes brethe out. Sum to
encrees the smell, put to it Belzoum, Spick narde, Agallochum, the shauings of
Cypres, Styrax, Ambra,, Mosch and destill all together, repeting also the
destillacion the second time.

Of oils that are drawn out of Woods.
Oils of wood are all destilled by descencion (as they call it) dounward. Of
the preparing of oyll of the wood of Juniper, Vlstadius, where we entreted of
destillacion dounward generally. He that desires more, specially perteyning to
the virtues, let him search in the duche bouke of Ryffius, who writes that
oyll is gotten out of Guaiacum also after the same manner utterly: and also out
of the wood of Rosemary. I will ad here that I found in a certain dutche book.

The wood of Juniper to make oyll thereof ought to be cut in ye beginning of
May, or harvest, which is grien or fresh, the barck being red. When y^ barck
is taken of, thou shall dry it diligently in an oven straight after the bred is
taken out, then cut in pieces with an ax, put it in a pot that has the botom
full of hooles, and set hansumly in a pot uneath it. The upper pot thou shall
fill with ye chips of Juniper and also of that whyt Popler which they call
trembling, with a part of the whyt stones whereof lyme is made: after when thou
have fensed the cover well with clay, put it into the nether and Brasauolus
commends the same against the pestilence. It puts away cold causes, and
represeth the typum of the quartain. Being anointed from the navel of women to
privy Rogerius.

Oyll destilled of the sticks of Juniper most dry, in two earthen vessels
with the mouths joined together, or also in a lembeck of glass, if it be holden
in y^ mouth, it takes away maruelously the touth ach sprung of a could reum,
and likewise it is good against all oher grieves coming of could humors, as
the griefs of the synewes, the conuulsion or cramp of the joints, the palsy,
and like, Matthaeolus. Oil of Juniper (says Mesues) helps the infections of
the skyn, crieping diseases, the cancar, angry byles, such as in sommens legs
the late writers call the deed evil. Fill a glased vessel with a narrow
mouth, with the small chippes of Juniper: ioyn a thin plate of iro^ boored
full of hooles after the manner of a meell sieve, to the hooll mouth: daub the
same with the mouth both of the said vessel and of the lesser, Mesues as
Siluius interprets him. In the same place Siluius in the commentaries. By
this art (says he) oil is made of the kurnelles of Pynaples to put away the
wrinkles of wemens skins, of the wood Gnaiacum, and stronger of Lignum sanctum
for the grieves of the Spanishe disease as byles (unto which oyll of Juniper
also and of Broum, and of ash and of like, is holsum) and of other woods,
specially that be fat and full of juice as fruits beries and seeds. The biggar
vessel also may be of metal, or if it be of glass or ston, let it be well
fensed all over with clay of wisdom, an erthen vessel because it is spongi
and not so cloos, when the oyll seeks to breeth out it would give it way:
therefore let it be glased within, the nether only in the circuit but somewhat
more thik, it may be also of metal: it may be at length of earth, so be that it
be glased and oyled as they call it: else that most thin oil would run out here
and there by the pores of the vessel: much les may that oil be received safe in
the pit within the ground having the sides walled with potters earth, (which
notwithstanding Mesues commanded.) Take this withal, that it shall not be
nedeful to dig the lesser vessel within the ground, if thou canste find a
means Syluius.

Oil of ash tree helps them that be sick of the spleen, besides that it can
do all that oil of Juniper can, and is made after the same manner Mesues. Where
again Syluius says. That oil anointed upon the left side under the short ribs,
and drunken, delivers mightily the obstructions and stoppings of the spleen:
it mollifieth also and digesteth.

This oil (says Rogerius) represseth the cold greues of the joints: it
heals a wound and brings skin where it lacks: it delivers from the
white morphew, and blackeneth it.

Io. Manardus in his epistles. 16. 4. giving counsel for ye health of the
cardinal Campegius sick of ye gout: I praise (says he) both the manner and the
use of the foam of the decoction of lignum sanctum or of Iudicum to the place
where the grief is. But I think the oil of the same wod, destilled after y^
manner more effectual, anointing the places therewith yt swel and where the grefe
is. They be wont also of the shauings of raw wode, sodden in some noble wine,
putting unto it old oil, to prepare an ointment which is very good to be
anointed upon the places where the grefe is.

Take ye wode cut like bordes, therwt fill a new erthe^ vessel, with a cover
of the same matter board through with little holes: yt diligently claid let an other
empty erthe^ pot digd in the earth up to the mouth, Andreas Mat in his book of
the frensh disease.

A man may also, as I hard of a friend, destil guaiacu^ after the same way that
Aqua fortis is destilled, best in an erthen vessel glased yt will abide the
fire, such as they make at Haganoa. This oil is good for the fauts of ye
joints through the frensh pocks, if it be anointed upon the places with the best
burning water. Oil of the wode guaiacu^, and more mightilye of lygnum sanctum,
is wholesome for the spanish diseases and biles. Sylu. A certain practicioner
even so also yet is the best oil destilled of.

Take the wod of iuy dried, and the beries and gum also if you may have it.
When they are cut in pieces let the^ be put in an erthen vessel bored through in
the bottom in.ii. or.iii. places: and then let an other pot be set under it.
Set the^ in the earth, and join the botto^ of the upper unto the mouth of the
nether with clay or paste: and the upper pot must stand hollye above the ground.
At length make a fire on every side, and the oil shall destill black into the
nether Rogerius.

The preparing of oil of capuistu^ that is smoked because it is made by
descencion, like as the oil of woods, I have rehearsed it in this place out of
the first book of Aetius, where he entreats of oils: from whence Nicholas
Alexandrinus also borrowed it in his treatise of oils. Vngues aromatici that be
black (melancho, Fuchsius the expositor of Nicolas reads Megalco^, that is
great) thus Masculu^, styrax bdellium pure Costus, of every one. v dra^s (of
every one.ii. ounces and a half, says Nicolas which I like better).v. sextaris
that is about. iiii pints of the best swiet oil, (ii. and a half says Nich.)
Hypni as much as need requires. The costus cut in pieces so^what gros, and
likewise ye styrax and bdelliu^ mixed together, put the^ into an erthe^ vessel
(a new one: Nycolas) without eres, ye mouth whereof you shall stop with the hypne^,
and without you shall defense it with the slips of aspalathus or some odoriferus
thing ha^somli of the other vessel that contains the spices agreing aptly
with the mouth, into which thou shall put.v. sextaries of sweet oil.
Afterward dig the ground and bury the earthen pot that holds the oil unto
the neck, lest it wax red hot after with the fire that shall be made about it:
then turn ye other vessel with spices and the mouth downward upon ye head of
it, and soioyn y^ mouths of the^ both, yt they may be closed together most
exactly. Then close ye hole vessel round about with clay, on every side where the
mouths are joined together, by and by thou shall kindle a fire putting under
coals about ye earthen vessel, and blowing. When the fire is kindled let the
spices being consumed, yt being set on fire theimai se^d out their vapor by the
mouth of ye erthe^ vessel into ye oil set under: For ye which cause it is
called capnisto^ y^ is smoked. The second day after remove away the oil, and
put it in a glass to keep. Women use this whose flours are stopped, anointing the
nether part of their belly and loins therewith. It is convenient tenesimus, if
it be received in wol (hot as Nicolas adds) folded together and laid to the
lower part of the belly and loins. The same seems to be called capnelaeu^ or
smoked oil: but the author of Kiranidum expounds it naphtha, in his.iiii.
book where he makes mention of the fish Ecleneis or Remora.

I have seen also of paper roled together in manner of a hod, and set on fire
over a dish of tin, holden at the very extreme top with a pair of sheeres (or
tongues) certain drops of black oil run out, which are praised for the spots or
whytneses of ye eyes.

Of true Balm, and antibalm, that is Oils composed by art, which are used
in steed of the true Balm both within and without the body.
BAlsamum is a word used almost in Syrian word without dout: for in only Syria
and in one only garden did it ones grow. Panag ye hebrew word in the.xxvii.
of Ezechiel, David Kimhi after certain does erpound it a proper place of
Iudaea: other Apharsamon, that is Balsamum. The later writers of the Jews wryt
Palsamon: Mycander Balsamo^ bycans of the verse. Opobalsamon is a liquor:
Carpobalsamon a sied, which sum call also Casamum: Xylobalsamum, bows or the
wood. I imagined once that it was called Balsamum of the excellency, because
it was most precious and most noble of all spyces: for Boal signifies lord
and master with the Hebrews and Sam spyce. Balsamon, is an aromaticall
flower, Varinus. And in died it is reason, that as the thing it self is proper
to Syria, so the etimology and interpretacio^ of the word should be taken of
the same tongue. Peraue^ture Belsuin also (other writ it Beniuin, other
Belzoum) a liquor of most swiet savor has the same Origin of his name. Not
same only with the Hebrews but also Bosem signifies a swiet smell ling spyce,
whereupon the Germans may seem to have called Mosch Bisem.

Balsamum or Balm sum in our age think it is lost, the juice of it was of the
color of gould, it he led wounds, and took away wrinkles, and kept the faces
of deed men from corruption: it brake also the stone in the reins, it did put
away the spots Aconitum, Hemloke, and the shaking of very fevers, and in all
thies things the virtues of Balm excelled. But now there is another kind of
Balm brought out of Spain, of a red color te^ding toward blak, weighty,
sauouring sumwhat of adustio^ or brentnes, and by and by it stryketh and
perceth the heed, and the tongue also sharply, but sumwhat long first as
peper. And it also heeleth new wounds more quikly then any other thing: and
takes away Goacomax, of the form of a Pomgranat tree and in bigness almost
like: but it has a lief a little biggar and a thinner barck as though it were
dry, the wood of it burns like a torche, the fruit is as the clusters of
grapes, but the kurnelles are sumwhat biggar, of a wyny color, which together
do yield that kind of oyll, Cardanus.

Certain merchants affirm that the gardeines of Balm were destroyed, what
time as Zelimus the Turck, the predecessor of Solomon, wan Chayrum, when the
Turks killing the gardeners, did not forthwith put other in their places the
year of our Lord. 1516. Moreover they say there is a Balm tree of an
indifferent bigness laid to keep to be compared unto many thousands of Ducates,
with the most famous man of our time, Lucullus, May. Antony Fuggerus: Joan.
Agricola in his commentaries upon Galen, of the diseased places.iiii. ix.

Antibalsama. Dioscorides prescribes certain notes and tokens of the true
Balm. In the collection and table of those medicines that may be used one in
steed of another, which is joined with Galenus works, and in the end also of
Aeginetas books, we reed: In the steed of ye liquor of Balm, Mirh that
destilleth, that is liquid and runninge Stacte. In the same place is red also:
In the steed of Opobalsamu^ the liquor of Myrti: but it seems it should be
more rightly ye liquor of Mirh. Auicenna in his second book in the chapt. of
oil: In steed of Balm (says he) is liquid Mirh used, or else like weight of the
oil Adhii, or Aldadi, or Dadi as Bellunensis translated it. Rasis in the latter
end of his Antidotarie separated, in oil Benedict or of tylstons: It is of
like force (says he) yt oil of Balm: but it is more subtle and hoter and more
Monaci upon Mesuen. And a little after, in the tretis quid pro quo, what for
what, which is imprinted with the books of the sun of Mesue, it is red. In
the stead of Balm, Turpintyn destilled, or oyll of Bays, or the gum of Iuy is
put. But what Dadi is, I can not easily say: it is described of Auicen the.
213. chapt. Bellunensis nether there nor in the gloses expounds what it is.
It is a grain (says Auicenna) like unto barley longar, more strait and cloos
together, more massy, bitter, could, declyning to heet, and dry CiTum or
Ladanum, for it is could also, so measurably yt it has a warm heet, according
to Galenus mind: it byndeth, stops ye belly, and dries in the second degree:
that is hooter that grows in hooter places, and the same mollifieth meanly
and dissolved, and concokteth or digesteth. Furthermore in Antiballomenis
Graecis, Propolis to be used in the steed of Tragopogona, that is Ladanum: and
Auicenna, in the resolving (says he) of hardnesses, ii. third parts in weight
of kur, that is Propolis, and the half of the weight of abhel. Dioscorides
says that Ladanum is mixed with medicines without smell: Auicenna, that it is
good for the griefs of the fundament and hemrodes. Now that Auicenna has,
that Dadi is like unto barley, but longar, straiter. etc. I plainly suppose it
to be corrupt out of Dioscorides, who makes Ladon that is the bushe of
Ladanu^, like unto Cisthum, with longer leaves only and more black. Last of all
the names Ladon and Dadi do not much disagree nor be far unlike.

Oil made of the flours of Rosemary (after the same manner as it is made of the
flours of Verbascus) may be used instead of balm, and a drop of it put into
water, does likewise go to the botto^e: Arnold de Villa noua. They put this
also emongst other to be a toke^ of true balm, y^ it will gather milk into
courds: I going about to try on a time, whether Ryffius oil of cloves. In ye
greek Antiballominis it is red yt the rotes of white violets Xylobalsanu^. The
Antiballomena or rehearsal of things y^ may be used one for another, which was
once imprinted with Mesues works declare in this wise. In sted of Xylobalsam, the
wod of iuy or Leucoradix. For carpobalsa^, the fruit of iuy or Xylobalsamu^ in
like weight. The same: and for balm it self, the gum of iuy. But peraue^ture
in all these ye expositer or tra^slater was deceived, when as no writer ever
compared iuy in any wise with balin: therefore I thought Arabik tongue to have erred,
or rather the Arabians them selves, confounding Cisson, that is iuy and Cisthum
together: for Ladanum is the juice of Cisthum, which Auicenna did put instead
of Balm.

In the Antiballomenis asscribed unto Galen I find these also: for the liquor
of balm, ye liquor of Carpasus: and for the liquor of Carpasus, the liquor of
Myrtus: and for the liquor of willow, the liquor of black iuye, which the
Arabians leuing certain things or changing them, translated them into Cassia
called Balsamodes because of the smell. Sisymbrium a kind of wild mint, growing
ni waters and for the most part so^what red, ye late writers many of the^ call
it Balsamita. There is an other Balsamina as the Ligures about ye river of Poo
call it: The Thuscans or Florentins call it the apple of Ieru salem, with the
leaves of the white vine, ye flower of the Cucumer, the fruit atboth ends round
and sharp like to a little cucumer. etc. the apple with ripenes Lentil, most
red, wheroute oil is pressed chief for wounds. Some season the apple first in
oil and set it in the sun a few days, then they bery it in horse dong or in the
earth so long til it be clean putrified: so they affirm it will get the
virtue of balm in closing of wounds, and thereupon has it gotten the name of
Balsamina: Ruellius out of Her molaus. Brasauolus calls this oil of Cochirs:
and and sharp husk, Cochia or Momordica. The oil (says he) is prepared diverse
ways, both by infusion and being digd under the earth many years, and by
seathing: and it is made of Echinus, of the leaves severally and of the seeds.
That which is hid in the earth, helps the greues of Hemrods. Generally, that
oil asswageth all greues, who so desires to know mo and very marvelous
effects of this oil and herb, let him read Matthaeolus Senensis commentaries
on Dioscorides the.iiii. book

Balsamaeleon, that is the oil of balm, of a plant a cubit high or.ii. cubits,
like unto wild Rue, by the bark whereof toward the East, being scarified rased
and wounded fat tears destill, that is the fatnes of the balm. Other do beat
in a mortar the slips of this plant in the beginning of Vere, sethe it in
water, press them in a press, and call it oil Mesues, Syluius translating
him. Where Syluius also in his scoles says: let Xylobalsamum and Carpobalsamum
most new be stiept one day in old oil: after let ye oil be drawn out by the art
of chemists, it shall be of no small estimacio^: or set Xylobalsamum new be putin
old oil and set The virtues of this oil so much praised of al me^ and nothing
spoken of Mesue, it is meruel, for it heteth moysteneth, extenuateth and makes
slender, digesteth, scoureth, closes. This virtue of closinge she wed unto the
old writers, makes it at this day precious. Opobalsamum is most rare and
gesen: and therefore most precious. Wherefore Petrus of Abanus, Guilielmus
Placectinus, Bartolemaeus Montaguanus, composed an oil of balm, nothing
inferior in virtues to balm. Other put the leaves and sede, and wode of this
plant in oil and set it Opobalsamum: This says Syluius.

The Egiptians make a counter^aite kind of Opobalsamum, of ye bark new most
swiet smelling heating it in oil of Almondes, of a singular smell when it is
fresh mixed: Alexan. Benedictus.

The oil that they call communlye Balsaminum raises up suddenly them that be
fallen of the falling ficknesse, being heide to their nose that they may
Balsamum.

Of balmes made by Art.
THe commun intentand purpose of all Balsams or balmes, to speak of their
virtue, seems to be, to close and heal wounds and biles, and that in a short
space, and to avoid and turn away the evil Symptomata or diseases which the
sores would otherwise parts: hereupon it is that all of them have Turpintyn
Rosin, sum also other rosins, as the rosin of Pyn tree, Mastik: here upon also
gums ar added Frankensence, Elemi, gum of Iuy, Bdellium, Sarcocolla, Mumy, etc.
Unto sum diverse spyces are put to smell the swieter: whereupon they are made apt
for the moo uses, and miet for diverse diseases even within the body also, as to
those also, unto which moreover diverse plants or their parts are added. They
are destilled the most part of the^ They do al season the vrin with their smell.
They must all be received within the body mixed with sum liquor, chiefly wine,
so that to a sponful of liquor, one drop of the oil or.ii. at the most be put.
The dregs remaining in the bottom are good for nothing, saving that they may
be used in steed of Colophonia or Scammonium. I same of late a practicioner
destilling I can not tell what kind of Balsam in a pan, with ashes laid upon
slates, as I described before in the mention of destillation Cucurbita of glass
so diep set in the ashes, that they were not above the matter contained in the
vessel. The vessel was full to the middle, able to receive, if it had been
filled up perauenture, iiii. pounds: he continued this labor.iiii. or.v. days
and nights also, never abating the fire. He separated only.ii. liquors, ye
first whyt, which was more plentiful and in gretter abundaunce: then a
redish, which was yielded lesser by the.iii. part. The clay wherewith the
Cucurbita, the lembeck it was good to chronicall agues, for the most part he
mixed with it sum spyces, as Ginger and Sugar with wine and the drop of the
oyll, that the sick should les perceive that he dronk only a drop of the oyll:
sumtymes he would mixed nothing else with the wine but one drop of this oyll,
specially to amend the defaut of a stinking breeth. Sumtymes he gave it in
water, other times he dropped it into a shyue of breed: sumtymes to phlegmatic
and gros men, with a sauce made of musterd and

An other moore noble of the same man's which is put in steed of Balm. A pound
and a half of oyll, Myrh, Xylobalsamum, Opoponax, Bdellium Aloes,
Carpobalsamum, Ammoniack, Serapinum, the nut of Inde, Hypericon, Mace, gum
Arabic, Tragacantha, of every one an ounce: broken till sherdes that never
touched water* red hoot and quenshed in three ounces of common oyll, vii. ounces
of cleen and clear Turpintyn. All pund and knoden together in a mortar, destil
them like Rose water. This water is proffitable against ye stoon, being mixed
with medicines against ye same. Hardnesses and skares it makes them even and
is used in every thing in steed of Balm.

An other of the same man's more noble. Myrh Carpobalsamum, the nut of Inde, of
every one half an ounce. Hypericon or saint John's wurt a drame (otherwise two
drames.) When they are pund sumwhat groos, let them be mixed with five ounces of
oyll, and an ounce and an half of Turpintyn. In the end put to five grains of
Musk, and. iii of Ambra, and an ounce of oyll of till stones, and let them be
destilled as before. It has the same virtues that Balm has and may be used in
all things in steed of it. This Nic. Stokker also an excellent if mifrend sent
me the description of it right when it was ready pressed, he adds at length
the Musk and Ambra, with oil of tile stones, and destilled them not as I think.
He has the former description in Luminari Maiore, as also the next following
of Montaguana.

A balm composed of Bartolomeus Montaguana out of his Antidotarie the first
chapter which is of oyntmentes. Turpentin a pound: white frankensence. iiii,
ounces: as much of bay beries: gum Elemi. vi. ounces: Mastik: Galangal, Cloves,
Cinnamon, Zedoaria, Nutmeg, Cubebae, Lignum Aloes well beaten, of every one an
ounce. Let all be destilled together, first with a slow fire: and first shall
run out a water called of Balm. Then when the fire is increased thou shall
gather an other water by it self. And do so the third time. The^ shall destil
forth balm in all trials. It shall be the stronger the oftener it is destilled.

Balm of Peter Aponensis in his addicion unto Mesues in the treatise of
oyntinges (for the diseases of the heart.) Mirrh elect, Aloes Hepaticae,
Spieaenardi, Sanguis Draconis, Frankense^ce, Mumiae, Opopanax, Serapinum,
Crocus, Mastik, Gum Arabik, liquid Styrax, of every one two ounces, two
ounces and a half of Ladanum elect, or Castoreum: half a dram of Muscke.
Turpentyn as much as the weighte of all the reaste break them that be to be
broken and when they are all mixte with the Turpentyne, destyll water of
Rooses. These says Peter, as the munkes that write upon Mesuen, said they
write it out of written books, far truer then in the Printed books, and it
is had in Luminare maiore. Aponensis says, we find no mention made by the
old writers of the annoyntinge of the back bone, perauenture not because they
were ignorant in so profitable and commendable a thing, but be cause they
would keep it secreate. For this is an excellent help, preseruinge the
subject of life Nucha, and it springs of the brain. etc. Therefore things
anointed with this shall thou comfort the cloking substance (that is the cauls
and cotes of the brain) and the spiritual substance and sinues, and all the
bones helping also the Palsy and all the diseases of the sinnewes, also the
panting and trembling of the heart, and manifest werines, and it is the chiefest
medicin of all other in the swifte comforting of the

When thou will comfort bodies that be extenuated and brought low, thou
shall mixte Roose water with it, and annoynte it upon the lower mansions, and
from the Nucha unto the raines. somewhat warm, an hour before the fit, leaving
upon it the token of it with Pecia, thou shall put away the shakinge of
wandering Agues, and of any simple agues. But quartaines and wanderinge Agues
it helps at the beginnninge of the course (this place and they which follow
seem unto some to be corrupted in the printed books) in the swoundinge or
debilitie, annoyntinge the extreme parts of the back boon: that the
instrumente for the purpoose may speak with voice, Aponensis.

The same manne wills to mixte this composicion in the steed of true

Oil (says he) Epiphanius Empiricus to all diseases of the sinews,

plain by manifest proves, specially in a cold matter. The same man commands
to still this oil in Balneo Mariae, which I like not.

to remove maladies which newly gnawe the flesh of man's body. The first drunk
with warm whyt wine, purges the stomach from al il humors: and withholdeth
the water that it cum not at the heart or principal parts, as it is plain by
often experiment. A fine lynnen clooth moystened in this water and thrust into
the Bombase, dipped lightly is this water, is very good to put in the ears
against any kind of deafnes. Being anointed it cures the rednes of the face:
the palsy of the tongue, and all cold diseases. The second and third water Noli
me tangere: against the kings il and also the disseases of the neck and
throot. Also against the fistula and the ill disease called Malus morbus,

linne^ cloth moystened therein upon it, it is made hool. They help also if a
ma^be beten with stones, or clubes or a staf. No poison can approach ny unto
them, and a spyder touched therewith dies. They be anointed upon most
proffitably against all palsyes. They strengthen all the

In the same Lullius a marvelous water is made in this wise. Cloves,

Whyt old honey half a pound. After twys as much Sugar as all the forsaid be. All

The virtues of the first and of the second water are thies. If the one of the^
be poured in to a wou^d whiles it is new, there needs none other remedy: But
within a natural day and a half at the most it shall be made hool, so be that
it be no deed wound. All ill soores or byles, Old, roten Cankred, Fistula,
Lupus, Noli me tangere, and like to them, let them be washed with either of thies
waters, and they shall be heeled within a few days. One drop only dropped upon
a Carbuncle quensheth carnosity bred upon it, drop one drop of thies waters
upon it every third day, and within nine days it shall be hool, except it be
utterly destroyed. A drop of them drunk with a little good wine breketh the
stone in the reins or in the bladdar, or in the yard stopped and that within
two hours, and delivers from the grief. If deed flesh be washed away
therewith, the place is shortly made hool. If a woman be sick of her womb or
belly, let her drink a little of them with sum juice. If a man have any

The third and bloody water, which surnamed holy and blessed, is so excellent
in virtues, that if one use half a sponefull of it.xv. days, he Astma or
disease of short wind, the dropsy, palsy, Ischia or Sciatica, the swounding,
the falling sickness, the drop in the joints called the gout, ye consuming
fever, the strangury, and many other diseases, and that within two months. It
recouereth youth unto old men: a man that lies a dying, out of all hope of the
physicions, it restores him, if one drop of it let fall into his mouth, be
swalowed, so that it may cum to the heart. If so be quantitye of a wheat corn
of this liquor with a sponefull of water of borage, destilled like Rosewater,
after the year is ended, he shall seem as though he were made new, in his
flesh, blud, and hole body, both in form and strength.

Balm artificial (says Matthaeolus Senensis, in his commentaries upon
Dioscorides, which I tried and found of maruelus strength against very many
diseases) have I made and composed in this wise. Take rosin Larignae, the teres
of firtre pure and liquide (some call it oil of fire, the Germans Bulhartz) of
either a pound: Manuae Thurus, Ladani, of either of them.viii. ounces: Spike a
dram mastik, Galangal, cloves, Casiae odorate, zedoariae Cubebarum, Agallochi,
of every one iii. ounces, Gum Elemi. vi. ounces: Aloes hepaticae, Castorei,
date stones, Storacis Calamitae, Myrrhae, Belzoi of every one an ounce. Beat
such as be to be pund and when they are mixed with the liquors destil them
artificially. First will a most subtle clear water run out, which burns
meruelousli, called the water of Balm. Then will follow a yelow oil, subtle,

Balm or oyll Benedict for wounds, palsy, etc. Oil of Turpintyn a pound. Oyll
of Bays two ounces. Galbani, Gummi Elemi, of either six drams. Gum of Iuy,

and change the receiver according to y^ changing of ye color of yt which is
destilled.

A Balm for skares. etc. If by the means of a struck (says Lullius about the
end of the second book of Quintessence) any great syne remayn in the Gummi,
Cyperi, Carpobalsami, of every one.ii. ounces. Saffro^an ounce. Turpintyn.ii.
pound. Oyll of Olives of the eldest.iiii. ounces. Pun the^ that be to be pund,
and sift them, and mixed them with the Turpintyn and Carpobalsamo, and together
with the oyll destill them with a slow fire. The destilled liquor pressed vynes.
Afterward thou shall use it, as true Balm, whose tokens and properties to
know it by it has every one, and may be sold in steed of it.

A marvelous water or oyll for strumes and swellings of the throote, whereupon
men are said to have swollen throotes. Oyll of Baies.vi. ounces. Olibani,
Mastick, Gummi Arabici, pure and clear Turpintyn of every one three ounces. Mixed
them in a mortar and destill them in a Lembeck. Afterward, put to ashes to the
water drawn (De la cendre Gallice) except it should be redd De la Cedre, that
we may understand the C^der fry. Then destill it again, and this second
liquor keep it like Balm. The swelling being anointed therewith oftentimes in
the day, ass^^ageth by little and little.

An artificial Balm, not to be destilled, but sod only, out of the French
book of Andreas Furnerius. Olibani, Galbani, oyll of white Poppy, oyll of

the Galbanum piece meell, and over a slow fire stur it softly now and then:
then put to the Mastick and the Olibanum, and stur it till they be melted by
little and little. Afterward the Pitche and Turpintyn, (but he left out the Pitch
before) with a slow fire fire, and put to the other two oils, and mixed the^ by
stirring, and again set them to the fire a little. At length put in the grienes
of the bras, mixed them thoroughly and streyne them through a new clooth into an
other vessel leeded also. When thou will use the forsaid oyll, thou must see
before that no sinew, or vein almost be cut. Then purginge the soor diligently
and making it cleen, thou shall make warm a little of this oyll in a vessel or
spoun of bras, and lay it upon so hoot as the sick may abide

Of oils of metals, tile stones, Gagate, Aumber.
WAters and oils, secreate by the singular Syluius. This virtue of pearsinge
seems to pertain chieflye unto oils drawn out of metalles, in the which
also is a greater force

I understand that Vinegar is chieflye used to be destild, for the drawing out
of oils out of Metalies, as Antimoni, Lead, Cerussa. Other use other sharp
and most hot liquors for that purpose, as sharply, burning water, urine
destilled, Aqua Forti.

Lullius in the fifth Canon of his first book of quintessence, when he had
taught to draw out the.iiii. elements out of plants, he added. And make
them to resolve with our Menstrue (I suppose he means our Urine) under
dounge for the space of a weke: the Menstruum must be sharp with some Vegetable
and strong quickenynge things which we shall declare hereafter in the
Questionary. After the metalles shall be dissolved set them to be destilled in
a fire of the first degree, and the Menstruum shall issue forth, and the lime
or powder of ye metal shall remain in the bottom. After this reiterat and
repete it again upon the dregs Menstruum as much as the weight of the metal,
and set it to putrifyinge for the space of a month and a half: and after this
destill it as thou did of the Vegetable or quickening things, but every time
put new Menstruum upon the dregs. Other diverse opinions of Philosophers in the
drawing out of the elements out of mineral things, we shall declare in the
third book: This says he.

I suppose it to be a commone thing unto all Cardanus signifieih and an other
certain author writes, that the drops oil of Vitriol or Coproos to be
ponderous and weighty.

Oil destilled of Orpment or Mysi (or Vitriol of Rom.) anointed upon ye
arteries and region of the heart, I suppose is hable to save a man infected with
poison, be it never so sharp and strong, and do kill a man only with
touching: Cardanus.

And a little after, but sense we are fallen into nor far from the purpose to
inquere this how oil may be made which being anointed upon the Arteriis,
makes the venom to break out by vomit or purgacion, or sweat, or urine, It
is sure, it must be of metal, which must be most strong. I saw such once
and by the waighte only I coniectured that it was without al doubt of metal,
It must also be of the nature of Venom: for by the immoderate heat, as it
is said, it vanquisheth first the evil infection conceived Laser or Assa
Foeetida. Therefore those things that must drive out the poysone, ought to be
metally poisons, but not most bitter: and most hot and discussing or expelling,
also in a manner contrary to the poisons. The matter therefore Mysi and Orpment,
and the juice of Laser or Assa foetida, and Gentian, and of the fat of venomous
serpents, and Aconitum. If so be it that in any land moo of these (foresaid
virtues, as to discus, expel, and resist poysones. etc. be to be gotten, the
oil extracted by the force of fire shall be best of all. And a little after.
But oil that only by anoyntinge of the Arteries does thruste out the
poysone, I would not call it the best in this sense drink, Triacle or Milk
or sum other excellent medicin, ye also it should be the more auaylable. That
dare I be bold to say, that the anointing of the arteries, and the things
ministered outwardly, are better and of more strength then those things that are
drunck, save only for this that the poison remains yet in the stomach. For
unto such (poisons newly taken, that they be not yet gone out of the stomach)
they that provoke strong vomities are most excellent, as Milk, Lie, Oyll,
water of Nucis vomicae or spewing nut. Therefore in venomous bytinges, in
stingings in oyntmentes that be venomous, in the drinking of venom which is
now already perced to the midrif or nether parts of the chest, the helps that
be ministered outwardly are more auailable and of greater strength. etc. Thies
out of Cardane have I written the more at large, that thereby also ye reason
migth be the more evident of that marvelous oil of Scorpions. etc. Which
because it is made without destillacion,

Oyll of Antimonium is most proffitable to all ill soores and byles, as I my
self dotry with good succes. But the Antimonium is purged first, oftentimes
melted and made liquid with the fire. Far from this does the oyll of Antimonium
which chymistes use, wherwithall they endeavor to set the color of gould upon
silver: Matthaeolus upon Diosc.

Oyll of Antimonium how it is made for soore byles and fistulas. I writ before
out of Vlstadius, Quintessence. But certain practicioners also make oyll of
Antimonium to diverse diseases within the body, which they extol with
marvelous and great praises. They make it after this sort, as I here. Antimonii
half a pound, whyt Tartari Calcionated as much: let the^ be beaten and melted
or dissolved in a gouldsmithes Tigillo (crucibulo) upon cooles: when they are
melted let them be powered out into sum earthen pot, where they shall be sturred
and mixed together: and when they are becum into a hard lump, let them Hyppocras
as they call it) in a moist place (in a hoot and moist place) as in a wine
celler, the colender hanging: for so shall the oyll destill out by little and
little which of sum because of the bloody color is called Rubedo Antimanii,
and likewise it is made of certain Gouldsmithes for a certain painting I can
not tell what. But for the remedies of man's body it ought to be prepared with
Quintessence of wyn Antimonium: and let them be sturde diligently in body, and
mixed, and then destilled in ashes three or iiii. times, till the breath lose all the evil smell. The dregs must always be mixed and sturd about when you powrest
i^ again the destilled water upon the^, or when thou powrest to them new
Quintessence, for I am not sure which way it ought to be done. Sum in the
beginning do stregthway mixed reed Antimoniu^ and Tartaru^ calcionated to be
melted together. But I wryt althies not as certain: but as I noted the^ by the
way as I hard them recited certain years ago of a certain practicioner. I know
that certain chymistes and goldsmithes search for ye oil or Quint essence of ye
Antimoniu^ as a most perfect treasur. Thies destil first sharp lie composed of
vnslect lyme and ashes Clauellatas by a Filtru^: and is this lie they sieth the
Antimoniu^ subtly and fynly beten, for the space of.v. hours, and again
destil this lie when it is cold, by a Filtru^: and that of ye Antimoniu^
Antimoniu^ put in a Cucurbita or body, most sharp red vinegar destilled, so
that it be above it iii. or.iiii. fingars thick: they set it in a hoot place a
few days, and every day they shake and* mixed it, x. or.xx. times: then they
power ye vinegar lightly into an other glass, so yt nothing be troubled. This do
^hey repete.v. or.vi. times (ever pouring new vinegar upon the Antimoniu^
remaining in ye Cucurbita quintessence of Antimoniu^, is of a color of blud.
With this they die Mercury, and say it is an incomparable treasure, a chief
mystery of ye chymistical art: as we translated out of a certain written doutch
book. A reed oyll is gotten out of Antimoniu^, very sharp, smelling lyk
brimston, and it reteineth ye virtue of y^ Antiminium, because of the grosnes
of the substance proffitable Cardanus.

Hooll Lyme unbroken, let it be slekt in co^mun oyll, and destill it in a

Oyll of Gagates or ieet, called Sacratum or holy, most holsum for them that
be possessed of spirits, that have the falling sickness, the palsy, the cramp,
the stifnes of sinews, the gout, or be chooked in their womb, it helps also
co^ceiuing. It is made of Ieet stones after the manner of oyll of Juniper (by
descention) or else by sublimacion (as oyll of tyle stones) Mesues of Iac.
Syluius interpretacion: out of whose notes we have subscribed thies words:
Seeing Bitumen is far lighter cost then Ieet, Bitumen, forasmuch as it is as it
were the fat of the earth, and is burned in steed of oyll of them that dwell
about the place of Asphaltita or deed sea, or else in other places where Bitumen
is. Forthermore he douteth whether it can heel the falling sickness, seeing the
falling sickness does the more appeer with ye foul stinking, smell of the
Bitumen. But me thinks it with it by attenuating, digesting, drying, which
virtues boothe it has them befoore, and that not feablelye: and also much
moore the liquor destilled thereof perfourmeth these things.

Nether does it not help this diese therefore, because it is likely that the same
should be vttred and appear with the perfume of that bituminous smell, as with
the perfume of Myrrh, Galbanum, and horns, houfs, and the skins of a she or he
got. For Phisicions use the horns and houfes of diverse bestes Bitumen)
specially white, holden to the nosthrils of one sick of the falling evil, with
coals in a spone and the Amber upon them, and by and by Mosch or Ambra will do
also. But that Mesuae writes of men possessed with devils (says Syluius) is
supersticious, and against the faith of a Christen man. Brimston and iet are
molten at the fire as al other liquors. The same.

Oil of philosophers (which some have named oil of wisdom, and of perfect
mastership, and divine and holy) by all the old writers consent, is most
effectuous to secreat diseases, and tha^ not to a few means of the subtelnes
of the substance, it digesteth and consumes al excremental and superfluous
expelled matter. Therefore it is very wholesome for the falling sickness, palsy,
turning sickness, for getfulnes, and for the cold diseases of the spleen, rains,
bladder, womb, sinews, al joints, and other sinewy parts. But one sort is
natural or mineral and an other of the sea, that runs out of Isles and rocks
(called Naphtha) of the which kind, that which is somewhat white is counted
the best: the (alchichil) or old claret, and as much as is possible let them
sok in the oil. Dry them by the^ selves in cases (caczobis. The Munks upon
Mesuen: where also is red shortly after, Caczola for a Cucurbita or body of a
Bulcasis) which stopped very exactly, set it up and keep it: for the elder it is
the stronger: Mesue as Syluius tra^slateth him, whose words also I have written
here out of his annotacio^s. Oil (says he) of tiles is so subtle, that more
subtle, more hot and more effectuous in cold diseases, the^ is oil of balm. It
provoke vrin, it breaks the stone, kills worms: it is holsom for the
singing of the ears coming of a gros wind for the palsi, the cra^p (spasmo
cynico) the sciatica or ach in the hukle bones, the gout in the knees, fete and
the greues of the other joints, being drunk or anointed upon ye place: but let
it be drunk mixte with a little portion of some water convenient for the disease.
The making of this oil is described also Rasis in his Antidotari or
preservative and by Bulcasis in his book entitled Seruitor: whereas Bulcasis
prefers new tiles, yt never yet touched water, because they drink in ye oil
better: and he bids divide the^ into pieces of an inche bigness, and putreth
the^ so in a vessel of glass, or glased, well claied yt the third part thereof
remain empty. The fire must be made soft at ye first, but so yt it touch ye
bottom, and encresed by little and little. First shall a water issue forth
afterward an oil (read Bulcasis) which must be gathered guaiacu^ and many other
things. Oil also of Chamaemel, and Nard also, which is called Benet or blessed
of Mesuae: but this (of tiles) is ye chief, and of so^e is called petroleu^ or
oil of stones. These Sylu. This is a stinking oil, but it perceth passingli.
Io. Iac. de Ma^liis. Read certain things before, where we have entreted generally
of ye destillatio^ of oils by desce^cion, out of Vlst. Bull. bideth to dip in
the pieces every one of an i^che bigness (a dram, Rasis) made read hot, in
most old oil, and when they are quenched to take them out of y^ oil and
when they are al so^what gross beaten, to put them into one or mo stillatory
vessels: which he calls belies, so that.ii. parts of ye vessel or more be
filled. He bids also to take heed that ye fire come not nie to this oil
(while it is destilled) because it will easily be set on fire, and hardly be put
out. Afterward again new pieces of tiles, dressed as before, to be destilled,
til oil enough be gathered: which he bids to be kept in a vessel with a
narow mouth: most diligently stopte with wax Rasis describe.

The oil that they call commonly Balsamyn (of spik) raises up suddenly them
that lie in the falling Alexander Benet.

This oil is made also with certain other things mixed with it, as I found in a
certain written book, the words whereof I will rehers here. Take and break into
little pieces red tiles very old or new, but yt never touched water, and they
being made red hot quench them in oil of olives, or else, y^ is better, in oil
of baies, by and by make the^ red hot again, and theiwer que^shed, if any of it
be left, if not, put new unto it to the measur of a fingar. Afterward put to it
Castorei, and Spicknard, or in the steed of it rue, of either of them one part:
Costi two parts. When thies are punned, mixed them with the tyles in a
Cucurbita or belly, which thou shall dig in horse dong about. rx. days. At
length thou shall destill it, increasing the fire by little and little. The
first liquor is good, the second better, but the third of a red color is best.
This oyll is good against Cynical Cramp, being anointed thereupon or drunke^,
also the Sciatica, and the griefs Ammoniack, dissolueth in short space the
impostumes and hardnesses of the splien. It is of force against the falling
sickness and the obstruction or stopping of the nose, being put into ye
noosthrilles. It heateth the brain, confirms the memory, asswageth touth
ache. Being put into ye womb, it provoke the flowers. It brings out the
child newly conceived either dead or alive. It opens the mouth of the veins,
and dissolueth the lunges from gros humors. A few drops of it drunken with
syrop of Rooses, helps them that draw their breeth peinfully. It consumes
marvelously the water descending down to the eyes, that is to say the disease
called Suffusion. If fishers anoint their nets therewith, they shall entyse
innumerable fishes. Iron moystened therein and put to the fire shall burn
streigth way. It kills wornes whersoeuer they be. Being made hoot in an egg
shell or other vessel, may be dropped Rasis in Antidotary attribute and Benet,
that is, that which is destilled of only tyles and oil. That is counted the
best (says Rasis) that is very red, of a strong smell, and of a subtle
substance.

For the making of oil of Brimston, a man must chose out yt which is pure and
never touched the fire, chiefly alive and of an ashy color.) This oil is made
many ways at Rome, by sublymacio^ and desce^cion. etc. It is good for many
things, and chiefly for fistulas, and for ye healing of ye wheels of ye
moutes excels most of all. For take and wet the end of a fether or other
like thing, as some young and tender spring of a try or herb, and touch the
wheels once or twyse only therewith, and by and by they shall be killed and
healed. The Munkes upon Mesue.

which thou shall set

Brimstone that never came ny the fire, or most yelowe, whiles it is brent,
gives a thick smoke to be received in a bell of glass or of stoone. Wherein
through the gros vapor an oyll gathered together destilleth into a large plain
vessel, in ye mids whereof the brimstone builded upon a little vessel is brent.
Other beating the brimstone consume the fyry substance of if, with Aqua vitae
set on fire, and after devil that which remains, like unto oyll of

ounces of odoriferous wine, til the wine be consumed, (as it is red in Luminari
maiore.) Syluius.

Put one part of quick brimston into.ii. parts parts of oil of Lynsied, beat
them well and diligently together, and let them stand in horse dong ii. dais in
a vessel well shut, and it shall be clear and fair.

But all these oils seem to be prepared only for this purpose that they may be
