Oil of Vitriol or copros is desired of chemists and likewise of physicians,
and as a most secret matter is hid. I will put here some descriptions

Make the Vitriol in to lime, as thou know, then pour burning water unto it,
so that it excede the Vitriol a little: then separate the burning water by
destillacion in a phiall or in a crooked still, or out, urge the spirits of the
Vitriol by little and little, increasing the fire more and more, til al ye
spirits be over passed. This liquor destilled put it again into some one of
the.iii. manner of vessels aforsaid, and destil it in a kettle full of water,
until whatso ever watery thing is in it, be separated, which thou shall endeavor
to bring to pas by all the wit thou have, that the watrines be clean gotten
out, either by a le^bek, (with a nose) or a blind lembek, whose nether skirt
have a hollow gutter or circle either by it self, or with waters convenient for
every disease. This oil I have tasted my self, it is swiet, pleasant, and
strong, in color (if I remember well) somewhat white.

An other way. Take.iiii. pounds of Vitriol of Rome, dry it in an earthen
vessel till it wax red, after when it is beaten put it into a belly of glass
diligently defenced with clay (as the manner is for Aqua fortis) and first
destilit with a soft fire, encresing the degree of the fire by little and little,
until Acetosae, somewhat warm, putting unto it half a dram of spices
Diamargariton, which is cold if it may be gotten.

An other way to make oil of Vitriol against in rabble diseases. Put as much
Vitriol of Rome as ye will in a belly to wax red, with such a fire as is used to
Aqua fortis, for ye space of.xxiiii. hours, as long as the water comes forth
without spirits, and more if it be not made red, ever kepinge the same fire.
When as it is now becomed red, take away ye head (lembek) and the receiver, and
keep the water for secret uses to be spoken of. After beat ye Vitriol and put
it in a belly with.v. parts of quintesse^ce of wine, that the Vitriol may be
soked: The belly Aqua fortis is made. So shall run out first the Aqua vitae, and

An other way for the same, that it may be the Crucibulorum glased within as

Of the virtues of oil of Vitriol, out of ye same written book. Drinck Malmsy

heals al manner of diseases, the body being first purged. It restores the

vitae. It heals ye palsy with water of wyld mint, or sage, and Hyssop, the

diverse inward diseases with water of Trifolium, all feebleness of the eyes with

of Adiantu^ and Hyssop, and the cough, also the disease of the side with water
of Pla^tain the Pleuresis with water of maiden hear, the feebleness Diarhodon. It
stinteth ye flux of the belly with the water of Plantain: the co lyck with water
of Rewe. With water of wormwod it resistith venomous bytings. It heals al
manner of impostumes and dropsy, continuing it. It is good for the limes that be
resolved, if it be Tamarindis: with water of Radish and and sea bremble, it
expels the stoone, and opens the stoppinges of theines. It heals all
agues with water of Agresta, and certain kinds of leprosy, continuing the use of
it. This is the true potable gold, and the true Sela^dyn or Chelidonia, and
more also in weigth it gives not place to gold, and it has the same and as
many virtues as potable gould. A little of it with a little water of Roses drunk,
restores ye

An other manner of oyll of Vitriol, which a certain old man a practicioner in

sweet after they had drunck it, in a bed.iiii. or. v hours, so that they put
not out so much as a fingar, by the which medicine I know many too be cured
of him from diseases hard to be cured. etc. When he died I saw the oyll which
me thougth was of sumwhat a white color or duskish, perauenture because of the
little pieces of silver that Vitriol, or as much as ye list in a large earthen
vessel glased, and fenced without every where round Vitriol many enough from
above in the very vessel, and leave it so til the Vitriol be clean brought to
lime, that no moisture or vapor at all remain in it, and that now it have
gotten a redish or yelowish color, nor any vapor or spirits seem to ascend
from it any more. For except ye Vitriol be very dry, oil effectual and strong
enough should not be drawn out of it, but mixed with a certain white fleum. When
as therefore it is sufficiently brought into lime, put away the fire, and
when the vessel is couled by little and little, take Vitriol as well as ye ca^
with a knife or otherwise: although it is no hard matter to get it out of a
glased vessel. The Vitriol taken out, break it small, and put the pieces into an
erthen belly, which the potter, as be spoken, has made of ye best earth, and
wonderfully well baked, so strong that it may be able to abide a vehemente fire
in a with clay, and yt, ii. or.iii. fold, yt is, first dried and parieted
again, and again. The form of the furnace shall be in this sort. This furnace
shall be butlded. 4 square, and in the top a little imbosed and vaulted round in
the midst of ye top of ye arch shall be made a hole or smoking vent, so larg, y^
eueri hour or as often as nead shall require, coals may be put conveniently into
ye furnace by it, with a long iron fire shouel, to put in the coals ge^tlely and
charily wtall yt they may slide down about the belly in such wise yt it be not
hurt. In the. 4. corners also above must be left holes or spirits should brethe
out. This clai must be suffered to be dried, before ye begin the business.
Moreover there must yet.ii. holes be made in the ii. contrary sides of the
furnace (not in yt side yt the belly looks out at, nether in the contrary, but
in the other two. These also must have their stopples to shut the^ with al, yt
the heat may be kept in. The use of these holes, yt the coals which are put in
at the top of ye valt may be hadso^ly disposed with some iron pro^g for the
purpose put in at ye holes, and ordered in such wise yt his full strength, and
this is a token of perfection. Therefore thou shall let the fire to go out and
the furnace once couled, take away the receiver, and turning it in thy hands,
and inclining it now to one side, now to an other, gather all the drops
together that cleue unto the sides, and the oil or lyquor so gathered, pour it
into a vessel of the best Venice glass: for it is danger least the commun
glass should be eaten through of it, for it consumes and eteth like Aqua fortis,
This liquor that practicioner Haganovv perauenture, or Colen or Acon: for there
men say the best and strongest

Of the burninge or broylinge of Chalcanthum, that is Vitriol, and his kinds.
Bulcasis, writes in a manner the same things in his third book of the
preparacion of medicines, which we have declared afoore in his preparacion for
the making of Vitriol Zimor, also he teaches to prepare after the same manner.
But emongst diverse ways of this oil of Vitriol, I like that best which I
described last. For the liquor that is destilled that way is the sharpest of
all, and also the tartest, in so much that it may be called vinegar of metal,
as me thinks: Wherefore of certain it is highly commended for the quenshing of
thirst in somer time, one drop of it put into a draught of wine, like as I
found in a certain doutch written book, where as this also is added: Vitriolum
is

dangerous that a liquor so destilled should be received within the body. But

I remember I have red in sum place in Lullius in his work of Quintessence,
where he makes mention of oil or Quintessence of Vitriol. But in what sort it
should be made, I could never yet find in any book that went abroad: so
greatly have they all kept secret this thing as a marvelous mystery. For the
description which I will declare here after out of the book of Nicolas Massa
upon the disease of Naples, can not be received within ye body.

When the Chalcanthum, that is the Vitriol, or Mysis that is Vitriol of Rome is
brent an oyll most sharp and hot is drawn out of it by the force of the
fire, in vessels of glass wherewith if a man touch wartes when they are cut or
wounded, they will go away. The same if a man taste it, it strikes the tongue
like as it were a hot iron. Yet the use of it is to dry byles within that be
out of hoope of recovery, where as they be not much filthy, as it chau^ceth in
certain that be diseased of Phthoe corrupcion and matter without grief. It
serves also to cut Cardanus. The same coniectur we write before, that oil of
Mysis or arsnick anointed without may seem to deliver from poyso^. The spirit
or Quintesseuce of Vitriol is praised of certain practicioners against the
falling sickness and Apoplexia or benumming of senses.

Shomakers inck lowseth the belly, both in honey and meed drunck to the weight of
a dram, and also in wine, specially the oyll thereof. George Agricola, Vitriol
does kill not only men but tries: wherefore it must be made in sum out syd or
place where no man dwells. Albucasis and other show the way of making it,
Brassauolus (In mine opinion not the oil, but ye smoke of Vitriol whiles it is
brent with fire and prepared unto destillacio^ is so hurtful.) And again, of
Chalcanthu^, yt is vitriol oil is made so burning, that we use it for
pote^tiall fire: for it is of a caustical, you is a burning nature, and with a
knief anoited with Olives. Whiles ye oylis preparing, ye must take heed of the
smoke: because it does not only kyl men but also the tries that be nigh, it
dries the^ up. The trial whereof Fra^ciscus de Mo^te the notable bone setter
whose tries of his archard every one died with the smoke of Vitriol whiles he
prepared the oyll thereof.

The oil of Vitriol is marvelous, burning like a hot iro^ without grief, and
is made in this manner, xxx. ounces of Vitriol of Rome or of Cypres, Salnitrum,
Bocia clayed for the fire of an alchymists furnace, and by the fire thou shall
have the oil incresed in the receiver: which is a marvelous Cauterium or
burning thing, and has no pere in any operacion, and chiefly in taking away of
wens and great wartes. But the receiver must be great, if thou will make the
forsaid oil: Nicolas Massa in Naples, and Thomas Philologus, who takes.xx.
drames of Vitriol, but of Alum and salt of either. xxiiii A water of diverse
metals (out of a certain dutch book) for the leprosi, spots and dunnes of the
eyes. The filing of silver coper, stiele, gould, of every one as much as ye
can get: the first day put it in vryn whiles it is warm made by a boy or
wenche that is a maid, the next day in the crumbs of hot bread: the third in a
white of an egg: the fourth in the milk of a Then put all thies into a still
and destill them with a little fire, and kipe it. For the virtue of it is
incomparable. It is good against the leprosy, and all the spots in the face, and
it procures unto the face a youthful brightness, it makes also cleernes of ye
sight thies shall thou reed otherwise in the Addicions upon the Breuiarium or
Bridgment of Arnold de Villa Nona. 1. 18.

Of Aqua fortis and such like.
E described a little before a certain water like Aqua fortis, destilled of
Vitriol, Sal Nitrum, and Alum against great wartes. etc. But the commun Aqua
fortis also, and the simple oil of Vitriol, if a man put a drop of them in to a
wen or warte first cut, they take it away: of the which thing I made a trial
in my self upon a sied wart on my fyngars end, wherinto when I had first cut
it with a razer, I Aqua fortis, and although it went not away by and by, yet
within a few wekes is was gone Aqua fortis or to separate metalles is thus made.
One part of Sal nitrum, liquid or molten Alum (that they call roche.iii.
parts: sand half a part, when they are dried diligently and purged with the
fire, let them be destilled in a vessel of glass. It is gathered by it self,
that which issues out first, at length when the glass looks like a safro^
color, increase the fire and an other follows: and yet if thou take it in
water of the fountain or well, it is yet so sharp that never the les it
dissolueth silver, and separateth it from Goulde. It is separated in this wise.
Take a little quantity of ye water drawn out, and put into it the weight of. xii
grains of very pure silver* and set it upon ashes til the silver be dissolved.
This shall send down into the bottom of the vessel, grounds like unto fine
lime, which taken away the pure water that remains, put it to the hole water
from the which Aqua fortis but of another manner of making.) But seeing it
vanishes away easili and consumes, it shall be kept in a glass diligently shut.
To a man that imagines how great strengthes it has, which taking Ammoniak
and Nitrum, with Chalcanthum (yt is coproos) and Alum molten in equal porcions,
putting unto them at last one fourth part of roust: this made It yet a man ad
and put to a little of the obstracite stone, called Smiris wherewith they polishe
precious stones, thou shall have more plenty of water and better, because it
will not be burned. These things therefore received and found true by trials,
let us see what should be cause that this water becomes so strong: for
manifest experience techeth, that the drier part attenuated and fined by the
force of the fire, receives a firye and a fretting or gnawing strength. But
why burns not Chalcanthum by the force of the fire, for as much as it
turns the driest part into humor, it is most sharp, and strikes the tongue
like fire. Cardan. Let no man think that this liquor pertains Chalcanthum
or Coproos an ounce of Aqua fortis: mixed it with an ounce and a half of
Roosewater, soores of the throat, palace, jaws and lips, let them be touched
twice a day with a little Cotton tide to the top of a stick and moistened in
this liquor: Thom. Philologus.

A water to whitten the tethe which Isabella of Arragonia, the Duches of
Millen did use. A pound of Salt purged and beaten: an ounce of Gla^sye or Isly
Alum, let them be destilled in a lembecke, Mixed an ounce of this water with an

An other like out of the same book. Sall Ammo niak, Sall Gemmae of either. iii
ounces, Sugar Alum an ounce and a half, commone Salt, an ounce. When they are
beaten destil them in a lembecke of glass: and with the liquor drawn out
thereof rub the tethe with a stone, and after wash the mouth with a little white
wine. Read before in the end of the Cosmeticall waters, the same description,
but without common salt, the use whereof is declared without destillacion.

Aqua Angelica of a marvelous virtue against blearednesse, Cankar, and burninge
with fire. Three ounces of vn^lekt lime and half a pound of rain water, let
them stand together in a vessel of glass or tin a.iii. days. Then
mirtinge them stir them together, and let them settle again a.xxiii. hours or
more in a vessel well covered. Afterward strain them tenderly through a
linen clothe till it be clear. Then put to it.x. drawmes of Sall Ammoniak
the whitest thou can Filtrum. This water heals the clothe or spot (La Toil
in French, that is the web) of the eyes, three drops thrice a day dropped
into them, continuinge till the eye be made hoole. It takes away also the
tears of the eyes, the redness and blearednesse, also the Cankar and burning
if it be rightlye steines of cloth both silk and woullen, if they be washed in
it a little warmed. Furnerius.

This emongst all other yt eat the flesh without grief, and dry up putrefying

A way to make red powder, yt is quicksiluer calcionated and precipitated, out
of Marianus ye surgeo^. Aqua fortis. iiii. ounces of quicksiluer, mixed the^
together in a belly or cucurbita of glass well claied, and with a head upon it, y^
nose end whereof shall be put within a receiver, let them be destilled with a

ry one.ii. pou^des: let the^ be mixed in a mortar, ever beating and grinding
with the pestil til they be well mixed. Then put the powder sumwhat groos into a

With this medicine without doubt thou shall dry and cle^se a filthy sore and
roten (whereupon the flesh shall after begin to bread) where as other clensing
things, as those made of ye juice Apiu^, or of ye juice of Cynoglos shall do no
good.

Nicolas Massa in his book of the French disease, calls Mercurium
praecipitatum, Angelical powder, because of the marvelous and as it were a
divine operacion of it in the French pockes, which he his self has not seldom
tried. This medicin (says he) dries, with a certain gentle eating of the soft
and superfluous flesh, and that without grief, removing also the evil secret
quality of the soores, and chiefly of the disease of Naples. It digesteth any
matter, and purges it, and lets the Corrosio of gnawyng and the canker: it
dissolueth groos matter, hard and raw, after ye opening of gummes. And no
medicine is to be compared with this in this disease. For if thou continue in
the use of it, it leads unto the perfect breding of the skin, as I have
oftentimes tried: and it is excellent in the evil sores of the yard. It is
made in this wise. But a pound of quick silver in a little belly of glass, and
power as much Aqua fortis unto it. Then put the belly in a pot and ashes in
Mercurium calcionated red. Grynde this, and if any part of the quick silver
remain with it, put the said powder in a cleen vessel of bras at the fire,
and be consumed. This powder may thou use to all the forsaid diseases and
specially to the sores of the yard, and of other places, where rottennes and
much il matter lets the knitting or heling up: and in fistulaes dissolve it
with wine, and cast it in by a brazen pipe, for it works meruelously.

Moreover water to separate gold from silver is thus made. Two pounds of
Vitriol of Rome Roche Alum.xvi. ounces: Sal Nitrum a pound: let them be put in
a crooked belly (writhe^ bakward)

But this angelical powder had I of a certain old Alcumist, and I made it
before Johannes de Vigo ever made any mention of it. Thus far Massa: and
part Thom. Philologus out of him.

Of the making and commodities of this read powder, read Johannes de Vigo in
his fifte book of Additionum: where he bids that the vessels rereceiuer
shall be thrice as big as the bealye, the xxxviii. leaf. b. And that white
found with this powder, is silver sublimated from the red: like as is what so
ever is yelowe or of a Saffron color. And also in his book entitled
Capiosa, the. Clxiii. lefe. a.

Powder of Mercury (says Matthaeolus Senensis, in his book of the way to
heal the French disease) is made in this manner. Take.iiii. pou^ds of water,
wherewith gould is separated from silver: Acetosae, of either ii. ounces, the
next morrow early take the waters from thence and put new unto it, and set it to
the fire in a vessel of bras or earth: when they are hot, cease not to stur them
about with an iron or woden spattle or s^is until al become powder and that
when thou list make such a recept against ye french disease as well that is
Flegmatike as Melancoly. Take electuarii Conciliatoris (this is made of diverse
cordial medicines, and spices, precious stones, pearls, gould, silver,
Camphora, Ambra, Mosch: and is described of him, Differentia. 196) half a
scruple, perls, hyacincts, of either.v. grains: the powder of Praecipitatum. v.
grains: powder of D^amuscum, Diamargariton, of either half a scrupull: Make.v.
pills (let the^ be gilded, Thom, Philologus Terrae Sigillatae and Boli Armeniae
of every.iii. grains,) let these be taken of the pacie^t an hour before day:
and let him keep his bed.v. hours. Shortly, believe me, shall the french pocks
be avoided with this receipt. For the fleum and blak choler also shall vanish
away both by vomit and downwardes. Besides this there are very many kinds of
diseases that we have cured with such a powder. For it puts not away only
matter and rotten flesh being strawed upon, but also without Tuneci, which
they call Carduum Benedictum, or with an electuarye of precious stones, if it be
not yet confirmed and stablished in the body, it drives it away meruelouslye.
Many also that be Limphatici, that is, mad, or Melancolike, whom they
believed commonly to be resorted unto of Devils, we have cured them with ye
same. What make I mani words almost dead of the quartain, putting unto it
Saccharum Buglossatum or triacle, or Mithridatium, some digestion made, an hour
before the fit, in.v. or vii. grains weight, according to the age and stre^ght
of the body of the diseased. Yea also it is holso^ to be ministered amongst the
griefs of the yard and great guts: for we have cured some that avoided their
dung by their mouth: leaninge to the moniments and sayings of Paulus Aegineta,
that says, how certain phisitio^s have ministered in that disease Athesin, did
prepare Mercurium praecipitatum with gould, and sold it like gold, which had
such virtue, to eat out gnawinge or grief. Se those that we shall declare
about the end of those that follow next after.

How Praecipitatum is made, which is a remedy against all diseases growing of
the rottennes of humors, out of a certain written book. Make a water of equal
porcions of Vitriol of Rome and Sal Nitrum, with a head and receiver, in the
which thou shall put the sixt-part of the weighte of raw Mercury, yt is to
say, if there be. 3. pou^ds of Vitriol and Sal nitrum, put.vi. ounces of Mercury.
Afterward suffer the water with his spirits to descend in to the receiver.
Then avoid all that is in the receiver into a clean belly and that is claied:
under the which put a head with a receiver, and destill it again: and when the
water is in the receiver, put it again in the belly in the which the Mercury
remained. Thou shall repete this till the Mercury wax red. Then when it is red
wash it with Cordial waters, as Borage and Balm and such like. Mercury so
prepared shall thou give to sick men within their bodies, in this manner. If the
body be very strong, give.x. grains if it be mean.viii. weakes, five: if it be
a child, consider diligently what is necessary to be done. But utterly mixed it
with triacle: so shall thou give it to him that is infected with poison,
droysy, pestilence or taken with other infirmity. If a sound man once a
year, or every third year, as it shall seem Praecipitatum with a dew
digestion of the humors, that is with a preparacion of purgacion, he shall
prevent many diseases. Note that in the stead of Mercury thou may use Amal
gama made of six parts of quick silver and of one part of gold and so shall
thou work greatter wonders. (This Amalgama must be made red like as Mercury
alone with Aqua fortis: although Mattheolus Senensis, as we declared a little
before, wry teth that the powder Mercurial may be made of Aqua fortis.) And
mark that thou may heal wounds with the first or second Praecipitatum,
which thou shall use thus: Put of it about the wound and within. And this is a
great secret. And know that in.iiii. destillacions thou shall bring this to
pas: which done, put it in a clean belly in the fire, that the spirits (that
is of the Hydrargyri that is the quick silver sublimated mixed with the
Praecipitatum) as much as is possible may depart, then make as is above
specified.

Of the sublimacion of quick silver, thou shall read Bulcasis in his third
book of his work that he calls Seruitor. But of the use of it in burninges
and seerynges, and for the French diseas, read Nicolaus Massa the sixte book,
the second chapter, and of the same book the fourth, how it is to be ordered
to seerynges, both otherwise, and that the seeryng or burning be done with none
or little grief. Certain use it at this day for the ill and angry skabes, and
ring worms or tettares. etc.

Quick silver (says Cardan) is sublimated in this manner. Put Quick silver and
Shomakers inck of either like weight, and with most sharp white vynegar mixed
it so long, till the quick silver be seen no more: then in a vessel of glass
parieted with clay sieth it till it grow together. If any do run abroad and
cum not together, break it again in a mortar with vinegar put to it, and sieth
it yet again. They use quick silver so excoct for painting faces. But it makes
the tieth fall out and briedeth a stinking breath. But for the use of silver and
for the art of Goldsmythes, it is proffitable not in few things. Of the
sublimation of Mercury, Marchasyt, Magnesiae, and Tutiae, look in Geber Summae
perfectionis, 1. 4. 45. and so forth. Also of the sublimacion of Brimstone and
arsnick. 43. chapter. Sublimatum, Praecipitatum, and Cinabrium are dissolved
with strong vinegar, specially destilled, and return into quic silver.
Syluius.

Auicenna makes mention of Arsnick sublimated. Arsnick or Orpment (says
Albertns Magnus in his book of Metalles) is of the kind of Stones, of a
Citriny color and reed, the which stone the chymici call one of the spirits.
It has the nature of Brymstone in heating and drying. Being calcinated with
the fire it waxes black, and by and by with sublimation it becummeth most
white. If it be sublimated three or four times it purchases such strength,
that it will

The medicines that ought to be used to a ca^cre exulcerated, ougth to be of a
very strong operation. Among other the best and safest help in this disease
Guido a Cauliaco says, is arsnik sublimated whose notable virtues we have
already made mention of oftentimes before, and hereafter will we celebrate and
bring it into renoune yet more. This kills) says Theodoricus) the cankar,
the wolf, Esthiomenum (that is, Sphacelum, or Syderatio blasting) noli me
tangere, the Fistula, and al such sore and wurst diseases, it kills the^ and
routeth them out the first day. But a man must have a great consideracion and
take diligent head to the places nigh about, whiles it is ministered and laid
Carcynoma or eating cankar, with Bol Armena, and other like. But also the
Arsmik sublimatum must be ministered in dew manner or just quantity (which a
rational physicion defines only by an artificial conjecture) etc. Io,
Tagautius in his institucions of

Zenzifar, that is Zinabrium, how it is made by sublimacion, look in Bulcasis
and Cardan, the fifth book de Subtilitate. The calcionating of orpme^t that it
may get a red color: in Joan. de Vigo in his Antidotary, the. 163. lief.

Tartarum, that is the dried lies of wine, how it is sharpened, that it may
have marvelous virtues for certain medicines (by the destillacion of burning
water). Lullius teaches in his second Quintessence.

A certain practicioner told me that he did dres and prepare the stoone called
Cyanence, that is, Lazulus in such wise, that it might bring up more stronglye
and more safely black choler from melancholy men and those that were mad. And
first if I remember me well he said it must be calcionated, then sublimated:
or first that the best Aqua vitae should be destilled upon it, put in a
Cucurbita or bellye, then sublimated, and be sprinkled or watered with Aqua
vitae. vi. times destilled,

Salt that it may be more pure to be put to medicines or meats, after it is
mixed with water let it be destilled by a Filtrum, and again let it be sod till
the water be consumed. The same purged in a gouldsmithes Crucibulo with a
great fire is molten and fused. When it is so molten, let it be mixed with
Salt Alcalis (or in stead of it with the white gall of glass (for there is
black also) which some call the norishmente of glass, other Zoza as Sal Gemmae.
When these are beaten they use them for Chrysocolla, or also they put a little
of Chrysocolla unto it. But this mixture has to much sharpnes, and consumes
some of ye gold. Wherefore they use it only to courser works, but to finer they
use only Borax. There be so^e that mixed Borax, commun salt molten and salt
Alcalis together in equal porcions, and in secreate matters count it for
Borax. But these are besides the matters perteining to physic.

Soot how it is gathered of pitche and butter, thou shall read in Dioscorides
and Bulcasis.

Of certain other not Alchymisticall or not destilled or sublimated medicines,
but cunninglye and wittilye prepared by other and diverse ways.
THe medicines that we have hitherto described are all comprehended under the
name of lyquors Corollarium or addicion which are not very common, nor to be
met with every where. But no man ought to look hear for a full or exact and
general way of composition at our hands: when as we shall teach only a few,
and such as shall come to mind by the way, and all these as it were by
matters besides our necessary purpose. diverse kinds and ways of making and
preparing of medicines, let him goo to the books of Iac. Siluius and other of
thies matters.

Of diverse Oils.
I Se the most part of odoriferous and precious oils to be sod in a double
vessel It is an easy matter to pres out an oil that shall be like the plant in
savor and Dioscorides the flours were poured into pure oil.iiii. dais, and were
set out in the sun, then the oil being pressed out, most diligently, as much
more of the new flours was added, and again it was set out in the hot Sun,

The second way it stands in pressinge out, in some things without any help
of Art, as Myrobalano, in other that be dry and odoriferous by their own
accord, as the Nutmeg, that is light stiept in wine and pres out with a pres.
So may a man draw oil out of spices. But they that be odoriferous, and lack
the substance of oils, as flours, upon Almondes and under them, and lay in
order the leaves of Almondes and of the flours by course, and keep them so
long pressed together, till the smell of the flowers may be vanished away, then
put them away and in their steed lay other flours after the same sort, and
do this so often (for there is not one way in all) till the Almonds have gotten
a very great smell, then have them to the pres. A man must first blaunch them
and parch them a little at the fire. By this means the oil

The third way is by destillacion (whereof it is said before.) Cardan.

Unto the most part of oils that be to be sod and made by fire, they put some
wine, by the which means they smell les of adustion and bre^tnes, and they be
sod til the wine be consumed. But flowers and such as be of a sclender
substance, it is Hyperico^ or S. Ihons wurt.

Iasminum is made of.ii. ounces of flours of Iasmin oil, which afterward is
sod with a slow fire.

Oil of swiet Almondes, hooll Cloves being added thereunto, let it be set in
the sun.viii. days, it shall be marvelous swiet smelling. But thou shall reed
diverse ways in Mesue, and in the annotacions of Syluius upon the same, of the
pressing out of oyll of Almondes, which also may be applied to diverse other
fruits and siedes from the which oyll is pressed out. We use the oyll of
Almondes am. (that is bitter) instead of oyll of ye Brassauolus.

I am wont to make an oyll of siedes and the reed codes of Capsicum, or
Cardamomu^ Arabicu^: other of the codes thereof alone, put in oyll, which is
wont to be used in place of oyll of Peper, or also of Euphorbium, if it be put
in in more abundance, for it is far more vehement then Peper. With us (they
call it reed Peper, sum of the co^mun people call it Siliquastrum, but not
ryghtly) but few of Cardan: which I praise not. Nether was the^ ever any man
said that fire was venomous, burn it never so much: when it has no venomous
quality besides. I have my self used both the siedes of this Capsicum and the
codes, without harm in potage but in a small quantity.

An oil of the kirnels of cheries cleanses ye face, takes away spots and
frakenes: it is good also for the gout, and the stone of the reins and
bladder. It is made as oil of swiet Almondes. Furne.

Oyll of hay is thus made. Let the hay be set on Rogerius.

Oil of whete is made between two iron plates moderatly made hot (or as Rasis
has, between a marble and a thick plate of iron,) but much better as I hear,
by destillacion: it is commended Furnerius and other. Sum say also that it is
good for fistulas, and the chames or chinkes of the skin: Mat. Read before
emo^gst ye oils destilled.

Oyll of Iuy beries, drawn out like as out of Bay beries, is good against the
gout of a could cause, or of obstructio^ or stopping: and it restores the
astonished members and taken with the palsy. Roge.

A marvelous oil of Hypericon or saint John's wurt. Three ounces of the tops of
Hypericon are in sufficient, afterward sieth it in a double vessel well stopped,
and pres it out strongly. Likewise stiep new Hypericon, sieth it and press it,
put to it.iii. ounces of Turpintyn, six ounces of old oyll, a scrupull of
Saffron. Sieth it till the wine be consumed. This oyll (says Syluius) no man
knows who was the author of it: Yet Galen writes that Tart or harsch wine,
wherein the leeues of Androsaenni or Ascyri (which are kinds of Hypericon) are
sold, does cloose great wounds: and as sum Hypericon hot, dry and of subtle
parts, is wholesome for them to drink that be diseased with ache in the hippes or
Sciatica. Of the which, and the virtues of other mixed here, thou may
gather, y^ which oil strengthens, digesteth and sclendereth. For it is
composed of contrary substaunces. Sylui. upon Mesuen. Brassa. puts the same
discriptio^ in Examine simplice. 519. chap. Sum (says he) put simpely the
flowers into a glased vessel, y^ the oil may be gotten out by it self with the
strength Syl. Thus prepared in a glased or glass vessel, they keep it. But
thies things are clearly expounded of Mesue and newer authors. Thies Brassauo.
Sum put to it worms or bowels of the earth, and let it stand a month or more
in a wine cellar in a pot stopped with clay or past, the^ they sieth it by little
and little in y^ same about That is the best that is streined first. The color
of the oil is almost red, the taste is sumwhat sour. The use of it is for
wounds, prickinges and all kinds of offensions and the swellings that cum
thereupon. Sum use them to the grievous soores of the legs.

An oyll is made also of Juniper beries, by expression, as of other siedes.

An oyll of the beries of Bays and Juniper, stepped in wine, is beaten out with
a miln, weary profitable for the^ that have grief in the hukle boon, and cold
diseases of the joints: Iac. Hollerius.

Oil of Baies. The beries of Baies grene and beten, let them be sod in oil and
streined. An other sieth them and strain them. Otherwise, set the beries beaten
in wine, and when they are stiept.iii. days, let them be pressed out in a pres.
Or else fresh and ripe beaten, let the^ be put in a sak, and the oil drawn out.
It is wholesome against the colic, the disease of the great guts and the
Sciatica: Rogerius.

Oil out of the Myrepsical Nutte, pressed with an Anuil and a red hot iron is
gotten out. Iac. Hollerius.

Oils to assuage griefs are made of certain Calen dulae (Mary goldes)
Rosemary, Maioram and other. Iac. Hollerius.

Oil of Nutmegs. Cut them in smalpeces, and when they have stand.iii. dais
infused in Malm sye, dry them in the shadow.ii. days, then warm them
moderatly in a fryinge pan: and thou shall sprinkle them in the mean season
with Rosewater, and pres them out in a pres Cardan.

I hear say some do not stiep them in wine, but a finger may suffer, and so put
in a little bag must they be by and by tied, and pressed in a little press of
wod, with a little lake or gutter of wod, having a spout enclining downward, the
little bag put there in. etc. They yeilde for the most part, the.viii. part of
the weight of the nuts, so that one ounce of the nuts give a dram of oil, if
the nuts be sufficiently moystie. A man may warm ye same matter again and pres
it as before: but the second time yeldeth very little oil and not so good. It is
better therefore to two extreme irons or ends whereof, consisted of. ii hollow
half spheres, so that when they were pressed together they might hold a Nutmegge,
or a little les, as occasion of the pressing served. The one of the half
spheres was bored through with.iiii. or v. little holes, that the oil might
run out, when the ends of the tongues moderatly made hot were pressed together. I
found also this descripcion in a certain written book. Take.ii. pounds of
Nutmegs. Three ounces of Malmesy, wherewith the Nutmegs

There be many ways to make oil of Rooses. It is made either with oil and ripe
Roses, or both of them unripe, or the one ripe, the other unripe and so there
is.iiii. diverse ways. Some in stead of Rasis in his Antidotario seperato
puts.iii. ways. First. Take a pound of co^mun oil washed, wherein thou
shall put the fourth part of Grene Rooses in a glased vessel (of glass
rather) which thou shall set in the sun for the space of.iii. days (ye.xl. as
Aegi neta has). Then strain it and put it in a glass. This way is better
then the other. The second: Take oil and Roses as before, and hang the vessel
in a well so that it may be touched of the water: and keep it. The third: Oil
and Roses as before, put them in a glass anointed within with honye, which stopped
thou shall let it diep in ye earth, where it shall not be touched nether with
water nor other moisture. ii, months. This oil will be better smellinge then the
other: These writes Rasis out of Aegineta as it appears. Aegineta bids
in the xx. chap. of the seventh book, unto a Sextarium or wine pint of oil
Omphacinum (made of olives not fully ripe) to put.iii. ounces of red Roses the
nails Mesuae in the. 411. chapt. describes.iiii. ways. First that fresh and
new red roses be set in the sun.vii. days, then let them be sod in a double
vessel.iii. hours: then (the Rose leaves wrong out) let other be put in, and
let them be set in the sun and sod as before. Which when thou have done thrice,
put to the oil water of infusion of Rooses, Syluius tra^slateth it, as much as
the oil is, which I like not so well.) So when it is set in the sun.xl.
days, strain it and set it long again in the Sun. The second, mixting
with the oil washed the juice of Roses, and the water of their infusion, and the
leaves beaten together: then setting it in the sun with swiet Almondes
blaunshed, and exactly beaten in a mortar, leaves of Roses be beaten again,
the^ make them in little lumpes or caakes, and keep them in a hot air.xxiiii.
hours. Then beat the^ again and kneed them in the mortar very exactly,
pouring unto it a little hot water of infusion of Roses. At length prees out
the oyll with a press, and put in a glass covered set it to su^. The fourth, y^
it be made with Sesamum blaunshed after the same manner as with Almondes. But
Almondes are Sesama for ripe. Thies has Mesue, where Syluius had it. The first
composition (says he) of the.iiii. now rehearsed, is used of many: but of the
Parisians, the composition of Nicolas, which shall be declared in his
Antidotary. And again, I hear that oyll of Roses is is made most
odoriferous, by putrefying the roses one month in dung in a vessel well
stopped. After the same manner of commun Mastick, and Roses incarnate and Musk
Roses and such like, Cardanus out of Symeon. There be sum that when the Rooses
are beeten, and sod in only water, say there swimmeth a certain fat foom,
which may be streined or gathered me, that the leaves of Roses new should be
sod in water til they be thick as honey almost, then crushed with a spoon, that
the oyll or foom may enter in to it: but sum water will be mixed also with it,
wherefore when it is gathered in a glass, it is set in the sun and ye oil swiming
above in ye top is separated.
