Of oyll of Tartarum, that is the dry Lies of wine.
OYll of Tartarum devised by Peter Argil lata, serves to cleanse the face and
to smouth it. Tartarum cleaving to the sides of the vessel, white rather then
red made into powder, is stepped in vinegar, after it is folded in a linen
cloothe, then let it be put in Tow moystened with water under the ashes:
after that let it be laid in a dish hielding towad the one side.iii. days:
then shall a certain humor sumwhat red destill.

Nicolas way to make oil of Tartarum cleaning Tartaru^ that is of good wine
beaten, folded in a linen cloth, moysten it well with strong white vynegar:
sieth it under hot ashes (burn it) til it wax black, beet it again, keep it in
a vessel lying on the one side enclyned eight days, in a cold place, til it
be resolueed into oil, which if it do not, pres it out and keep it. The same
ways does Mesues make oils of eggs. It were better, when ye Tartaru^ is
calcinated and put it in a Hippocras bag, as they call it, and into a vessel
set under it. Syluius. I find an other certain manner in Furnerius in a French
book of decking, where as he bids to take Tartaru^ burned and calcinated,
as much as can be taken at two handfulles and tied strait in a linen clout
to be burnt and calcinated in a furnace of Glass, Lyme or Brick: the^ to be
powered into a good quantity of water with as much Alum as a nut and to be
much sturred about: then when thou have let rest.xxiiii. hours, streine this
water (casting

Sum put Tartarum to be calcinated in a new pot in a potters oven, and when
the vessels are all baked then take it out. But I think it should

An other way. Take equal porcions of Tantarum Salnitrum pund, burn them in a
larg pot: then grind or break them with a hot iron and put them in a little
bag in a wine celer, that they may destill.

An other. Beat Tartarum and Nitrum in equal parts, mixed them and burn them
that the Nitrum may be consumed. The Tartarum that remains: put in a bladder
(that is a glass like a bladder) hang it in hot water, and it shallbe streighte
resolved into oil. An other. Tartarum well washte from the dregs and very well
dried shall Filtrum: then let it gather into a Iellye: when it is so,
calcinat it again that it may wax more white. So at length shall thou hang
it in a little bag, as an Hippocras bag in a moist place: lay on some
broad thing for a cover, that no unclean thing fall into it, and setting a
glased pot under it.

Some destill it in a lembeek of glass (that is to say in ashes) from whence a
water runs first, then the fire increased by little and little, the oil.

The use. It is good for all scabs and Ringwormes, it makes the skin white,
clear and youthlike. I have seen it used to runninge soores of the head: to
the which I thinck that is better where with Nitrum is burned together. It makes
coper also and silver white, and puts away spots and steins in linne^
clothes. It is put to colors to make the^ ink, of the color of the bresill
wode and other. Rogerius also. 4. 9: teaches to make oil of Tartarum. This
(says he) cleanses cloths and spots remaining after birth of a melancoly
cause, and purges the face.

Of oils of the yelkes of eggs, worms, and Scorpions.
Oil of eggs, out of Rasis. Put the yelks of eggs in an iron kettel upon the
coals till Mesues) is proved with much experience to purge the skin, to heal
thoroughly tetters, ringwormes and other faults of the skin, to bread hear, to
sores boyles and fistulaes. Thirty eg yolks or there about hard roasted,
crummed with the hands, let them be parched in an earthen frying pan or
skellet leaden with Mesues. Whereas Syluius in his annotacions says. It takes
away meruelously the foulnes of the skin, and skars, specially that be left in
burnte places, for the most part it smells somewhat strong, yet at the last
destilled by sublimacion, les. It increases hear, as Serapion says in his
Antidotarie.

Oil of eggs of Nicolas fashion. Frye sodden yolks of eggs with a slow fire
made of coals in an iron skellet, continually sturringe them with an iron
rod, till they be well roasted, press them Syluius) to frye the yelkes raw,
and move them continually with a spone, til they being roasted, and pressed with a
spone, give an oil in a vessel hielding, which put in a glass reserved good a
great while. Out of xx. yolks thou shall draw out in.ii. hours.iiii.
ounces or there about.

Matthaeolus wrytinge upon Dioscorides, prayseth this oil for the roughnes of
ye skin, for ringwormes,

In a wound upon the brain pan, pour in Abhomeron, Abynzoar teaches,
which we have also tried with good succes: Marianus Sa^ctus.

An oil for the sores or boyles of children.xvi. yolks of eggs sod: an vure of
Mirrh.iiii. grains of black Helleborus, let them be mixte together with an iron
spone in an iron skellet set upon a few coals, then pres the yelks, and get out
the oil, till it begin to be clear without skin: put yt oil in a glass and keep
it for thine use: anoint the sores twice a day, and the scabs shall fall away
by their own Alexander Benedictus in his.xxx. book of experimentes.

I have seen this oil made.ii. ways: first, frying the yelks in a frying pan
til they wax red and yield an oil: secondly, siething them much longer, til
they appear blak, and all the moisture gone out of them: for at length they
yield an oil fodenlye: which is separated with a spone removing y^ frying pan
from the fire streightway, and pressing ye yolks with the spone: this some men
think to be

This oil makes skars subtle, dissolueth Tetters and ringwormes: Rogerius. But
Brassauolus writes that the Apothecaries do falsely attribute unto this oil the
virtue to put away skars, to the intent they may get eggs from folishe
people. Thou shall read more of the virtues thereof in the Antidotary of
Arnold. Some tie the yolckes so fried in a linen cloth, and pres them in apres

Oyll of worms or bowels of the earth is commended for the asswaging of
griefs, specially of the sinews and joints, also if they cum of the french
diseases. It is made, the worms purged from their earthy excrementes, either by
styping these excrementes through the fingars, or rather by letting them stand
alive in sum vessel where hay is, to crepe: then sod with a little wine in oil
and strayned: or set in the sun before they be sod. Sum sieth them in water and
gather the fatnes that swymmeth

Sum get out the peth of biggar elder pypes and somewhat strong, and put the
worms into the^ pouring in also oil: and so stopped, they let them stand in a
furnace for the space of half an hour: then they take away the oil and keep
it.

Oil of Scorpions (says Mesues) breaks the stone of the reins and bladdar,
and expels it, being anointed upon the loins, the privy parts where here
is, and betwien the tuel beneath and the Aristolochia gentian, Cyperi, the
barks of the rotes of Capers, of every one an ounce, let them be set to sun in
a Sextar of oyll of bitter Almondes, xx. days in a glass stopped: then put in the
Scorpions from ten till.xv. in to the oyll: stop the glass and set them in the
sun again a month. He that as he cummeth out of the bathe, in the.iii.
forsaid parts, and let a little portion of it be put in at the yard every
hour. It is marvelous. Sum had rather used the first, sum ye latter as more
effectual and better wurking. Thies Mesues. But Siluius in his annotacions
says, it is prepared also now with old oyll putting to simple medicines good
against poison: and it becummeth an oil of marvelous virtues against al poisons
and pestilence. I know a man with the help of this, that seteth Syluius.

In the secrets of Variguanus, we reed that certain have been presently and
out of hand delivered oyll of Scorpions.

Oyll of Scorpions whose operacion is marvelous against poisons specially of
Serpents and other beats, and peculiarly against Napellum, is written by
Matthaeolus Senensis in his sixth book of his comme^taries upon Dioscorides
imprinted in Italian, from whence we have translated it into Latin. This oyll
(says he) anointed upon the pulsing veins, where they appear most, as of
the temples, hands and fiet, and in Hypericon or saint John's wurt fresh, (with
the herb and flowers.) Put the oyll into a belly of glay, twyse as big as for
the measur thereof, and when the Hypericon is a little beaten put it in upon it.
Then the vessel being stopped, set it in most fine sand up to the midst where
it may be made hot the hool day of the sun, for the space of ten or twelve
days. After thou shall put it in Balneo Marie four and twenty hours. Then
pres out the oyll from the herb and put to Hyperici, Chamedryos, Calaminthae,
Cardui sancti, of every one a handful moderatly beeten: and put them again
into the bath for.iii. days. Afterward thou shall streine them and pres them:
and putting to them.iii. handfuls of flowers of Hypericon well picked from the
stalks, and well beeten, set it again.iii. days in Balneo Mariae, and pres it
out as before. This shall thou repete.iii. or.iiii. times, till the oyll have
gotten a color as red as Hypericon, or the husks like to the corns of barley,
wherein the seeds ly hid, in such plenty that it may be equal with the three
handfulles: beat thies sprinkling upon them a little white wine, and power it
into the said oyll. then bery it in sand in the sun eight days, and straight
after in the bath for.iii. days. Streine and pres it as before. And do this
three or.iiii. times putting in new tenderlynges of Hypericon, till a very sad
or dark reed Scordium, Calamint,, the les Centaury, Carduum sanctum, Veruin,
Dictamnum Creticum, of every one half a handful. When they are beaten, put
them into oyll: and put them in a bath for two days. Strein them and pres
them as before. Then take Zedoariae, the root of white Dictamni, Gentian,
Tormentillae, Aristolochiae root, of every one three drams: of fresh Scordium a
handful. When they are beaten together, power them in and let them again, put
into the oil Styracis Calamitae, Belzoi or Laserpitii, of either.vi. drammes:
the bearies of Juniper.iiii. drams: Nigellae. iii. drams: odoriferous Casiae.
ix. drammes, white Saunders.iiii. drams: Scoenanthi, Cuperis of either a dram
and a half, when they are beaten pour them in, and put them into the bath.iii.
days, strain and pres. After, take.xxx. live Scorpions, gathered in the
Caniculer days, and put them in a belly of glass upon hot ashes, and when thou
see them sweat Rhabarbi Electissimi, commun Mirh, Aloes Hepaticae, of every
one.iii. drams, Spiknard.ii. drams, one dram of Saffron: Triacle elect,
Mithridatii perfecti, of either half an ounce. When they are beaten pour
Napellum, wherewith those.ii. thieves of Cor^ica were infected, whose history we
recited in ye fourth book, where we made mention of Aconitum, to which place I
send the reader. These writes Matt.

Cardanus thinks that the oil which should be anointed without the body
against poisons, upon metal, as of orpment or drawn out of Myssi, see before
in the beginning of the tretise of oils destilled of metally things.

Of oil of Serpents or black Edders, also of oil of frogs, read Mesuae. etc.

Oil of Castoreum is composed with many other spices, and hot gums. etc. as
Syluius describes upon Mesuen: it may also be made simple and single, and for
the hard and Massy part of Castorei the fattines cleauinge to it to be added,
or rather both, as I counsel.

Brimston is made hot in oenostagmate (I understand burning water) till a
certain skin swim above (they call it an oil) which a man must take in a
shell, sometimes the water must be changed, till oil enough be gathered,
wherewith they say Hydargiron, that is quick Silver sunken in a body is
entised out, if it be anointed streighte way when a man comes out of the
bathes. Iac. Hollerius. See befoore amongste the destilled oils.

Of fomentations and perfumes*
FOmenta are called of the Greeks Pyriamata, all things that be laid hot to
the body without, either to assuage the grief, or to draw out the matter,
both otherwise, and also to dissolve swellings: this perauenture is done by
dri fomenta^ions rather, now the one now the other both dry and moist by
course, as in gouty greues either in the feet or other joints, where as it is
ieopardy least the more subtle part of the matter drawn out, the groser be
left behind and made more hard. Moist fomentacions seem to prepare the matter
for the dry, to extenuate and make sclender, to mollefy, to digest, to make
them vapor out: some perauenture do none of these, according to the diversity
of the matter. But dry fomentacions do dry and Filtrum, that is a shred of
woollen cloth. Bladders or like things full of hot water or oil. Cataplasmata
also may be numbered amongste these, which are ministered hot, that is to say
herbs sod, and Milium, Salt, Sand, bran, Otes made hot in a kettle, and
sturred about, are put into a linen clothe or bag. Celsus in some places
commends the juice of hot Salt, and in an other place he says it is most
wholesome to make fomentacions with moist Salt. You shall, as he expounds
put a little bag into hot water, and lay it hot to the place diseased:
putting into the bag now and then an iron s^ise hot, and sprinkling water
lightly upon it: therefore you shall have.ii. slices redi, that while the one
slis is put into the bag the other may be heated in the fire. In the disease of
the neck called Tetanus, which is the stifnes of the sinnewes (says Celsus)
it is necessary to have a moist and warm fomentacion. Therefore the most
part of men do pour often upon the necks much hot things. That procures
presently ease, but it makes the sinews more apt to receive cold: which ought
to Paronychia or whitflow, or ilgnawing sore upon her out, and many medicines
used in vain, she laid unto it a fomentacion of the mos of a walnut tree sod
in wine, so that she held the sore place a pretty while in that vapor: then
bound part of the mos hot thereupon, and was by and by made hoole. A fire brand
also brent, foulded in a moist cloth is instead of a hot fomentacion in
Celsus. I have put Caret or Cumin sede beaten in a bag moystened with burning
water hot, unto the navel of them that were sick of the colic, and have had
often a pype. Hyppocrates describes a peculiar vessel for this purpose. Let
them put out their head the while, which are to weak for to abide it, or such
as it is to be feared lest they swound or chance into over great thirst. etc.
Sum in bathes power water or wine, simple or mixte with medicines upon reed
hot tyle stones or dros of iron, or stones. Sweet in bed is provoked with hot
tyle stones foulded in moist linen clothes and put in to the bed, or with
tinnen bottelles filled with Celsus. Sum put hot ashes, or burning cooles
rather in a vessel Matricaria. be laid unto by course. Celsus wills men in
sum place to lay about the places diseased wull perfumed with the smoock of
brymston. Hereunto may be referred all such things as Fuchsius and other wryt
of fomentacions, apposicions, embroches, little bags and insessions. The greek
word aeonein signifies the water upon and power upon, whether it be done
because of fomentacion, that is with heat, or other cause. Galen does praise ye
perfume of the fire stone or miln stone sprinkled with vine Cinabrio that is
made of quitksiluer, sum also with orpme^t and Marchasita. etc shall thou find
in Nic. Mass. and certain other which have taught ye heeling of this disease.
The leeues dry of Tussilago made in perfumes so ye smok may be drawn in at the
mouth upon, does help ye congh and Orthopnaeu^, and breaks the impostumes in
the breast. The same operacion also has the rout Dioscorides.

Of certain juices.
THe juices of certain herbs wrong and pressed out, are sod at fire or dried in
the sun, as Bulcasis teaches severally of the juice of Hamsig, Plantain,
Lettis, Singrien, Purselan, Rostrum Purcinum, or hogs snout, Scariola, Fenel,
Smalage, Volubili, Sorrel, and other.

A marvelous way to draw out the juice out of black Elleborum, which sum use
as a secret would not hyde, lest I should seem to be sorry that our posterity
should have any excellent knowledge. who found this way first I can not say I
learned it of certain my most secret friends I mean that black Elleborum
which communly all men in Germany name in like manner, like unto Consiligo,
very many keep it in their garde^s: but that which grows upon the
mountains, to be found in our country Heluetia is best. A man may try the
same way in Colocynthida Esula, Laureola, etc. Elleborum cleen and washed, set
infused in the same first a night or more, small cut. Thou shall take heed both
in this and in the other decoctions that thou skim away diligently all the foom
that swymmeth above, as venomous. This water shall thou keep and again power
other unto it warm, and heet it a while moderatly, changing the water so often
til the routes retein none or very little bitterness any more: which shall cum
to pas when thou have Filtrum or through a brush: and at length sieth all with
a slow fire, or with burnt cooles rather, so that they boil not, yet let them
be always at the point of boiling, until they be as thick as honey in an
earthen pot glased, or of bras tinned, the pot covered or little open. When as
now a little water remains, about the end of the siething, stur it about
gently now and then with same time for a pound of rotes of Elleborum, thou shall
put.ii. drammes of Mastik pund, and cease not to stir it, other continuallye,
or by little distaunces, til the juice seem out and out sufficiently thickened,
which will chance suddenly for the most part: and that the matter may be
the les burnt, the nere unto the end and to the thickning the juice is, so
much urge it with les fire: nor be not weary of the time: for thou shall have a
most excellent and exquisite medicine against diverse Asarum or Asaraba^cha,
and Cloves, but stronger, ye burning as it seems to the taster: yet is not
burning in deed, that is because of the tenuitie and sclendernes of the
parts, as I would have tried. It is ministered an hour after supper, in the
mou^tenance of a pease, in all diseases whereunto Elleborus is conueniente, and
where it is good to lose Elleborum alone, but infused and decocted with other
diverse medicines, as I shall declare by and by. taken grene or dry. But a man
must take a pound at the least of the rotes. Lullius and other (Cardan) have
made mention of the quintessence of Elleborum, where unto I think this juice
to be nothing inferior. This truly is worthy to be wondered at, that the
juice so long decocted is not only nothing weaker but also much stronger:
for it seems, (that I may speak of conjecture) that one part of this juice
to be ten times and more stronger then Elleborus it self, and yet nothing
Mastik is added at the last, specially for more hard and grievous diseases,
where necessity requires extreme medicines: and for Frantic persons and
otherwise mad and dotinge, which when Acatia and of Berberies in Bulcasis.
For the straininge of it only is decocted, not that which is depressed out:
also the juice of Galbanus and Licoris: but the water in them

Other juices are made all by expression, and aresod til they gather into a
Ielly, as of S^landine, Poppy, Wormwod, nightshade, Vinae Acerbae, Memithae,
Agremony, Hypocisthidis, the barks of the rotes of Mandrag. The same Bulcasis
also, wills to put to a little gum to certain juices that be pressed out which
are sod by them selves at the fire, that the parts of the^ may better cleue
together, as the juice of wormwod and gasid, that is Eupatorium or nightshade:
also of Centaury, and Mastik is mixed with ye juice of Elleborus, not only for
this cause, but also to amend the venomous virtue thereof and contrary to the
stomak. Moreover in certain other medicines, where a man list to increase the
strength and virtue of any medicin which is to be left and laid away in some
liquor, we chaung not the liquor, but the medicin: that is ever when the first
is strained, putting in new medicin into the same liquor, as in many decoctions
Elleborus we do contrary: For keeping the same rotes of the Elleborus, we
change often the liquor. I may tell the cause, because ye rote of Elleborus
above all other things, has most strong virtues, not in ye superficial and
other parts only, but in ye hole substance imprinted vehemently and fastly:
wherefore it may be also kept most long of al other. I my self used it, when it
was.x. years old, in his full strength. And certain common Practicioners
bid men in a part of it has sooked a night and the next day is strained,
and the rotes to be dried again: for they are nothing or very little weaker
therewith although they be somewhat often repeated to the same use and dried
again. But of Elleborus it self and what we have tried and experimented thereof,
perauenture we shall once have an other place and occasion to write of.

Pills of blak Elleborus, or rather of his juice, is to be taken every seventh
day in the curing of the Matthaeolus cou^selith. Three drams of the roots of
black Veratrum or Elleborus fresh and new: Dauci or yelow-Caret, Anisi, Peper,
of every one a dram.vi. grains of Mosth. ii scrupuls of Epithy mii or the
harder time: let all these be lightlye beaten: then when they have stand to
soke a day and a night, v. pounds of Malmsye (hear seems to be some fault of
the printer, for it is to great a measure of wine) let them be mightilye pressed
out. Take.iii. Cochiarum, Aurearum, of every one. ii drams and a half, knede
them together, and then let them be dried. Moreover when they are dry and made
again in powder, thou shall stiepe them again in the same Malmsy, as
before.iiii. times. The pills thereof, must be taken at one time, to the
weight of one dram.

Our juice composed and made of Elleborus. Two pounds of Black Elleborus
new cut: stiep them a night in a good quantity of water. The next day after
sieth them an hour and a half with a slow fire, or.ii. hours, power it out
straining it, and put in to it other water warm (which shall be ready in a chafer
by the fyr for this purpose. Repete this seven times or more. Then castyng away
the routes, take the hooll water streined or destilled by a Filtrum, and sieth
it by little and little and when as yet a little of it shall remain, power unto
it this decoction: Seven handful of Betain, Anisi. iii. ounces, stiep the^ in
water and sieth them till the consumpcion and wasting of the third part: pres
it out, strain it twys or thrys, at the length put to five ounces of Agaricum
elect cut small: flower deluce two ounces and a half: Cinnamon six drams, Ginger
half an ounce: sieth them to the half, pres them out and strain the water as
before. Then power this decoction to the decoction of the Elleborus boiling
moderatly: and let them be ^od together to the thicknes of honey. Scammonium:
thies dissolved together in a little of the decoction of Elleborus, that it may
be like the substance of honey, not much before the end of the decotion power
it in, (when the juice shall be moderatly thyck, or begins to be thick) and
thou shall stur it about till the juice be consumed. Thou shall try now and
then a drop of the juice, let it fall upon a tinnen ^rencher, and when it
shall be so thick that it the trencher on the one side or lifts it up, then is
the juice perfect. But by the space of an hour or more before the end thou
shall now and then stur it about with a slow fire, lest it burn too. Of this
decoction I had almost.xi. ounces of juice: I thinck I should have had skarsly
ye fourth part, if I had sod Elleborum alone. This juice have I used already
sumtymes: for I ministered it unto a young man sik of the falling evil, with
good succes, who now of long time God be thanked, is well: but Ascarides or
little round worms breeding in the long gut, many medicines tried in vain, was
restored with this juice once received, and a few days after taking hearts
horn burnt. Pills of the bigness of a pees, from.ii. to five may be ministered
after supper, that is from half a scrupull or there about to a hool. But a man
may try thies better in after sopper, ye next day I felt no small grief about my
stomak, I avoided su^tymes downward, but I vomited not. A man may more safly
give it to the^ yt be sufficiently full of flesh or fat, of a moist stomach, and
a stomach of moo exquisite sense.

Again of the juice of black Elleborus: and of the drawing out of the virtues
out of purging medicines and certain other, as I learned of a certin friend.
THis drawing out was invented for delicate Elleborus. iiii. pound of blak
true Ellebo. new and fresh, let the^ be straight ways washed: the^ cut small
power upon the^ this manner of streining, ye juice of Buglos, Borrage, of
either.ii. pounds: let the^ be purified strained most diligently, y^ they may
be as pure as gold, and let the^ be laid or set up for a time. The^ take fenel
roots Cichori, sperage, persely, of every.iiii. vnc. Iuiuba ru^, sebesten, of
either.ii. ounces. Melon siedes, cucumer siedes, gourd siedes, citrul siedes, of
every an ounce, sieth the^ according to art in. 16. pou^ds of rain water. Unto
this straining power ye forsaide.iiii. pou^des of juice: let the^ boyll a little
at y^ fire: afterward put in y^ cut and beaten Ellebo. and sieth the^ lightly
by little and little till yt the roots appear above, the^ pres the^ out again,
strain the^ through a filtru^, sieth this straini^g to ye thiknes of honey with
from half a scrupul to a hool or further. It is ministered to louse ye belly,
folded or molded in a little moystned dow unleavened, mixed with a drop or two of
oyll of Anis.

The extraction of Rhabarbari. Cut a pound of the pieces of Rhabarbarum elect
small, and beat it sumwhat gros, and power to it ye clarified juices of Borage
and Buglos, of either two pounds, let them stand.xxiiii. hours. Afterward
sieth them at a slow fire, till the Rhabarbarum appear and Balneo Mariae till
it be thick lest it should put to which is better, as likewise in a double
vessel. In purging give it to be swalowed down mixed with a drop of oyll of
Cinnamon and Anis, and foulded in unleavened dow, making a trial Rhabarbarum by
it self does not purge greatly, do sharpen the hooll extraction with a little of
the best Dacrydium, which hurts not.

An extractio^ of pills. Take any lump or mas that y^uo will of pills composed
most diligently, and of the best medicines: when it is broken into little
pieces, stiep it eight days in rain water juice of Borage, juice of Fenell,
in equal porcions, as much as suffiseth. Then sieth it in a glasen double
most clean wullen clothe, that will lose none of his hears, and sieth it
again in a bath and double vessel diligently to a convenient thicknes, and
frame little pills whereof thou shall give.ii. scruples or there about. A matter
drawn out in this wise is most pure, very tender and melts easily in a man's
hand. In like manner may also the virtues of other purging medicines be drawn
out. But head must be taken, that ever softening and slippery juices be mixed
therewith in the decoction,

A description of a purging Electuari extracted, which a certain excellent
Physician at Norinberg did use.xiii. drammes of Colocynthidis, blak Elleborus,
senae Alexandrinae, of either an ounce, of most white Agaricum an ounce:
Rhabarbarum Electum half an ounce, or if the Rhabarbarum be not very good, an
ounce: xiiii. drams of Dacrydium: Turbirh, (if I read right) Stichas Ara. of
either Lignum Aloes, Mastik, red Mirrh, Asarum, spiknard, Styrax liquid, of
every.v. scrupuls. Pour altogether and digest or putrify the^, as they call
it.x. days or.xiiii. in warm Aqua vitae thrice destilled. The juice pressed out
thereof shall be joined and mixed with.iii. ounces of prepared Aloes. But the Aloes
did he prepare in this wise. About apou^d of Aloes is put into a basen, caudron
or pot, putting thereto vinegar made with Rosewater, and Roosewater of the
vinegar of Roses, then of the water of Roses. Let them boil together with a
slow fire.ii. or.iii. boylinges: then strain them pressing them strongly.
When it is strained, let it boil again with a slow fire to the thicknes of
Aloes sturring it continuallye with a slise. When it is could, let it be kept
for use. The species or particulers rehearsed are first cut small, then beaten,
infused in Aqua vitae, as much as is sufficient, they are let stand in the
infusion warm in Balneo Mariae or sand.xiiii. days, sturring and chafinge
them in Diacy donion of the said medicin. If there remain yet any moisture let
it be put upon burning coals in some vessel as much as is sufficient.

Of the juice of the Flour Deluce and Rape. etc.
OVre Flower Deluce is more strong in operation in the dropsy, as seems me,
then the Florentines: of the which opinion a friend of mine also a very well
lear^ed Physician is, who of late sent me these his experimentes and trialles
of the juice of the root of Flower Deluce and Rape. Take a hens of the Flower
Deluce in place, and mixte it diligently with the yolke: and when thou have a
little warmed it in the ashes, give it for a mornings draught, which shall
avoid downward above mesure the dropsy water.

This also (says he) have I emongst my secrets for them that be sick of the
dropsy and can not sweet: take the juice of a round Rape beaten in a mortar and
pressed out, seasoned with Sugar or cinnamon, that it taste something else then the
Rape,

The juice of Purslan, Singreen or Houslike, Cotyledonis, and of other likewise
that be slimye and Clammy, because of the clammines can not be pressed out
alone: The herb well punde with Omphacium is pressed out. Other when they are
punde heat them at a flow fire. Other beat them and let them stand in a cold
place, as a wine celler, put in a basket of wikars, to destill down into
Syluius, in whom thou shall read moore of the preparinge and preserving of
juices, in his book of the preparacio^ of simples.

Of Lignum sanctum and any other, we draw out tears, which is moore
precious then the wood it self, in this wise. The Lignum is cut in pieces,
the thicknes of a finger, which are put in the fire: thereby is gathered what
tere so ever is in the Ligno. Cardanus.

Milk is gathered of Esula and Selandin, cuttinge the highest bows, and laid
hielding in a vessel, pressing (milking or sliping* the^ one by one. The
liquor so gathered shall thou dry in the su^ne. But the juice is gathered when
the herbs are punde and pressed out: Bulcasis.

Gummes that be purginge and other, (if any refuse to swalowe in the form of
pills) put them in boylinge water, let them be dissolved, strain them: and
put in oil of swiet Almonds, and give it in drink with rob.

Put any strong purginge medicine in water or wine: Then in the same liquor
stiepe dry prunes, or dry Figs, or Rasins till they swel, then wash them
in wine. These fruits received within the body do maruelouslye and without
grefe loose the bealye: Arnold in his book of wine. Sieth.iiii. ounces of
Passularium with water of Violets half an hour, lift them through a sieve: the^
sprinkle in.ii. drams of Scammonium, let them be dried. Then when thou will
give.xxx. or.xl. Panatella. Epiphanius Empericus.

Some stuff a fat Goose with medicines, with well lykinge Kitlinges chopte
small, with Salt, and roastinge it softlye upon a broche, gather the liquor
that drops therout, to annoynte goutie members therewith. Io. Goenrotus. I
hard say how a certain practicioner roosted a gose stufte with Mise, chopte
small, and ministered the liquor gathered there of to the annoynting of the
crooked vunche upon the back.

Of decocted things.
WE call decoctions liquors, water, wine, or vinegar: in the which any medicines
be sod at the fire, and then streined. etc. of the which for shortness sake at
this present we will speak nothing, saving that we will admonish, that when
they are beaten or chopped they ought to be first stiept which should be sod and
decocted in a vessel diligently stopped and cloosed.

A decoction in vinegar against the pestilence. Sieth two handful of the
biggar Salandin, the herb and root together, in.iiii. pound and a half of the
best vinegar, upon cooles in a glased pot with a cover the mouth whereof shall be
fensed with clay of other. And of late I red almost ye very same medicine in a
certain book of a certain practicioner, written in french, and a few years
past when the pestilence was here, a certain friend of mine sent unto me a like
description. Many would have the Selandine sod in the vinegar, but other put
other things to: sum, as I said, Rue only: other also Sage noble, Rosemary,
the leeues of flower deluce, not the root, Zedoaria and sieth it in a pot well
covered: thereof they give a few drops

Otherwise.. Wormwood, Rewe, the yonglinges Selandin. iii. parts. Sieth the^
in white vinegar q. s. that is as much as is sufficient in a pot clayed, till
the third part be consumed, let it be given as soon as ama^ is infected, and
after let him sweet.

Otherwise.. A pound of the roots of Sela^dine, Brimstone, Saffron,
Turmentill, an ounce, Triacle.ii. ounces: pimpernel, Gentian, of every an ounce
and a half: Pilosella or mous ear, with the root and all. Rewe, of either an
handful, Sage with.ii. quartes of whyt vinegar til the third part be consumed.
In this decoction dissolve electuary of an eg (or triacle) to the quantity of
a been let it be given to the pacient as thou know.

Of made wine, and mixed with medicines.
LAst of all we will teach and declare here certain things of made and
counterfeit wynes, yet not of all of purpose, Dioscorides, Aetius in his last
book, and to the book of Arnold Villa nouani of wynes. Wynes mixed with
medicines are made diverse ways: first siething the medicines with the wine
while it is must and new, for the most part to the consumpcion of the third
part, skumming in the mean season and straining afterward. Sum sieth the wine
alone: other till ye Zedoaria, with Inula, also with Borrage and Melissa, in
Arnold. Sum sieth a little new must and when it is couled, they power it upon
the herbs in the vessel. Secondly putting the wine, before it be hot, that
by the heet of it in the vessel, the virtue of the medicin may be mixed with it:
So the most men with us prepare wine of wormwood, and they let it stand a
hooll winter in the vessels, ever filling the vessels again, if they draw any
thing out of it, in the beginning of vere they take the herbs out: sum leave
them in all summer also, but yet it is easily corrupted, waxes hoor and moldy,
and sower specially if the vessel be not full. Sum set a plate of iron tinned
boored Cariophyllata dry be put to, y^ wine shall be preserved the lo^gar. Other
sieth simpely with wine the medicine whose virtue they would have to pas in the
wine, at any time of the Inula. etc. Wine may also be mixed and streined with
the juices of herbs clarified: or hanged in a little bag in it a few days, to
be pressed out and when it is strained to be mixte with the wine, and new
medicines to be put into the bag, etc. for if the matter be pressed out often
and new put to, the wine shall be the stronger, and we shall fill the vessel
now and then for that that is drawn out: for if not, the wine will become
every foot weaker.

Wines medicined (as we have said) may be made, leauinge the medicines put in
a bag, or Arnold in his book of wine. Such as a man list to sethe, it should
be best to sethe it in a double vesselor in Baln. Ma.

Aromaticall made wines or with spices may be kept for.iii. or.iiii. days
clear, afterward they are troubled. Plinius teaches the confections of wines
of diverse simple medicines. 14. 16.

Wine of Wormwode how it is made, I have Wormwode: other mixte with it other
diverse herbs, specially hot: as Hyssop, Rue, Sage, Cardnus Benedictus, Penny
royal, Costus Hortensis, Phyllitides, the flowers of Eldar, the Barks of
Ash. I make sometimes in a glass forthwith, putting the leaves of Wormwode dry,
into Malmsy and burninge water, thrice destilled of either like much. This may
be long kept: a little of it put to a great deal of wine, does season it holly
with the quality of Wormwode. It is good for a cold stomach, Arnoldus in his
book of wines. A better way of making it says he, that the Wormwod grene or
dry be beaten, and that the wine warm be straind upon it certain times, til
it receive the savor and virtue of it, and to season it with Sugar or Honye:
this way to make this wine is better then other, because all the strength of
the Wormwod is in the superficial Galangal or Anise may also be added, or any
other thing as a man thinks good. Wine of Mugwort is made in the same manner
that wormwode wine is.

Wine of the kirnels of Alkckengi or Haliacacabus, is made the same way,
straining the wine up on them beaten, for one Dosis, of it thou shall take
from.v. to.x. it brings out stony matter from the Arnold in his book of wine)
whereof I my self also made a trial of late, straining together Anise, the rote
of Carlina, the kirnels of Peches, and y^ little stones of crabs, whereupon the
vrin holden now of long space, followed within an hour. Some put the grains
of Haliacacabus hole into new wine in a little vessel, that they may sieth
together, and keep it for their use.

Wine of Betain is good for the griefs of the stomach. Alexan. Benedictus,

Wine of Buglos of the rotes of Buglos stieped in wine, is maruelouslye
commended of Arnold against the diseases of Melancholy, with a story of a
certain woman healed with the drincking thereof, which through anger, became
oftentimes a fool. Wine of Buglos, wherein the rotes of Buglos well washed have been a day and a night or the syrup of it, it brings mirth and is good for
melancholical persons. Certain me^ have been cured by the use of that wine from
madness, and the Arnold, of the co^seruacion of youth, the.iii. chap. An other
wine composed with the rotes and flowers of Buglos, with Sena or without it,
shall thou read in Arnold in his book of wine, with this title. A marvelous
wine for Melancholik persons and cardiacal. etc Wine of borage is made, if in
new wine y^ flours of borage be put til the perfect fining: or else if Dia
borraginatum or conserve of Borage be dissolved in new wine, and kept for the
use: Arnoldus in his

A composition of wine against burnt humors, and for Choleric persons and
Frenticke, inducinge mirthe. Take a pound of the rotes of Buglos made clean:
Red Rooses. Flowers of Violets, Borrage and Buglos, of every a pound and a
half: Been white and read, of either an ounce. When they are beaten together
put them in a bag, and the bag in a vessel conteininge.iii. burdens of good
wine (new I suppose) fresh and newlye pressed out white and clearest that may
Arnold, of the co^seruacion of health.

Wine made with Inula. The Roots of Inula cut small as herbs to the pot,
sethe them in a new earthen pot glased in.ii. pintes or more of Inula
together with the new wine wherein they sodde, and when as yet they have sodde
a little while together, ye shall set it down from the fire and keep it.

An other way. When the new swite wine is skimmed and sodde to the
consumpcion of the put in y^ roots of Inula. (for.xxv. congies or there about,
which is about.xv. galons of our measur, take.ix. ounces:) and when the must or
new wine has yet a little boiled, take it of and when it is cold kiep it, and
hang the roots of the Inula (the very same that were separated in the
streining of the wine) in a bag within the vessel. The roots should be
gathered in October in the spring of the moon, or in the beginning of summer,
and to be dried in the sun. This did I translate out of a certain Dioscorides,
which names this wine Nectarite.

Wine Arceuthite. For.xx. congies or there about of must, take half a fourth
deell of Juniper beries (a fourth deel we call of the measure of Heluetia, but
that varieth also, the fourth part.) First thou shall straw the bottom of the
vessel with thin chipes of hewed ash, lest the beries may stop ye entrance of
the tap: then straw the half part of the beries in upon that: thirdly the
rest of the chipes, furthly

Our contrymen make wine in summer, with ye Visula, putting them hool into the
vessel, leuing the fourth part empty, pouring in upon them white old wine,
which in short space will have a reed color, and sooner if all the stalks be
cleen taken away, more slowly if they be so cut that part of them be left in,
but yet thus, they keep the color lo^gest. But they are wont when they have
drawn any wine out, to fill the vessel again by and by. It strengthens the
heart and stomach, asswageth thirst, couleth, dries, byndeth. Other put them
not in holl but

Wine of wild Plums Bulies or Sloos, is made in the like manner in heruest,
when they shall be now sufficiently tender and ripe enough.

Wine Raspoticium (dutche men call it rappis) Raspish wine, that is, which
biteth the tung with a certain sharp bi^ding, it provoke appetite byndyng the
hears of the stomach. It helps the body dissolved with heat, chiefly in
summer it is proffitable for Choleric and Sanguine persons: it is made in
this wise. Some sower grapes together with the ripe are put in the wine pres to
be pressed out together. Or yt is better, let the grapes be kept and brooken
together with Raspaciis, and put into the vessel with the Must: That Must or
new wine, by the juice of this Raspacia (Scapos French men call grappes, our
co^trymen rappen, whereupon the wine takes the name) or kirnels in the grapes
which are sower, does get a certain ponticitie or taste like wormwood and
bynding: Arnoldus de Villa Noua. In our country they make it otherwise, they
fill the wine vessels with holl clusters well rypet, and power old wyn in to
them, and as often as they draw any wine out of it, they fill it again. Sum
put in clusters and Sage leeues in course. It refreshes the stomach, asswageth
thirste. Nicolaus My repsus in the letter D. the. 85. Chapter, describes the
making of a certain Dysentericall drink with good Raspe, or pure Marathratum,
that is wine made Elelisphacatum wine, that is wyn made with Sage. Fuchsius
says, for Rhaspe, perauenture ought to be reed Rhoites or Rhodites In the
same author, the first preservative is made with good reed wine or Rhaspe: and
likewise the last preservative. I understand that to be Rapysh wine, which
always is made with Sage. etc. and reed always.

Sum put spices also to the Raspish wine, as Galangal five ounces. Cinnamon,
Cloves, of either two drammes, Zedoaria half an ounce, Coriander iii. ounces,
make sumwhat a gros powder thereof.

Wine of small raisins, which Arnold calls honied, is made siething* the
Rasins in wine (in must) till it wax swiet, which is put furthwith in to a
vessel, and the small rasins beaten are cast in to the same, which go to the
bottom into ye dregs.

But the same Arnold, describes an other also very excellent, which he calls
Passulatu or made of small Raisins, the Raisins sodde with Cinnamon Must not
much, which afterward is powered to the other Must put in the vessel already.

Of Aromaticall vvynes that is made of Spices.
ARomaticall wynes are wont to be made two ways, either hanging the spices only
in a little bag, within the vessel which is let stand in a wine celler: or also
putting Mulsum or Melitum, they call it communly Claret and Nectar and
Melicratium but improperly, with white wine. If they put to sugar for honey,
with reed wine, they call it Hippocras. There are made at the Apothecaries thies
spyced and aromatical wynes for the honey be put to.vii. drams of spices:
and.iiii. pou^des of white odoriferous wine. Or to.vi. drams of spices, half a
pound of most pure Sugar.iiii. pounds of red wine, or les: other put to much
more sugar, and spices also. Sometimes half a dram of saffron or much les is
put to, to couloure it, specially unto Mulsa, that is such as are made with
honey. But before ye wine be streined, it should be let stand in a hot place or
in a stoue with ye spices infused, certain hours.xxiiii. at the most.

Wine called Hippocras is of the kind of made wines, after an easy way:
most acceptable to the French men, specially on this side the Alps.
Cinnamon, Sugar and Carpesium are beaten and bruised in a bag, through it the
wine is poured, that in passinge through, it may drink and soke those
qualities. This is brought forth in principal feasts, with Escharite pane in
stead of banketing dishes, the which fashion, but with an other kind of wine,
we read, was peculier to the Athenians. Hermolaus Barbarus Corollarii. libr. 5.
in the chapter of the Grape of oenanthe.

An other. The inner barks of Cinnamon. vi drammes: half an ounce of white
Ginger hoole* Nutmegges elect.ii. drammes, Cloves, grains of paradise, of
either a dram: Cardamomum, Pepper, Calamus Aromaticus, Coriander prepared, of
every one a scrupull, mixte them and beat them somewhat groose. Eight pounds
of wine, clarified honye.xxvi. ounces, mixte all, and strain spiced wines with
Almond milk.

An other for Cardiacall persons, described by Alexander Benedict, in his tenth
book. Take a pint of Austere and hard wine odoriferous, white ^uger half a
pound, cinnamon, Ginger, of every one half an ounce, Galangall, a dram.

An other of the same man's for weakness of the stomach. Take Cinnamon half
an ounce, white Ginger two drammes, Cloves, long Pepper, Nutmegge, of every
one two scrupulles,

An other. Take an ounce of Cinnamon, half an ounce of Ginger: Galangal two
drams Cloves a dram: Grains of Paradise two drammes: white Sugar viii.
ounces: of the best wine. q. s.

An other. Cinnamon an ounce and a half: Ginger half an ounce: Cloves.ii.

half: Red wine.ii. measures, (that is.viii. pounds,) mixte them, and it will
become Hippocras.

An other. An ounce of inner Cinnamone: White Ginger half an ounce, Grains of

Galangall, of either a dram and a half, white Ginger half an o^nce, Grains

An other that seems to be ordained for the defaultes Cardamomum, Flower
Deluce, of every a dram and a half, Sugar most white.iii. ounces, when every
one are diligently pund, let them be infused with these that follow. Take

Hippocras Laxatiue. A wine against the Quartaine, Quotidian, and bastard
Tertian, i^ concocteth and prepares the humors and leads the same by and
by out by the siege. Take a pound of Esula: Epithy mi. vi. dra^s, Polipodii,
cinamo^, small rasins, of every.iiii. dra^s, Mastik, gin. Zedoria, cloves, of
every one an once. Sugar as much as needs: Arnold.

Nectar in Arnold. For a pint of wine, take Ginger elect scraped, Cloves,
Cinnamon scraped, of every.ii. drammes, grains of Paradise, a dram. Let it be
made wine, or Grekish, which is better: and in stead of honye put Sugar, with
a gran of Musk, and it is most noble. A syrrup or Iulep is made of wine to
conserve health and youth, puttinge into.iii. pounds of good wine, two pounds
of Sugar. Let it be made a Sirup, the use whereof is with water. It may stand in
stead Arnold in the book of conseruacions of youth.

Wine made with Sugar decocted, is good for old persons, cold and feable,
and in whom the natural moisture and heat are diminished: for it norysheth,
and breeds blood, and fills the principal parts with spirits. Take the
best wine of Vernacia, or Grekish, or like unto them.iii. pou^des: a pound of
white Sugar Caffetini. Let the^ be sodde with a soft fire in manner of Syrrup,
Rabi Moysses in his book of the manner of diet for old men and such as are in
recovering their health, does approve and allow.

Of svviet vvines spiced.
OF wine made with sugar and spices it is already Mulsa or wynes made with
honey and spices. A man may in all the forsaid composicions, both in steed of
Sugar put honey, and in steed of reed wine, white: in those that follow
contrary wise. So of Hippocras wine, may thou make claret or clear: and of
clear, Hippocras. Sum make no difference of the color of the wine.

The honey ought always to be sod first with a little water and to Diosc.
Alchandicu^ is a wine sod with spices and sugar Ebenesis.

Certain composicions of Nectar of claret, out of a certain book written in
Latin, when a man would have a greater quantity thereof to be kept in his
celler in a barill or vessel of wood. Lotum, as it appears, is a name of
certain mesure of wine, in Spain perauenture.

Claret or good Nectar shall thou make in this wise. Cinnamon a pound,
Galangall, Ginger, Cardamom. Grains of Paradis, Cloves, Cubebae, Macis, of
every an ounce: Long peper, and black Schenoanthum, of every one.iii. dra^s,
Saffron a dram: Let all be beten very small and mixed. So have thou spices for
half a horse lood, yt is for one barell, (peraue^tur a. 128. pounds. The nectar
shall thou make thus. Put all the wine in the vessel, then put the spices in
sum linen cloth large enough, and let the clothe with spices into ye barill
wherein thou will make thy nectar, in such sort that the sides of the cloth may
hang over the Lignum Aloes of the best, Foliu^, and in the stead of honey put
sugar finely beaten in a mortar, then dissolved in the wine.

An other very good wine with ye same species, but in other weight. Take.xii.
ounces and a half of cinnamon, ginger, galangal, grains of paradise, cloves,
cubebae, macis, Cardamomu^, of every.ii. dra^s: long peper a scrupul:
spikenard, Schoenanthu^, nutmegs, of every a scrupul and a half: saffron to ye
weighte of a (Deranii) of Turona or a little more. Mixed al these together when
they are most small Lotu^ Nectar of the city Burgens. Then take a pound of honey
with a pint of springwater sod to the consu^pcio^ of the water, and set it aside
to cole. The^ take about a Lotum, of good wine and when the juice with the
forsaid spices is put into the vessel, streining the wine through it, pour it
in by little and little, and the honey also mixed first with a little of the wine
shall be poured in by little and little, so that the juice be not pressed together.
If so be it ye will make it more preous, Agallochum of the best Folium of
either.ii. grains, and a little Musk. Otherwise: hang the species in a bag with in
the wine mixed with honey or sugar, and after.ii. or.iii. dais take it out and
pres it well. But the former way is better and finer.

A drink named Manus Dei.. Cinnamon an ounce and a half, Ginger.iii. drams:
Cardamomu^ ii. drams, Folium, Galangal, of either a dra^, spiknard, Carui, of
either half a dram: long Pepper or Cloves, Nutmegs, Maces, Grains of paradise,
of every.ii. drams, clarified honey a pint: a Burge^s Lotum of good wine. And if
thou can in stead of honey, put a pound of Sugar. If this drink be rightly
made, it shall be profitable against many diseases, specially cold, and in old
men, and them that be somewhat weak and feeble unto copulacion. It shall be
convenient for Fleumaticke and Melancholik persons meruelously, that not
without a cause it may be called Manus Dei, that is the hand Saxifrage and of
the stone called Lynx be added

Of spiced vvines with burning water.
THat burning water does drink in easily all ye odor and virtue both of other
medicines and also of spices, if they stand to stiep in it a few hours, Arnold
in the descripcion of simple Aqua ardens or burning water. The confection or
making of the wine which they call commonly Hippocras. Put into (the name of the
mesure is not expressed) of burning water, destilled.iii. or.iiii. times or
more.ii. ounces of Cinnamon, Ginger half an ounce, grains of paradise, Furnerius.

A way to make Malmsy.. Galangall of the Let al thies sumwhat gross beten
stand. 24. hours in a vessel of wood well covered infused in water. Then hang
them in a linne^ cloth by a thried into a vessel of a soom as they call it,
(which is about the bigness of a hogshead) or half a Soom of clear wyn.iii.
days. And thou shall have wine so good and strong as is the very natural
Malmsy or Traminuum: A dutche written book.

A wine that tastes like Rhetish wine. In a vessel of glass or of earth

After the some manner is the taste of Muscatello wine made: take a Nutmeg with a
little Macis: mixed them beaten as is before said.

Cloued wine. Beat half a dram of Cloves with a little Cinnamo^ and tie it in a
linen cloth, as is aforsaid. The taste of Elseter wine: bynd sugar ca^^y
vessel well stopped, and when thou will use it, wringe out a linne^ cloth dipped
in this liquor, into ye pot. All thies have we borrowed out of a dutch book
written.

Of certain other Aromatical vvynes, specially such as are made by hanging a
little bag in the vessel.
CLoued wine is made hanging the cloves in a little bag within the vessel with
must. It dries Arnold in his book of wine.

After the same manner any spyces (and medicines) both cold and dry, may be
hanged in a little bag and put Must, which we would have seasoned with their
virtues and quality: In the same place.

An aromatical wine is made to co^serue youth, if spyces be put in a linen bag
vnbeten, but cut in to parts, so that the substance of the spyces be
wandering in the little bag, and let it be put in the wine, the mouth of the
wine diligently closed. Arnoldus of the conservation of youth.

An other way of aromaticall wine, for the confirming of the temperature, and
youth. Cubebae, iii. drams, let them boyll in a little bag in.iii. pound of good
wine, till the co^sumpcion of ye third part: put to Sugar, and thereof morning
and evening give an ounce or other about at every time to drink. Or else let
it be made like claret: Arnoldus in the same place.

Saffrond wine brings mirthe, and takes away Melancholines: Arnold in his
book of conseruing youth, the.iii. chapt. The manner of making it teaches he
in the second tretise of the same book,

Of Artificial vv^nes which resem* ble the taste of strange wynes, because of
the spyoes hanged in a little bag within the vessel.
HOw diverse Aromaticall wynes be made and a counterfeit taste of certain strange
wynes with burning water and spyces, it is showed before: Here will we ad how
witty men may inuitate certain strange wines without burning water: not to
the intent coue tous men may learn disceit thereby, but that physicions may both
gratify sumtymes and proffit also the sick specially such as be sumwhat
delicate. We have taken al y^ follow out of a dutch written book.
